Canadian Sonnets
The Red Page
Time.
/ saw Time in his workshop carving facesj
Scattered around his tools lay, blunting
griefs,
Sharp cares that cut out deeply in reliefs
Of light and shade; sorrows that smooth the
traces
Of what were smiles. Nor yet without fresh
graces
His handiwork, for oft-times rough were
ground
And polished, oft the pinched made smooth
and round;
The calm look, too, the impetuous fire re-
places.
Long time I stood and watched; with hideous
grin
He took each heedless face between his knees,
And graved and scarred and bleached with
boiling tears.
I wondering turned to go, when, lo! my skin
Feels crumpled, and in my glass my own face
sees
Itself all changed, scarred, careworn, white
with years.
FREDERICK GEORGE SCOTT.
THE HOUSE OF NIGHT.
Though all the light were lifted from the
land,
And a great darkness lay upon the sea;
Though, groping each for same not-careless
hand,
I felt sad men pass over wearily;
Though it ivere certain dawn would not come
in
With the next hour; that after many days
Would no moon rise where the grey clouds
grew thin,
Nor any stars resume their ancient ways:
Though all the world word thus, and I more
blind
Then the dead, blundering planets raining
past,
I know I should not fancy Time unkind;
For you, as once of old you came, at last
Would surely come, and with unfaltering
faith
Lead me beyond the dominance of death.
FRANCIS SHERMAN.
AMERICA.
Columbus came to thee and called thee new!
New World to him, but thy rich blood, bright
gold,
Lay cold where once the fires manifold
Raged fiercely. New? Primeval forests
grew,
Had fallen, and were coal! Thine eagles flew
Undaunted then as now, and where the bold
South Rocky Mountains rise in fold on fold
The Aztec to his God the victim slew.
The tropic verdure of thy far north world
Had passed for ever, moon-like fading out.
Sky-piercing mounts had reared them from
the seas —
The lost Atlantis had been depth-ward
hurled,
Since thou wert new! — Old! all thy land-
marks shout,
And bid us read thy waiting mysteries.
BABRY STRATON.
THE LAUBENTIANS.
These mountains reign alone, they do not
share
The transitory life of woods and streams;
Wrapt in the deep solemnity of dreams,
They drain the sunshine of the upper air.
Beneath their peaks, the huge clouds, here
and there,
Take counsel of the wind, which *all night
screams
Through grey, burnt forests where the moon-
light beams
On hidden lakes, and rocks worn smooth and
bare.
These mountains once, throned in some
primal sea,
Shook half the world with thunder, and the
sun
Pierced not the gloom that clung about their
crest;
Now with sealed lips, toilers from toil set
free,
Unvexed by fate, the part they played being
done,
They watch and wait in venerable rest.
FREDERICK GEORGE SCOTT.
THE WIND'S ROYALTY.
This summer day is all one palace rare,
Builded by architects of life unseen,
In elfin hours the sun and moon between,
Up out of quarries of the sea and air,
And earth’s fine essences. Aladdin's were
But tinsel sheen beside this gloried dream,
High, sunny-windowed, walled by wood and
stream,
And high, dome-roofed, blue-burnished, be-
yond compare.
Here reigns a king, the happiest known on
earth,
That blithesome monarch mortals call the
wind,
Who roves his galleries wide in vagrant
mirth,
His courtier clouds obedient to his mind;
Or when he sleeps his sentinel stars are still,
With ethiop guards o’ertopping some grave
hill.
WILFRID CAMPHELL.
From “A Century of Canadian Sonnets”
(IVJussen, Toronto).
The cult of furniture is not one of the
vices of the Australian people; the average
Australian home is an artistic horror; but
for the few who are interested there is the
series of “Little Books About Old Furni-
ture” (Heinepiann, 2s. 6d.), of which the
two latest issues are devoted to Chippen-
dale and The Sheraton Period, well illus-
trated. The two previous volumes dealt
with the Tudor to Stewart period and the
Queen Anne era.
2180 A.D.
Here are three frenzied persons mightily
concerned about the future of the race:
Hilaire Belloc, in The Servile State (Foulis;
Is.), Edward Isaacson, in The Malthusian
Limit (Methuen; 3s. 6d.), and F. W. Bus-
sell, in A New Government for the British
Empire (Longmans, Green; 3s. 6d.), each
strenuously advocating his personal panacea.
Belloc’s thesis is that; “Our free modern
society in which the means of production are
owned by a few being necessarily in unstable
equilibrium, is tending to reach a condition
of stable equilibrium by the establishment
of compulsory labor legally enforced upon
those who do not own the means of produc-
tion for the advantage' of ‘those who do.”
This condition of things he terms “the Ser-
vile State,” as the logical development out
of the present capitalist state; and he at-
tempts to prove that a legal status of slavery
is inevitable for the working classes. Of
course, it is recognised that the present capi-
talist state must dissolve into something
more acceptable to the mass of the popula-
tion ; but, brilliant as is Belloc’s argument,
he does not convince us that the socialistic
or the collectivist state is impossible, lie
certainly does manage to point out the ex-
treme difficulty there would be of putting a
state like the United Kingdom upon a social-
istic basis; but his argument is vitiated be-
cause he has confined his reasoning to that
country, and is apparently unaware of the
progress made toward the State socialistic
ideal in Australasia. He has taken the
working classes of England, content, it
seems, to receive the dole of a minimum
wage, with no hope of ever escaping the
status of a laborer into the status of a pro-
perty owner.
“If you were to approach those millions
of families now living at a wage, with the
proposal for a contract of service for life,
guaranteeing them employment at what each
regarded as his usual full wage, how many
would refuse? Such a contract would, of
course, involve a loss of freedom; a life-
contract is the negation of contract and the
acceptation of status. .. .Even as a volun-
tary offer, and not as a compulsory obliga-
tion, a contract of this sort which would for
the future destroy contract and re-erect
status of a servile sort would be thought a
boon by the mass of the proletariat to-day.”
And he asks ; “Of what are ,the mass of men
now most afraid in'a Capitalist State? Not
of the punishments that can be inflicted by
a Court of Law, but of the ‘sack.’ ”
Let him put that question to an Aus-
tralian laborer, and Belloc would be sur-
prised at the answer. Labor has the weapon
of the strike —of which Belloc seems never to
have heard —even in England. And it has
behind that weapon the general desire of the
public for fair treatment of all wage-earners,
as evidenced by our Wages Boards and simi-
lar institutions. The trouble is that Belloc
sees in the British Insurance Act and the
suggested minimum wage an evil conspiracy
to destroy for ever the independence of the
laboring classes. However, he makes a valid
point when he says :
“When the first experiment in a mini-
mum wage was being debated in Parliament,
what was the great issue of debate? Not
that the miners should have an avenue open
to them for obtaining possession of the
mines; not even that the State should have
an avenue open to it for obtaining such pos-
session ; but that the minimum wage should
be fixed at a certain satisfactory level.”
Well, Australia does not go that way to
work. We have the minimum wage—but
the Labor platform itself demands the
nationalisation of all big industries. This
jim-jam. of Belloc’s imagination would be
easily cured by a visit to this continent or
a cursory study of its press.
A New Government for the British Em-
pire, according to F. W. Bussell, should be
brought about by breaking up the British
House of Commons into four or five provin-
cial assemblies, by remodelling the House of
Peers to make it an assembly of delegates
from the whole Empire, by giving that body
sole control over imperial policy—a task
which the present House of Commons is
notoriously unable to undertake, and by
making the sovereign the president of the
Cabinet. “Only with the restoration of re-
spect to Authority (which in this instance
is George) can any real improvement or
better feeling come into our public life.”
That expresses aptly the curious reactionary
point of view of F. W. Bussell.
Edward Isaacson, in The Malthusian
Limit, ranges farther afield. He is
worried over the world in a.d. 2180,
or thereabouts. His theory is that
the falling birth-rate will ultimately
reach its logical end, and the whole globe
as thickly populated as it can be, and pro-
ducing the utmost quantity of food, will re-
main, as regards population, in a static con-
dition. The time when the whole earth will
be populated up to the limit of food supply
has been calculated, from present tendencies,
at a.d. 2180. Then the race will necessarily
be reproduced only at a rate that will keep
its numbers at a constant figure—a condi-
tion of things which has never been the rule
for any large number of the race. This
paper has pointed out that the countries
exporting food are one after another chang-
ing into the category of countries which
have to import food. Ultimately every coun-
try will be so thickly populated that it will
not be-able to export any food. Then, with
just enough food to go round in Australia,
the Mr. Fisher of that day will bring in his
famous bonus system—£s paid yearly to
every wife who doesn’t produce, a baby.
It is an investigation of the conditions of
this static race that Isaacson attempts. He
foresees a division of the race into two classes,
one of which will marry and provide just
sufficient babies to keep the population of the
globe up to the safe limit, and the other, tne
surplus class, who will marry or not as they
like, but will not be allowed the privilege of
babies. Only the physically fit will -be al-
lowed to enter the fecund class, and they
will be kept in the most favorable localities
by themselves, given healthy farm work to
do, required to cultivate all the old-fashioned
“domestic virtues,” and to produce their
quota of infants to carry on the race. The
surplus class would live in compact, child-
less cities, men and women in large, artis-
tic and hygienic clubs, occupying them-
selves in doing the rest of the work of the
world. The sole concern of a member of
this class would be his or her duties to soci-
ety ; he or she would have no concern with
family, and necessarily his or her moral
standards would differ fundamentally from
those of the fecund class. “Really, if people
have no children, the exercise of the sexual
function has no more consequence for so-
ciety than the blowing'of the nose.”
This segregation of the fecund class from
the childless class would have important
bearings "upon city life.
“About half the population of the "city at
present are children. Not only would these
be eliminated, but also all the persons who
are there chiefly on account of children....
and the greater part of the domestic ser-
vants, who are chiefly employed in families.
Most of the inconsistencies and hardships in
modern city life come from the attempt to
keep up the forms developed where the social
unit is the family, under conditions suited
to the individual unit. The most important
of these forms is the household establish-
ment; something economically justified in
modern city conditions only by the presence
of children. Eliminate these and....a city
which now contains a million inhabitants
would probably be reduced to 300,000 or less,
and carry on all the present activities of
general usefulness just as well.”
This condition would practically eliminate
the economic waste known as transportation
of human beings or commodities. Thus, the
disused tube railways of the Sydney of those
days could be easily devoted to the culture
of mushrooms. But, as the ingenious author
remarks: “Further details seem unneces-
sary.”
The theme of the book is its only import-
ance. It is too soon yet to worry over the
problems that will confront our posterity of
a.ek 2180. But it is just as well to remem-
ber that the time is surely coming when it
will be *as great a disgrace for a married
woman to have a baby as, at present, it is
for a woman unmarried.
There is nothing much except words and
three bull-frogs in Australian Idylls and
Other Poems, by E. A. Henty (Mrs. Edward
Starkey), (Elkin Matthews’ Cabinet Series,
Is.). The first bullfrog appears in “Wattle
Blossoms.”
Where a gay old bullfrog splashes ’neath a
boulder out of sight.
In “An Australian Spring Morning,”
And a gay young bullfrog, busy serenading
on a reed,
Disappears beneath a boulder loith a sigh.
And in "Dawn,”
And a pompous bullfrog disappears with a
splash in search of a wife.
It almost seems as if the poet rather over-
does the local color of bullfrogs. There is
plenty of other local color, but verse can-
not live by bullfrogs alone. In “Rex Coron-
atur,” the poet loyally takes liberties with
natural history:—
And beyond tramping of feet 1 and martial
music, an intangible something stronger is
heard—
’Tis the loyal hearts throbbing in far-away
countries, for the pulse of a mighty nation
is stirred.
So a fat brown bee, who's in love with a lily,
booming lazily home on the ambient
breeze,
Stops an instant to whisper to a shy little
rosebud: “Those are England’s voices
from over the Seas.”
While she, answering, nods with a wave of
sweet perfume—in her heart, since the
dawn, is an angel’s tear —
“How they love their God —their King and
Country—and with things like these she
has nothing to fear.”
Feeble stuff, evidently taken by her Eng-
lish publisher as an authentic Australian
voice.
Bards are reminded that the competition
for the guinea offered for the best “place-
poem” submitted closes on the last day of
February. A “place-poem” may be defined
as a poem in which some place is enshrined,
not necessarily described. Many of the verses
already sent in consist of mere topography in
metre.
The Next War
With modern weapons and mass armi« s
the next great war will provide new problem,
of an extraordinary difficulty. It is to anelu
cidation of these new conditions and the
changes in strategy they will produce that
General F. von Bernhardi, of the German
cavalry (retired), has laboriously addressed
himself in the first volume of his work On
War of To-Day (Rees, pet* Sydney kook
Club). One outstanding result of modern
fire will be the elimination of the cavalrv
charge ; but the author finds new and neces-
sary uses for this arm in reconnoitring and
scouting, and the offensive aginst the enemy’s
flanks and communications. The armies
of masses that will be brought into being in
any war with Germany will depend for their
victories chiefly upon (he successful solving
of the problems of supply; for the days are
long past when an army can live on the
country. And the modern army wilt neces-
sarily possess a lower standard of efficiency
and of morale than the smaller, more com-
pact and continuously trained armies of the
past. The difficulty of moving such vast
bodies of men may prove insuperable, or end
in the complete break-down of the strategic
mobility of these armies. In a modern
battle, fought with extended troops over an
immense front, it will be almost impossible
to convey orders from head-quarters to its
parts; thus subordinate commanders must
be largely left to themselves, while each sol-
dier is practically isolated even from his
immediate superior officer. Thus personal
initiative will be restored almost to the posi-
tion it held in medieval times. All the more
necessary, therefore, will it be for every unit
in the huge army to be cognisant of the
general intention of the commander-in-chief
This means that a general scheme must be
communicated to the divisional commanders,
and thence to the privates, before the battle!
The individual soldier must know, in general
terms, what his commander is after. This
German view, it may be noticed, is quite
contrary to the British practice; but the
author shows how fatal that strict central
control proved in the Boer war. Self-reli-
ance has in the modern war been dramati-
cally restored to the private. And it may be
noted that it was the self-reliance of the
Australasian troops sent to the’Boer war
that proved its superiority over the ignorant
mechanical obedience of the Tommy. The
individual soldier must himself want to win:
he will be necessarily almost left to himself
in action ; he must himself estimate his dis-
tances and adjust his sights ; he must know
whither to advance, and what point in the
enemy’s position life is to reach ; and, arrived
there, he must know what to do.
. ‘‘Once the troops have come within effec-
tive range of the enemy’s fire all regular and
comprehensive issue of orders ceases. All
success is entirely dependent on the clear-
sighted action of individual groups and men.
This is what a modern infantry action looks
like; self-reliance_ is everything. .. .Acting
with self-reliance in the sense and spirit of
General Headquarters and of the uniform
plan of battle known to us, is the decisive
factor in modern battle.”
It is to be hoped that Australian Head-
quarters, which learnt that lesson in South
Africa, has not reverted to the British
practice.
By command of Edward and under the
gracious auspices of George, also with
humble gratitude to the King and deep obli-
gation to the Queen for encouragement and
assistance, \iscount Esher has meekly and
humbly ventured to edit the youthful diaries
of Victoria the Good, and publish these in-
nocuous lubrications under the title of The
Girlhood of Queen Victoria in two lordly
volumes (Murray, 365.). The work can be
confidently recommended to all K.C.’sM.G.
The English. Character, by Spencer Leigh
Hughes (Foulis, 55.), .consists chiefly of re-
marks that the Englishman is supposed to
be this and is frequently that, likewise he is,
just, as likely as not, something else. But
for its illustrations in color —the delineation
or English _ types, strongly drawn with just
the_ requisite touch of character-revealing
caricature —it is worth its price. A more
interesting book is The Light Side of Lon-
don, by Edmund B. *D’Auvergne, whose The
of Paris will be remembered,
lhe author writes lightly upon such sub-
jects as London Bohemians, the suburban
pub, and Sunday, in London, with much
knowledge of his subject, and he ex-
udes much recondite information. His
frank chapter on “The Flirtatious Lon-
doner will be read w r ith delight. Ap-
parently the one thing that interests the
Cockney is the pursuit of the woman. Full
1 are here given in the gentle art
of picking up,” which, it may be explained
for the benefit of the decorous readers of
this paper, is the habit—not altogether con-
fined to London—of speaking to a glad-eyed
girl in the street.
The London paper, The Referee, hit upon,
or adapted, from the “Red Page,” the idea
or getting its readers to write the account
°* a day in their lives. The result, published
as A Day in My Life (Macdonald and
Evans, London) and edited by “Vanoc,”
shows, that the Londoner cannot write so
graphically of his conditions as the Aus-
tralian can of his. Several of the contribu-
tors write from Australasia.

expensive storage battery. Can be started from drawing-room or bedroom.
ith the exception of keeping the plant clean, lubricating and filling the

The Inky Way.
“Galleryite”: Cordially disagree with “The Wild
Colonial Pressman” when he says that Deakin is
the most difficult Australian politician to report.
Bill Lyne, Vic. Labor leader Prendergast, the caca-
phonous Peacock and Senator Millen, are a few of
those who beat him. All these, except Lyne, are
ahead of him in rapidity of utterance; Millen, by
actual test, is five and a half words per minute
in front of him. Prendergast could not be reported
by the Angel Gabriel if it were not for the merciful
fact that every minute he uses that blessed expres-
sion “at all events” three times. This reduces his
per-minute total of necessary words by nine. Millen
uses no extraneous words, and he ought to be cau-
tioned by the Australian Journalists’ Association, or
compelled to write his speeches. Lyne can’t be
fully reported at all, and he can’t be condensed to
satisfy him or anyone else. His speed is nothing.
Neither is his lucidity. Peacock one can become
accustomed to. Leave out every second sentence,
and it’s all right. He always says a thing twice.
His speed goes up to 220 words a minute, and a few
days ago reporters in Melbourne were hoping that
he’d be left out of the Watt Ministry. I maintain,
in the face of much opposition, that Deakin, in his
wildest flights, is always grammatical and coherent,
and never fails to bring his many parenthetical inter-
polations to a satisfactory end so far as good Eng-
lish is concerned. Many a time I’ve cursed when
I've found a sentence continuing on the third page
of my notebook; but I’m ready to swear to the
correctness of his speech. I’d rather not swear as
to what he meant. One of the best things about
him was written by a Melb. Argus contributor long
ago. Mentioning his failings the man said: “It is
true he has not always succeeded in making himself
misunderstood. ’ ’
“S.S.”: Windsor, so far as I can see, was the last
of a race of non-commercial daily-paper editors. He
was a literary man first and always, whereas the
successful editor of a later generation is mainly a
trader, a man of the markets. I shall never forget
my first meeting with Windsor. He was editor of
the Age, and I a very nervous neophyte. I had sub-
mitted a longish set of verse for the Sunday
supplement. He had written asking me to see
him. He could not print my rhymes, he said,
but he offered kindly criticism and advice, and in
half an hour’s talk maintained a friendly and plea-
sant attitude. No other daily paper editor I had
met before, or have met since, left any impression
but that I was in some way guilty of an offence
in contributing, or endeavoring to contribute, literary
rubbish to a great commercial vehicle.
* * * *
“Evan Blessus”: One of the most earnest advocates
of signed journalism is N. S. Wales Attorney-General
Holman, and he has taken more than one opportu-
nity to impress his views on pressmen in the Common-
wealth. In the January number of the Australian
Journalist he expends some ink on the subject.
“Anonymity,” he writes, “gives the journalist the
individual status of the driver of a parcels express
van. Just as the boss directs ‘one of my drivers’ to
take drapery to Potts Point, so the newspaper boss
directs ‘one of my reporters’ to write a story, or ‘one
of my leader writers’ to comment upon it.... And,
similarly, the leader writers on a big daily” (why
not a small daily, or a middling daily, to say nothing
of a microscopic monthly?) “are simply the paper’s
men—or, more colloquially, the paper itself.” Well,
let’s take the case of the reporter sent to do a
story. He has to get his information (we’ll say
about the unsatisfactory state of Parliamentary Labor
politics), and buttons on to Holman. “I want you,”
he’ll say, if_ he is on a proper footing with his
man, “to give me the whole strength of things,
or as much as you can.” We’ll sav Holman does it,
and the story is printed. Whose signature ought to
go on that ? Or, perhaps, a dozen sources are drained
similarly for information, and, maybe, a hunk of
encyclopaedia padded in. Is the man who puts other
people’s knowledge into shape to collar the credit (if
there’s any about) for the lot ? A reporter doesn’t
know half the subjects he is called upon to tackle,
but if he is a good reporter he finds somebody who
does. If, then, he signs the matter as his own
goods, he is obtaining notoriety and status under
false pretences. The same with the leader writer
a large percentage of him. He is given a subject and
a general outline of policy to which he may be
opposed. Of course, he could decline to do the iob
and resign, but, being an ordinary human being he
writes what, in most cases, are the views of’the
editorial staff’s caucus meeting, just as Holman has
to get busy against his judgment when the Labor
caucus runs counter to it. Now, why should the
writer have to sign what are not his convictions?
Also, what good would the signature do anybody?
As for finding employment on a paper where he would
be free to express his multitudinous views, and sign
his work with satisfaction, there wouldn’t be enough
papers to go round unless every man owned one for
himself.
* * * *
From R. P. Blundell, M.L.A., member of the Ade-
laide Daily Herald’s Board of Management: Th e
effusion of “Wus” (B. 2/l/’l3) amused me; at the
same time, I felt a pang of regret that genius should
be so often drowned. Our paper, thrusting itself
into competition with the Grenfell-street old girl and
the Waymouth-street wobbler (both chiefly interested
in “outing” us), has had a fairly rough road to
travel. And not the least drop in our cup of joy
was provided by those who made the office a haven
They were a fine lot of “rejects,” with a bag-full of
testimonials. (Our enemies frequently supplied the
testimonials.) This will explain why we have had
occasion to pass out a good many men. On the
other hand, a perusal of our records will show that
there are men with us who have been there from
the infancy of the weekly Herald ; and the majority
of our employees came to us when the paper became
a daily on March 10, 1910. That “Wus” is not
among this majority suggests that there is some-
thing radically wrong with him and his pal
“Innards.”
* * # *
That much-harried morning newspaper, the Wel-
lington (M.L.) Times, is to have one more set of
bosses. The new crowd is mostly the Bretts and
Geddeses, Auckland and Napier paper magnates. W.
J. Geddes has been installed as managing director!
with George Dixon (who was guide, philosopher and
administrator of the famous All-Black hoofballers
that cleaned up Britain) as general manager. Of
course, the first thing done, in keeping with old red
sandstone precedent in the office, was to alter the
make-up of the paper, and milk-shake the editor’s
policy, and clean up the literary furnishings gen-
erally. Judging by the varnish on the leaders the
last few mornings, the main goods in the political
policy department is to be lou Ward and Co. for
another flutter. Still, it is impossible to say. Life
is short; and the man who takes a trick at the
wheel aboard the Times, 10, the Sudden Hand smiteth
him, and his name becometh Mud!
* * * •
Adelaide, which hasn’t got a monthly paper or
magazine at all, is threatened with one shortly. It
is to be a purely theatrical affair. Parkes (“Van
Eck”), of the Mail, and Gerald Longson, who has
done some press-work in two hemispheres, are cher-
ishing the idea within their shirt fronts.
* * * *
“Rosna”: Melbourne pressman “Billy” Moore, in
the intervals of thrusting Australian plays at London
managers, has been lecturing the Royal Colonial In-
stitute on “Australian Literature and Art.” Georgie
Reid presided, or rather came along to say he
couldn’t, and then made his escape, leaving J. G.
Jenkins to act as locum-tenens. The lecturer said
all that could be expected of him in the time, and
there were refreshing lantern slides of famous Aus-
tralian daubs to serve as eye-openers to the dear
British public, the artists represented being Lambert,
Streeton, Bunny, McCubbin, Longstaff, Lever, Phil-
lips Fox, Coates, Shirlow, Norman Lindsay, Bess
Norris, Dyson, Dora Meeson, Ruby Lind, Fullwood,
Leist, Mahony, Tom Roberts, Hans Heysen and
Quinn. A number of the victims went along to see
that they were not unduly libelled, and Lambert and
Will Dyson were drawn into the subsequent talk.
Incidentally, Dyson perpetrated the one bright re-
mark of the perfectly decorous occasion. Someone
had accused Australians of teaching their grand-
mothers to suck eggs. Dyson said it might be a
disrespectful proceeding, but was also a neat retalia-
tion, since Our grandmothers always wanted us to
suck the eggs of their generation.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The River by Night.
On high a growing moon
In star-surrounded state
Is reigning, where of late
There reigned the royal noon
And all its after-hours
Of day-time, birds and flowers.
A low wind yields its breath,
And in the brilliant west
A star goes down to rest;
It is not life nor death
This mood of the river-reach,
But a thing akin to each.
There rises over all
The hoot of a great-eyed owl,
The whimper of water-fowl,
And the unobtrusive call
Of timorous things that hide
In the reeds of the river-side.
The wearied willows weep
Above the passing wave
That hastens to its grave
In that devouring deep
Whose billows evermore
Assail an iron shore.
A child of rain and range
The river goes its way,
By night-time as by day,
Unmindful of all change—
Indifferent to the light
Of noon, the stars of night.
There is a stream sublime
Wherein each high-set thing
Hath transient mirroring.
It is the tide of Time;
Full well, full well we know
How fickle is its show.
Where now is Egypt’s god?
And where is Babylon?
And where each Shining One,
Who, girt in glory, trod
The earth in elder days.
Inspiring song and praise?
Their realms no more abide ;
Long, long ago. they passed,
And newer lights are glassed
Within the callous tide.
A little while, and then
These, too, shall pass... Amen!
The owl Oblivion
Shall hoot derisively,
And what is now shall be
Of like account anon
With yon small life that cries
Its little hour, then dies.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
To-Day.
To-day we walk, to-morrow we fly ;
To-day we think that we all must die;
To-morrow we know that a medicine man
Has sterilised Death in his vacuum pan.
The day before yesterday all we knew
Was that nothing we’d learnt before was
tme,
And that all the world was a bunch of
shakes
That only form in our fancy takes.
But yesterday found us making terms
With a universe peopled with working
germs; .
And only this morning we woke to be
All radio-active energy.
I can only hope that, they’ll tell me soon
If the world will exist this afternoon,
And whether I’ll be, next quarter day,
A wave, or a germ, or a violet ray.
MAJORCA.
WAIF.
The Sphinx.
Lonely, impassive and inscrutable
She crouches, like some creature of the
waste,
Gazing across the sands with granite eyes,
And still mysterious smile, and scroll-like
brow
Stamped with the cipher of the great cold
stars,
And the fierce sun, and restless desert winds.
Still does she smile, watching the centuries
Pass, and has seen kings die and conquerors
fall,
And empires rise -and fade and be no more,
And gods grow old and perish; aye, and
seen
Man striving slowly to his strength—as Nile
O’erspreads the fields in Spring resistlessly
A little while, ere to his former bed
He sinks back, and the fields are green
again,
With no sign where of late the river lay,
Save for a little sKme that dries to dust
The first wind whirls away.
So gazes Time
Across the desert of Eternity,
The grim lips curved in the same awful
smile,
And the same secret in the lidless eyes.
N.S.W.
FRITZ S. BURNELL.
The Wrong Brand.
His right hand grasped the steering-wheel,
His left arm clasped the lady;
The night policeman saw them reel
Upon a comet made of steel
Along wide streets and shady.
The night wind whispered near
“He’s wearing out his father’s car!”
“And where were you last night, my boy?”
Inquired his dad next morning.
Then Father’s Hope and Mother’s Joy—
A heart of gold without alloy —
Mean tarradiddles scorning,
Replied, “You told me, Dad. to toil.
I lurnt a little midnight oil!”
N.S.W.

COMMER LORRY — 3-TON CAPACITY.
“COMMER” Motor Lorries
Capacity : t Ton to 7 Tons.
Time was when the “Commer” Motor Lorry was an “innovation.” Time
was when a few enterprising commercial “experimentalists” each installed a
Commer, . and the balance of the business-world watched the new depar-
ture with interest.
Time passed—and established the fact that what had looked like a daring
and costly experiment had proved the finest form of business economy. The
“Commer”—that was an “innovation”—has become
An Absolute Business Necessity.
Bj increasing the radius of your business the “Commer” adds to your list
of customers. Its speed and capacity mean that you can reach and handle
business that formerly might have needed the establishment of a local branch.
The “Commer” Lorry deserves the earliest and fullest investigation by
the merchant or the carrier. It means the expediting of business, the con-
venience of the owner, and the satisfaction of the customer.
Carrying capacity, 1 ton to 7 tons. Engine, 4 cylinders, cast in pairs;
Bosch high tension magneto ; gear driven, also coil and accumulator; chain
drive with chain cases ; automatic lubrication; efficient radiator; three, four
and five speeds forward and one reverse; direct drive on top gears always
in mesh with dog clutch drive; foot brake on driver between gear box and
differential, operated by pedal on foot board ; side hand brakes, operated on
drum of rear wheels; internal expanding positively taken on and oft' (on
larger models a special spring buffer drive is fitted between differential and
gear box, the latter being of the “fool-proof” type) ; complete set of tools,
ball bearings throughout.
Any Type of Body Fitted.

THE WORSHIP OP THE GOLDEN CALF.
The N.S.W. Labor Conference has censured the McGowen Government for assisting Private Enterprise to enter the Iron and steel
business In defiance of the Labor policy of establishing State iron and steel works.
"They have turned aside out of the way which I commanded them, and they have made a molten calf and have worshipped it. And
as soon as he saw the calf and the dancing. Moses’ anger waxed hot, and he cast the tables out of his hand and brake them.”-Exodus xxxil.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-

Cheques. Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to ah extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any

pressly reserved by the publishers.

ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-

of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short

will be regarded as strictly confidential.

conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-

applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-

sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
