THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 6, 1913.
SWEATED GENIUS.
One of the scandals of civilisation has always
been the miserably inadequate way in which
nations pay the men who have served them,
by comparison with the lavish scale on which
they recompense those who have been con-
cerned merely to serve themselves. While
Lord Roberts was winning Britain’s battles,
Blundell Maple was making its furniture.
Lord Roberts is a poor man to-day, as Eng-
lish country squires go, while at least one
descendant of the Tottenham Court-road
trader is worth millions sterling. Kit-
chener, Cromer, Churchill, Meredith,
Curzon, Russell of Ivillowen —add together
the whole of the fortunes owned or be-
queathed by the possessors of these glitter-
ing names, and the sum total will fall well
below that for which some nationally-useless
London financier or stockbroker might draw
his cheque in the certain expectation of hav-
ing it honored. In bygone days a certain
tvne of public man did his bpst to correct an
anomaly which was naturally a source of
chronic irritation to him, by stealing from
the State the difference between the small
sum which it paid him for his services, and
the generous figure which he considered
those services worth. In the reign of
Charles 11. almost all the public officials
were underpaid, and most of them re-adjusted
the inequity by accepting bribes, secret com-
missions and so forth, to their own greater
comfort, but to the manifest detriment of the
Commonweal. Thus it is as certain that
Pepys habitually thieved from the Admir-
alty as it is that Fox drank and that
Casanova was a card-sharper. Of course,
there have been honorable exceptions all
through the ages. We have had them in
Australia —herds of them. They have dif-
fered in many respects, but one characteristic
has been common to practically all of them—-
they have died poor. The life story of old
John Robertson, for whose indigent de-
scendants a public collection is being taken
up in N. S. Wales to-day, is typical
of that of hundreds of public servants who
did their duty according to their lights.
In their cases honesty was its own punish-
ment. The second decade of the 20th cen-
tury finds the cost of living soaring upwards
with a speed that baffles statisticians, re-
viewers, scientists and all the other experts
who in the past have provided soothing ex-
planations of such movements, and mentioned
explicitly when the end might be ex-
pected. New standards of luxury have been
created. Also, many things which used to be
regarded as luxuries have developed into
necessities. The business man who earns his
vast dividends by supplying them, or some
of them, to the public, has no need to turn
a financial hair. His income swells auto-
matically with the bloating prosperity of his
State. The same applies, more or less, to
the architect who builds for him, to the
lawyer who stands by him while he litigates—
in fact, to every class of professional men.
The one citizen who is shut out from the
rush for the cash spoil—provided he remains
honest —is the unfortunate who is required
to battle along on a fixed salary—a salary
fixed, too, in almost every instance, at a
time when the world was not only much
younger, but incomparably simpler and
cheaper in its habits.
No sympathy need be squandered on the
nonentities. A. tragically big proportion of
our politicians figure in this class. They
could not earn their screws at any other job
than the leisurely one which they hold at
present. In the search for first-rate men —
meaning men who would shine, at their own
calling, in any company in the world —one
has to go outside politics. For example, to
the High Court. Chief Justice Griffith
has his obvious defects and weaknesses. He
is no monument of courtesy and toleration
and considerateness in his relations with
those of his learned brethren from whom he
chances to differ. The cast of his mind is
such that he interprets the Constitution in
the narrowest possible fashion. But that
he is a very great man no reasonable person
will attempt to deny. Australia has never
before had a judge or a lawyer of his
calibre, and it has had some good ones in
its time. There is no bigger figure on the
British or American Bench to-day, and if the
opinion of contemporary lawyers of all
types and of every shade of political thought
goes for anything, the verdict of history will
put him on a level with the legal giants of
all time. Incidentally, as Premier, and ulti-
mately as Chief Justice of his own State of
Queensland, as an earnest Federalist, as
Chairman of the body which drafted the Bill
embodying a basis for constituting the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth, and in various other
directions, he has deserved extremely well
of his country. What does his country do
for him? In return for services of the
supremest importance and responsibility, for
work of a most technical and arduous de-
scription, necessitating the discomforts of
constant travel, it pays him £3500 per an-
num, without a pension. The head man in
at least one big Australian store is reputed
to get £5OOO per annum. It is possible to
point to local managers of a dozen foreign
importing firms who do as well —men of
the type, in certain respects, of the genial
citizen who lately hashed up a sewing ma-
chine co.’s affairs. Archbishop Wright, of
Sydney, is paid £2250 per annum, and al-
lowed, in addition, a rent-free house. It
was mentioned in a paragraph only last week
that the Archbishop of Melbourne gets £2OOO,
with extras, and that the diocese expects
to have to pay the next man £3OOO. A high
order of mentality is not demanded. There
may be subtle and tremendous difficulties in
connection with the art of successfully Arch-
bishing in a country like Australia; but they
are not obvious. A series of undistinguished
English parsons have followed one another
in this holy and well-salaried enterprise.
No failures have ever been reported, so one
may take it they all did satisfactorily. By
the general look of things any intelligent,
sober and well-behaved ex-curate could do
as well —provided always he was an English
ex-curate. Fight promoters, theatrical en-
trepreneurs, book-makers, proprietors of
vaudeville circuits and pony racecourses, and
others even less worthy, live uproariously
and leave fortunes when they die. Mere
genius works desperately for what would
scarcely keep these illiterate and non-pro-
ductive, and, in many cases, absolutely unde-
sirable, citizens in vintage champagne and
motor-cars.
Obviously there is something seriously
wrong with a social system which encourages
a Yiddish bookmaker, of a low order of in-
telligence, no particular education, and
deplorable habits, to wax fat and affluent,
while the owner of the very best brains and
character that the race is capable of evolving
has to pinch and save to keep up the appear-
ances essential to his position. This paper
is not a Socialist as the lower orders of
Toryism understand the term ; that is to say,
it does not advocate dispossessing the Hebrew
of his gains. It does not yearn to see the
more successful children of the Ghetto
levelled down, in a financial sense; but it
does claim that the time has arrived when
it would be all in the public interest if the
salaries in a number of callings were lev-
elled up. Failing a resolute move in this
direction, the factor of human nature will
enter into the problem, and our supply of
men for public offices will begin to deteri-
orate. As apnlied to Federal Judges this
will do incalculable harm to the Common-
wealth. Already there is a certain disin-
clination apparent among leaders of the
Australian Bar to accept a seat on the High
Court Bench. The primary reason is that
they can do better, whether as counsel or as
judges, in their respective States. Thus,
Chief Justice Cullen, of N. S. Wales, gets
the same annual salary as Chief Justice
Griffith, of Australia, plus a generous pen-
sion when he retires. The work of the
State Judge is easier; it does not involve
violent unpopularity with any section of the
community; it does not provoke enraged
criticism from any political quarter; and
most of it is carried on within reasonable
distance of home comforts. Parliament
should begin a badly-needed reform cam-
paign by instituting a system of pensions
for High Court Judges. It is not so much
a question of keeping up the standard of
the Bench —though that is, of course, a thing
of vital it is of doing an act of
plain justice. State judges, in almost every
case, are lavishly pensioned. In the United
Kingdom, under the Judicature Act, of
1873, judges of the Supreme Court receive
pensions equal to two-thirds of their salaries.
The Lord Chancellor of England gets
£5OOO per annum on retirement, no matter
how short a time he may have held office.
The Lord Chancellor of Ireland —a far
poorer and less populous country than Aus-
tralia —goes into private life with nearly
£4OOO a year. One of the few re-
spects in which the American Constitution
differs from ours is in the circumstance that,
under it, Federal judges get an annuity on
retiring. There exist in various parts of the
world army and navy pensions, perpetual
or hereditary pensions, political pensions,
civil list pensions, ecclesiastical pensions
and a heap of others. There is no more
justification for any of them than there is
for the granting of pensions to the members
of the High Court of Australia. When the
pensionless Federal judge has lopped off part
of his annual salary for payment into a sink-
ing fund to provide for his old age. or for
the support of his dependents, he is liable to
be a very indigent official indeed. The
larger his family the more he has to sacri-
fice. There has already been one instance
of a Federal judge who, after living the best
vears of his life to the service of Australia,
found himself so situated that, though his
health had failed badly, yet he was not. in
a position to retire. In a self-respecting
community, possessed of the faintest capacity
for gratitude, a situation of that sort should
never arise ; and it is to be hoped that the
tragedy will never be possible again.
THE TWO THINGS THAT STAND
FIRST.
Writing of Freetrade in Denmark. A.
Pesciicke Koedt makes the striking re-
mark that, “consciously or unconsciously,
military defence is everywhere mixed up
with tariff protection.” This is intended
as a gibe at those who believe in the efficacy
of tariff walls to foster industry and advance
prosperity; but Protectionists may accept
the statement at its face value, and find in
it a tribute to the clear view and sincere
purpose of those who, awaking to a full
sense of nationhood, take measures for
guarding that nationhood at every point.
To say that the same man who desires that
the people shall be able to earn a decent
livelihood also desires that they shall use
every means in their power to guard their
hearths and homes is to say that the Pro-
tectionist takes a broad, honest view of the
world as it is, and is awake to the dangers
of modern civilisation as well as its enor-
mous possibilities. Yet in the curiously-
warped view of the Freetrader this attitude
of mind is an offence, and its mere state-
ment is supposed to have in it something of
scorn. It is true that much may be par-
doned to the Danish economist owing to the
peculiar position of his small cowyard in
the Baltic, which represents the modern re-
mains of the empire of Canute or Valde-
mar. Having no industries to protect, and
devoting himself wholly to agriculture,
Freetrade does not look in his eyes the in-
jurious thing it appears to others; and
although in Denmark the business of de-
fence is attended to pretty thoroughly and
22,000 fresh recruits are drilled every year,
yet, after the tramping down of his little
armies by Prussia in 1864, the Dane may be
forgiven if he thinks that a good deal of
his war preparation is waste of time. In
plain fact, Denmark is a falling not a rising
State, and though its people are industrious,
well educated and sufficiently patriotic, its
big national dreams and ideals faded out
and were lost several centuries ago. In
most respects the Dane is a very fine fellow,
but when it comes to speaking in the voice
of nations his is the word of the well-
whipped, tail-down race; not of the people
of new energy, strong hope and big economic
ambition. The economist from Copenhagen
may manage his parish affairs to his own
complete satisfaction, but he is not qualified
to .speak for the world at large.
Taking Koedt’s remark at its plain face
valtie, however, recent Australian history
gives it a good deal of point and force. In
the Commonwealth the matter of armed de-
fence has gone hand in hand with protec-
' tion of industries. A dozen years ago Aus-
tralia rose strong and united, welded into
one nation, and realising how great might
be the destiny of a people who possessed
complete and unchallenged ownership of an
undivided continent. At once two questions
sprang into positions of prime importance
—the fostering of Australian manufactures
by tariff duties and the efficient guarding
of Australian homes'by an armed citizen
force. The demand for these things was
the natural expression of the new nation-
hood. At all hazards the white man’s in-
heritance in the splendid land must be kept
inviolate for the white man’s children, and
by all reasonable means the inheritance it-
self must be developed to provide industrial
employment for its. people. To that demand
the legislators of the country slowly rose.
They introduced Protection of a kind for
industries, and a Defence system of a kind
for safeguarding the national possessions.
In neither did they realise the ideals of the
people; for if one thing has been more ap-
parent than another it is that the alleged
leaders of the people lack the vision to see
and the imagination which the call of the
great future demands. Still something in
the way of Protection and Defence was
attempted, and the people, accustomed to
long and weary waiting before they came to
their own, were prepared to exercise some
patience, believing that, a foundation having
been laid, the work must sooner or later g 0
on to completion. They are still exercising
that patience, while the foolish political
parties toss the ball to and fro.
Military defence and protection of in-
dustries are still linked together in Austra-
lia. At the outset of the Federation they
were linked together by a splendid hope; to-
day they are linked together by something
like a common wrong. On the one hand we
have an alleged Protection which does not
protect, and on the other a system of De-
fence which cannot adequately-defend. Over
the low and insufficient tariff walls cheap
foreign imports are coming in at a greater
rate than ever, while Australia’s little chil-
dren are being trained to fill their places in
the citizen army which should from the very
first have been recruited from the ranks of
strong manhood. Tariff increases, which
might save our young industries and be the
cause of a large increase in our manufac-
turing activities, are delayed because they
do not fit in with the party scheme of poli-
tics ; and, at the same time, all the simple
aids to Defence, in the shape of improved
transit facilities between North and South
and re-arrangement of gauges on the inter-
state railways, are hung up and neglected
because the men in the party struggle are too
blind to see beyond the lobbies and the Cau-
cus. Our parish politics are not confined to
the States Frights that infest our minor legis-
lators. There are men who claim to be large
Australians whose views are just as restricted
and narrow, and who, by their apathy on
great questions or their inability to grasp
the real situation, are handing over the work
by which Australians should earn their
bread to the cheap foreigner, and risking the
destruction of Australian homes at the
hands of the brown Asiatic. Australia has
any number of questions slill to settle (in-
cluding the vital one whether John Smith
shall or shall not have sixpence a day more
than he is paid at the present moment) ;
but the two great matters which overshadow
all others are those which from the birth of
the Federation have been inseparable in the
public mind. The Federal capital may be
called Mvola or O’Malley or plain Mud in
the days to come, but the man or the party
which fearlessly tackles the problem of
nation building must place first things first,
and leave the things which are merely orna-
mental to follow in their natural order.. The
matter of national security, both against
unfair trade competition from without and
the guns and warlike pleasantries of any pos-
sible invader, is above every other; and he
is no statesman, but the poorestvsort of petti-
fogging party politician, who fails to see it.
THE RARE AUSTRALIAN HORSE,
AND THE RARER AUSTRALIAN
HORSEMAN.
From “Cavalryman” :
I see that Defence Minister Pearce talked to the
papers the other day as follows !V-
Two years ago we had not a solitary battery in
Australia for which a special horse tearn was pro-
vided. Now we have horses for every battery. &>me-
thing, therefore, has been done. The horses we Jiave
were all bought locally, and are quite efficient, and
we can get more of them for as many batteries as we
wish. Previously reliance had to be placed on
brewery and quarry horses for battery work, and the
same horses could never be obtained for two consecu-
tive drills.
It is magnificent, but it is mostly incorrect. Only
a few short weeks ago the identical journal which
printed the above was responsible for these depress-
ing truths: —
Complaints are heard throughout the ranks of the
Field Artillery—known prior to the recent reorganisa-
tion of the Citizens’ Forces as the A.F.A. —that as
regards facilities for exercise with the guns the bat-
teries are worse off now than under the old system
when horses had to he hired to draw the guns It is
said that horses are now practically unobtainable for
battery purposes, and officers and men alike are won-
dering where all the horses purchased by the Remount
Department, and trained for artillery purposes, have
gone to.
It was mentioned, casually, that the guns of No. 1
Battery had been in use by the Victorian Battery of
R.A.F.A. during the previous five or six weeks, so
that, during this period, the three metropolitan bat-
teries of field artillery had but one battery of four
guns to drill with. The paper wound up
In the circumstances, it did not matter much, for
in any case there have not been sufficient horses
available for some time past to completely horse even
one battery.
Now, if the trouble in the Balkans has demon-
strated any one fact more clearly than another, it is
that efficient artillery is of priceless value in modern
warfare. But the best of guns are useless without
the right sort of horses, arrd plenty of them. Has
Australia sufficient good-class horses available for the
military exigencies of the moment? And is it
making any serious preparations to meet the require-
ments of the future?
To both of these urgent queries the citizen with in-
side knowledge will promptly say “No.” The first
delusion which the city-dwelling Australian (and a
lamentably large proportion of Australians are city-
dwellers) needs to get out of his mind is that the
backblocks are teeming with the splendid animals
over which our horse-poets have expended so much
superfluous ink. Marvels of the type of Pardon, the
son of Reprieve, are as rare in the remotenesses of this
great country as honest fruiterers. Thanks to careful
breeding on the part of big station-owners and others,
Australia had a useful line in horseflesh to boast of
a couple of decades ago. But the big estates are
bursting broadcast under the influence of the land
tax. And, anyhow, the vogue of the horse, except as
a racing-cim-gambling machine, has passed. The
era of the motor is here. People no longer lay them-
selves out to acquire smart hacks and good-looking
boggy horses. In country pubs, and other places
where bucolic idlers and yappers and braggarts fore-
gather, one hears more nowadays of carburetters and
cylinders and the blastiferous price of tyres than one
does of sloping shoulders and the beauty of a game
head and a flat pair of legs. In short, the factors of
private enthusiasm and sporting rivalry, which have
done so much in the past for horse-breeding, may be
dismissed from the national mind. They will never
serve Australia again to any extent worth mention-
ing. The second delusion which requires to be dis-
pelled is that Australians, as a race, are skilful and
experienced horsemen and horse-masters. It might
have been so in the old, wild days, that we still read
about occasionally in the Strand Magazine, the Lon-
don Ladies’ Home Bleat, etc., etc., but in these
prosaic times we are about as much at home in the
saddle or the stable-yard as the average inhabitant of
Glasgow or the Outer Hebrides. British and Conti-
nental cavalry men are required, as part of their
training, to ride across country. For years Lord
Roberts would have no one on his staff who was
not willing to follow him over any fence. Amateur


riders, and of no mean quality, abound in the jump-
ing events of the English and Irish horse-shows. At
Australian horse-shows the same iron faces turn up,
aboard the different jumpers, year after year. They
belong to pros., who make a living by steering the
circus-animals, which appear regularly at these en-
tertainments, over the tall timber at a guinea or so
a time. The amateur who takes anything of the sort
on is regarded by his friends as a lunatic. So far
as hunting is concerned, it is poor in quality and
confined to an infinitesimally small section. The net
result of these things, plus the bloating of the big
cities, and the decline of the horse as an animal of
fashion, is that about 90 per cent, of Australians
simply cannot ride as a soldier understands the term.
It is probable that this country, which the simple
Briton pictures as seething with buckjumpers be-
stridden by icily calm youths in red .shirts and bowie
knives, is almost as incompetent in the matter of
horsemanship as Japan, whose cavalrymen, during the
last manoeuvres, fell crashing to the earth practically
every time their mounts shied or pecked.
What does the Defence Department propose to do
to ensure that (1) Australia shall set about acquir-
ing afresh some of that huge mass of equine capital
which it has squandered so recklessly of late years,
in the “Eastern” (meaning the Indian and Ceylon)
trade, in the S’African war, and by way of sales of
blood and other stock to Japan ; and (2) that Aus-
tralia’s citizen soldiers shall be able to ride and
handle the animals when they materialise? As re-
gards the first question, it is plain, for the reasons
cited above, that to rely wholly on Private Enter-
prise, either now or at any future time, will amount
to madness. A Government horse-breeding establish-
ment, on a big scale, is a first essential. Here, with
the help of the right class of sires and mares, a
steady stream of animals, suitable for cavalry and
mounted infantry remounts, and for artillery work,
might be turned out. There would be nothing like
enough of them, to start with, to meet the present
furious demand. So it would be an admirable thing
if all the different States could be prevailed upon to
subsidise certain stallions of recognised worth, and
supply them with certificates of soundness and merit
generally. (The Commonwealth has no power to it-
self interfere, in this way, with the internal trade
and commerce of the States, nor will it have, until
the electors have, by referendum, agreed to an amend-
ment of the Trade and Commerce clause in the Con-
stitution.) All uncertificated stallions would be under-
stood by breeders to be, ipso facto, undesirable in
some way or other. This would do a lot to check the
output of useless weeds, which is one of the marked
-features of horse.-breeding in Australia to-day. The
offspring of these sound and well-shaped sires would
need to be considered by skilled buyers. There is a
general feeling all over the Commonwealth that the
nation’s horse-trading is being unskilfully done. The
authorities have, admittedly, bought some good
animals at a reasonable price here and there, but
they have also bought a large number that would be
villainously dear at any price. Let the buying be put
in the hands of recognised experts, appointed in
every case for no other reason than that they know
all there is to be known about the fine points of a
horse. The depots where the' young animals are
broken in are fairly languishing at the present time
for decent horse-masters. From Enoggera, in Queens-
land, to Guildford, in Westralia, the same dreary tale
of incompetency is heard in regard to the way green
horses are mishandled by the butchers who mas-
querade as skilled breakers, and draw the State’s good
money on that false pretence. One depot only emerges
with a more or less clean sheet from such investiga-
tions as it has been possible to make —namely, the
one in Victoria. It is significant that it has been
supervised not by soldiers but by professional horse-
exporters, men who know from A to Z the game of
turning out good-conditioned and well-mannered
steeds. But the immediate need is the establishment,
without delay, of a State stud farm or haras, such as
France, Russia and most other civilised countries
possess. Ideal places for such a venture are to be
found in the Northern Territory and North-West
Queensland. Given an experienced supervisor and
good stock to begin with, the perfect climate and
environment could be relied on to do the rest. In a
little while the grotesque complaint that Australia—
once the horsiest of countries —had not sufficient
steeds to pull its gun-carriages, would cease to be
heard. And in the course of time—after our break-
ing-in depots have been reformed, and after our
young men have learned not merely to talk about
horses, but to ride and drive them —the Common-
wealth artillery arm may begin to find itself on a
level with that of Bulgaria and Montenegro.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Apple Speck Elections.
After all the noise with which the inhabi-
tants of the Apple Speck have disturbed the
sleep of the summer-tourists during the past
week or so, the local political battle has
resulted in parties remaining exactly as they
were before the elections. Premier Solo-
mon’s side of the Assembly still numbers 16
“Liberal” noses, and John Earle’s side re-
tains its 14 pairs of more or less horny
hands. Norman Cameron, the elongated
Tasmanian-bred Scotch paradox whose ex-
travagant humor filled the Solomon Gov-
ernment with jumps and deadly fear during
the past Parliament, but who voted on the
Tory side in all important divisions, has
disappeared from view. Yet as his place
has been taken by the ancient and dread-
fully dull Jonathan Best, who was for a
good many weary, dreary sessions Chairman
of Committees, and was displaced last Anril
by the Cameron person, it is hard to dis-
cover how the House or the Ministry or the
country is likely to benefit by the change.
Cameron was sometimes entertaining; Best
is always ponderous and preachy. There the
difference ends. Cameron often threatened
the Government; so will Best. There the
similarity begins, and the end is not yet.
As ex-Minister and ex-Chairman of Com-
mittees llean is amonest the slain, it seems
probable that Best will demand and obtain
the Speakership, which, it is understood,
Davies does not intend to take this time.
The onlv other changes made by the elec-
tors are that Lib. lawyer Fullerton dis-
places Lib. builder Valentine in Denison,
and L. F. Giblin replaces W. Sheridan on
the Labor side in the same electorate; Lib.
Sadler squeezes out Lib. McKenzie in
Bass, where also A. Anderson, union secre-
tary, displaces Jas. Guy, Workers’ Political
League general secretary. Tim place in this
electorate formerly filled by Bakhap, who
is to try for a Lib. Senate seat. 1S n< 7 w
occupied by a new man named Hayes. An
elderly coit named Cotton takes llean s
job in Franklin. Though the party numbers
are unchanged, there is a sort of gam to tne
Government in that a couule or three dan-
gerous by-elections have been avoided n>
holding a general election. But as ex-1 re-
nder Lewis is pretty sure to got a chance or
climbing to the Supreme Court Bench
within a few months, there will be a by-
election for Denison, and Labor will
probably win the seat. Parties will then be
lo all, and the deadest sort of deadlock ever
heard of in the world of Parliamentary Gov-
ernment will ensue. While Solomon owns
a majority of one on the floor of the Assem-
bly, he had better use it to alter the Hare
electoral law. Otherwise there will be abso-
lutely no business doing for perhaps years—-
nothing but elections. The easiest way to
overcome the trouble would appear to be the
alteration of the Electoral Act to provide
for an odd number of representatives in each
of the five divisions, instead of the even
number now obtaining. Then one side or
the other would always have a majority of
at least one ; Under the law as it stands at
present, it is quite possible for neither side
to have a majority of members in the House,
though there may be a clear majority of
votes cast at an election in favor of the
candidates of one of the two parties. It is
an interesting situation for the student of
electoral systems to watch, but for the tax-
payer who expects Parliament to do some-
thing useful for him, it is not much joy.
A Leader Who Requires Gagging.
The egregious Joseph Cook has only been
in command of the hosts of Liberalism for a
matter of days, yet already he has made as
many damaging mistakes as his predecessor
would have been guiltv of in a year. They
have been mostly of the niggling variety—
the sort that stamp their perpetrator as the
owner of a small and absurdly provincial
mind. Thus he complained bitterly, last
week, that no Liberal had been invited to the
Government Dockyard picnic in Sydney. As
a matter of fact, an invitation had been sent
to him, also to C. G. Wade and other Lib-
erals. But, in any case, the voicing of such
“wrongs” constitutes the politics not of a
nation, but of a parish. Alfred Deaktn
has his faults, and so have Forrest and W.
11. Irvine and a dozen other prominent Lib-
erals that might be named ; but it is impos-
sible to picture any of them moaning pub-
licly over the fact that he had not been
bidden to a Dockyard picnic. About the
same time that the Dockyard wail appeared,
a Sydney daily published the following :■ —
Mr. Cook took the complaint of mismanagement at
the City Council’s fish market as an instance of a
Socialistic failure. “There must be failure in all
these matters,” said he. “The slightest test of prac-
tical experience seems to bring down these Socialistic
houses altogether.”
One expects that sort of imbecility from very
old ladies, the more illiterate class of com-
mercial men and so forth; but in the mouth
of the leader of a responsible political party
it is amazing. Instead of “practical experi-
ence bringing down Socialistic houses,” a
steady increase of municipal Socialism has,
for years, been the hall-mark of every pro-
gressive modern city. Such great British
towns as Birmingham, Bradford, Manches-
ter, Liverpool. Glasgow and Leeds own and
administer “Socialistic houses” which con-
trol every conceivable sort of markets ; also,
Turkish baths, plunge baths, swimming
baths and lavatories; ferries, river steamers
and docks; libraries, parks, railways and
real estate. Colchester owns and exploits its
own oyster beds —and after Private Enter-
prise has poisoned a few more citizens with
Middle Harbor oysters, Sydney may begin
to think about following suit. The chief
attractions at nearly all the big European
spas and watering-places are Socialistic. In
this connection The Bulletin quoted from
the S. M. Herald, only last week, as
follows :
The municipality of Manly has no enterprise such
as we find in the seaside resorts of the Old World.
As a contrast to Manly take Torquay The corpora-
tion’s proposals include a modem installation of
medicated, electric and seaweed baths, the improve-
ment of the Princess Gardens, the erection of a public
library, municipal offices, town hall, and pavilion for
municipal orchestra. The Princess Gardens con-
tain a theatre, a cafe, a restaurant and roof promen-
ade, dressing-rooms, etc.
The Herald had no rebukes to direct at
Torquay for its Socialistic tendencies, as evi-
denced in these enterprises. On the con-
trary, it expressed the hope that Manly would
find in them a source of inspiration. If
there is one sociological fact that is surer
and more patent than another, it is that the
more the modern municipality gets of Social-
ism the less it is appalled at it. Which is
merely another way of saying that munici-
pally-run services have not proved failures in
the vast majority of cases, and that the poli-
tician who argues to the contrary is either a
liar or an uninstructed ass. This paper s
view is that Joseph Cook is the latter
variety of calamity. In that event, Liberal-
ism’s best hope will be to firmly gag him.
But history presents no instance of a politi-
cal party which surged to glorious victory
under the sway of a firmly-gagged leader.
Pulsford and Protection.
Edward Pulsford, who was dropped from
the Senate as soon as possible after his old
Freetrade fetich had been cast overboard,
has contributed to an English magazine,
The Burden of Protection, an article on
fiscal affairs in the Commonwealth As
furnishing examples of the sort of argu-
ments which a Freetrader can extract from
awkward figures and circumstances, this
statement of the case is not uninteresting.
Manufactures have increased enormously
under Protection, and so have natural pro-
ductions. The average person would leason
from this that a Protective policy was shown
to be an advantage to manufacturing enter-
prise while in no way injurious to primary
producers. Not so Pulsford who pro-
fesses to see in the figures a proof that Aus
tralian prosperity is due to primary produc-
tion in spite of industries which should never
have been fostered. Again, he quotes List
as having said that the tropics are non-manu-
facturing and non-competitive ; . and from
this the remarkable economist infers that
\ustralia (whose settled portions are
mainly outside the tropics) should be also
non-manufacturing and non-competitive.
Further, he asserts that Australian prosper-
ity is largely dependent on the markets of
Europe, and yet ignores the fact that Aus-
tralian manufactures, fostered by Protection,
mav help to make us independent of those
markets. But Pulsford can do better than
draw false deductions from ascertained fact.
In a later paragraph he makes the state-
ment that, though Australia gives to the I ro-
tectionist politician and manufacturer th«
power to break down the imports, and
hough they have tried to exercise that
nnwer, the flood of imnorts still increases.
The truth, of course, is that it is because
politicians have not used their power that
imports are increasing. Finally, the busted
economist makes the usual remarks of a
Freetrader about the increased cost of liv-
ing. but characteristically fails to note that
while it costs a lot more to live, the people
have never been so well able to pay that
cost and to take their best girl to the picture
show after it has been paid. Also, he fails
to observe that the increased cost is very
largely due to increased rents, which are
mainly due to the congestion of certain
towns, which is mainly due to the policy
favored by the party of which Pulsford is
one of the latest remnants.
The Pacific Naval Problem.
Richard Arthur, M.L.A., writes : —-
Your article, “To Safety—with Danger in the
Road,’’ has fully persuaded me that, for some years
to come, the fate of Australia may be decided in the
North Sea. Japan will strike at Australia if opportu-
nity offers, just as it struck at Russia. But the pre-
liminary and essential condition is that Great Britain’s
hands must be tied. Japan would not dare to move as
long as the British fleet was in being in the North
Sea. The statesmen of Nippon could not venture to as-
sume that the fleet was tied immovably there. Germany
is not going to war simply to pull chestnuts out of the
fire for Japan. In fact, a Japanese attack on Australia
might bring about an Anglo-German alliance to com-
bat the Yellow Peril, which the Kaiser foresaw 20
years ago. On the other hand, should Great Britain
and Germany come to a death grip in the North Sea,
Japan’s psychological moment would have arrived with
a vengeance. At its leisure it could throw 100,000
veteran troops and more into Queensland, and with
that as a base over-run the whole continent.
It clearly follows that, for some years to come,
Australia’s true defence lies in the existence of over-
whelming British naval superiority in the North Sea
and Mediterranean. In the meantime Australia’s chief
business is to so increase its population that its ex-
panding revenue may allow the building of super-
Dreadnoughts at once. According to the Henderson
programme, our next battleship is not due till 1920,
which is ridiculous. We should order two more at
once and a dozen submarines, which are being recog-
nised as the best form of coastal defence.
Later on, Australia and Maoriland must stand on
their own feet. It is folly to depend on Canada or
the United States. Canada knows well that it is in
a very different position to what we are, and is, there-
fore, not called upon to make the same sacrifices for
its existence. It knows that it has the United States
fleet behind it should any aggression on its territory be
attempted. Whether the States would move to our
aid from sentimental motives is another question alto-
gether. The Yankee Government might, however, have
a far more weighty argument brought to bear on it.
It might be told plainly by the British Government
that, unless the United States were prepared to play
the part of a “good white” in the Pacific, Great
Britain would no longer remain the tacit upholder of
the Monroe Doctrine, and would refuse to interfere
with any designs that Germany might have on South
America. That would bring the great Republic to a
sense of its moral obligations quicker than anything
else.
Finally, I again maintain that the programme
should be: The Australasian Dreadnoughts in the
North Sea for some years to come, the quadrupling of
the present immigration stream, more battleships to
be ordered at once, and three or six months’ con-
tinuous training for our citizen army.
And The Bulletin merely repeats its old
opinion that while it is good to have gaols
to hold the burglars, and policemen to patrol
the street, it is wise to also have a lock
on your own door. Wherefore, while Great
Britain ought to be relieved of all expense
in the protection of these parts, so that it
might devote every available penny to its
preparations in the North Sea and Medi-
terranean, Australia ought to keep its
Dreadnoughts. They are the lock on our
door.
A Serious Flaw in Defence.
“Don Q.’s” informative volley :
The three military rifles with which the three great
English-speaking races hope to defend their lands
are: —
(1) The Springfield rifle (United States) ;
(2) The Ross rifle (Canada) ;
(3) The Lee-Enfield Mark 111. (British Empire).
The Canadian Military Gazette, commenting on the
Palma trophy competition (perhaps the greatest rifle
shooting match in the world), recently stated that the
Springfield rifle completely outclassed the Ross rifle.
Now, recall the riot at the last Bisley competitions
over the question whether the Canadians should be
permitted to fire with the superior Ross weapon, and
you see where we are with the Lee-Enfield Mark 111.
as compared with the Springfield. Australia’s small-
arms factory was opened a few months ago, and Min-
ister Pearce iterated that it had the most up-to-date
rifle plant in the world. Well, why is the most up-to-
date plant turning out a third-rate article? Six
months ago it was announced that Britain had decided
to refit its army with a rifle which Colonel Seely, of
the War Office,' told the Commons would be equal to
the world’s best. The intention of the War Office was
to put 100 of the new rifles in the hands of army ex-
perts, so that thorough and practical tests might be
made. Where was our Department when those
samples were handed out ? Having adopted the policy
of blindly accepting any old recommendation of the
conservative British Y/ar Office, it was surely up to
our people to smell a rat or an elephant when the dis-
carding of the Mark 111. was made known by the
W.O. The Springfield military rifle (admittedly the
best in the world, and made in the U.S. by that use-
less fraud, Socialism) has a muzzle velocity of 2750 ft.
per second; trajectory, 800 yards. The text-book
says of the wretched Mark 111. that its muzzle velocity
is' 2000 ft. per second. The trajectory is not given,
but experts quote it at 600 yards. But although the
velocity is put down at 2000 ft., it is actually some-
what less. In the Mark 111. type, the breech mechan-
ism was found so weak that the cordite charge had to
be reduced to avoid the blowing-out of breech blocks.
This weakness our Lithgow factory may have remedied,
but I do not think so. The first two rifles made there
have a better finish than the imported article; but
finish and alteration of type are two different things.
I ask the Minister for Defence these questions: (1)
How many thousands of the Mark 111. rifles does his
factory propose to manufacture? (2) Has the factory
strengthened the breech mechanism of this type? (3)
What steps has the Department taken in the matter of
producing an up-to-date Australian type of weapon?
(4) Will his Department obtain a sample of the Spring-
field rifle, England’s latest rifle, and the most modern
Continental military rifle, with the object of providing
Australia with a first-class weapon ?
Bigjohn Forrest has been tying himself
into a large knot in the West. As a Fusion-
ist he had, of course, to curse the Labor
Government and the Referenda proposals.
Things had been going to perdition, said
Bigjohn, “and the Referenda proposals were
the last straw that broke the camel’s back.”
The Labor party “would destroy itself in
time” ; but just now Socialism was bringing
loss and ruin on the State, and if the Refer-
enda proposals were carried they “might as
well throw up the sponge.” That was Big-
john talking in the usual large, vague way
at a political meeting. But presently the
same Bigjohn was switched on to propose
“The State and Trade of Westralia” at the
opening of a new warehouse, and this is how
he did it: —
D and W. Murray, Ltd., had shown great enter-
prise in building such a magnificent structure. The
co had shown not only its faith in the country, but
had also given an indication of the prosperity that
had come to Westralia during recent years.
Then Bigjohn proceeded to quote figures to
prove that the man who had told the vague
yarn about tottering camels and last straws
and ruin and sponges had been pulling the
fool-public’s leg. How long will the electors
stand this sort of flagrant political dishonesty
in its public men?
The familiar yarns about the “good old
times” were uncorked once more at Welling-
ton (M.L.) the other day, when the windy
place had an anniversary. It was a bit of
bad luck that on this occasion figures were
given which permit one to test the quality of
those alleged good times. In 1545 tjie Public
Service at Wellington numbered 34, with
salaries ranging from £BOO to £4O. The
average works out at £l57 —not as much as
the average flax-cutter can knock out now.
Then, taking the import returns, a few values
run out this way: Butter, Is. 3d.; flour,
£2O 155.; wheat, 10s. (bushel) ; potatoes, £7
(ton) ; coal, £4 (ton) ; bacon, Bd. These
prices are worked out on the declared values
for Customs tax purposes, and at least 50
p.c. must be fastened on to arrive at the
retail price. Where, then, do the “good
times” come in? A man got much less pay,
and, on these figures, he also got less tucker,
etc., for the little pay that he did get. He
simply pulled through because, poor clod, his
“wants” were few. The youngster didn’t ask
for jam because it didn’t know that jam was
any more obtainable than slices of the moon.
But it didn’t follow that the youngster
wouldn’t have liked jam ; and life is a very
different thing for the youngster now that
there is jam.
When a company cannot pay a larger divi-
dend than 10 p.c., in addition to carrying a
big sum over to visible reserves and stuffing
other sums into invisible funds, its affairs
are beginning to look rather blu|e. At least,
such is the natural inference from the re-
marks of W. C. Goddard, chairman of the
Sydney Ferries, who has just declared that
there must be “a limit beyond which the
company cannot go,” and has foretold an
increase in the price charged for periodical
tickets. The real trouble appears to be not
that the company is not paying decent re-
turns on the original capital, but that it is
not paying enough to those who, speculating
on the city’s congestion, have recently pur-
chased shares at big prices. The question
which the directors of the concern naturally
ask is why the Sydney landlord should have
the game all to himself, when those who own
the means of transit between the houses of
the people and their daily work have an
equally important monopoly by means of
which they can squeeze the public. The pub-
lic is a slow beast, and its vision is limited,
but the last word of the Sydney Ferries
should awaken a large number of those who
take their daily risks on the harbor to a
knowledge of the fact that a congestion
policy is merely promoted for their plunder,
and that the increase in the cost of periodi-
cal tickets on the boats is a mere nothing
to some other things that will come along if
the congestionists are given a free hand.

The Bulletin receives from Queensland a
circular headed “Federal Secession Fund,”
appealing for money to “eventually over-
throw this absurd Federal form of govern-
ment.” The promoters don’t mention what
sort of guns they are going to buy ; but it
is made clear that they want to restore the
old order and “deliver the glorious States
out of the hands of the political Philistines.”
Incidentally, and as the great reason why
the fund should be established, it is said
that “Mr. T. A. Dibbs recently attributed
the failure to raise a paltry three millions
by N. S. Wales to the eviction of Lord Den-
man and the pernicious clauses in the Gas
Bill.” The brilliant asses who issue the
circular evidently don’t know that Denman
is G.-G. of the Commonwealth, and that it
was a blow aimed at the Commonwealth
which, in Dibbs’s opinion, disgusted Cohen.
Also, thev apparently don’t know that the
Gas Bill was a State matter, and their pro-
posal is to give the States more powers so
that they may introduce still more of these
disastrous measures. In fact, as a sample
of muddle-headedness the “Federal Seces-
sion Fund” circular is almost unique even
in State Fright literature —and that is say-
ing a good deal.
While a necessity, until mankind devises
a better medium of exchange, the industry
of getting gold out of the earth is an econo-
mic foolishness. Millions of men all the
world over, aided by hundreds of millions of
capital, are engaged in digging holes in the
ground, dredging rivers, removing moun-
tains, pounding hard rocks to sand, and em-
ploying every device that engineering and
chemical science can supply, and the out-
come of it all is the winning of a metal that
is for all practical purposes of considerably
less value.than brass. After all, what man-
kind wants during its sojourn below is
tucker and clothes and houses and motor-
cars and picture shows, and all sorts of
other things that give comfort and happi-
ness and knowledge and amusement and
general welfare, and what we are here for
in the long run is to each do our share in
producing these things. Intrinsically the
shining bits of metal we call sovereigns are
much less valuable in this respect than
trouser buttons; and the discovery of some-
thing better than gold as the medium of. ex-
change would set free a lot of enterpiising,
intelligent and industrious people to pro-
duce the things mankind really wants, in-
stead of material'for the counters that re-
present those things. For instance, about
20 men, two suction gas-engines, a battery
and various accessories, several horses and
drays and a few dogs (interested but not
active participants) toil madly for a fort-
nightly yield that consists of a bar of yellow
metal very considerably smaller than half a
brick, which bar, if it were not that gold
is the medium of exchange, would probably
be of less value than a similar bar of lead.
Somehow, it doesn’t seem a commensurate
return for so much industry and intelligence
and perspiration and bad language. The re-
former who will devise a system whereby
such energy will be directed to producing
useful things, instead of their common de-
nominator, will be of more service than a
State Governor. A lot of good tucker and
clothing and tools and other items is use-
lessly converted into unnecessary and dan-
gerous holes in the ground by the unfortu-
nate practice of looking for gold in the place
where it isn’t, and if gold wasn’t WPrth
looking for people would soon grow out of
this wasteful habit.

Sydney.
The S. M. Herald continues to find joy in
the Australian navy, and particularly in the
fact that Sydney is to reap the benefits
which belong to a first-class naval port. It
is cheerful to find the Tory thus coining
into line with national thought, even though
his vision is a little obscured by an ecstatic
dream of the further congestion of Sydney.
But although the Herald has been con-
verted there is nothing of humility or con-
trition about its attitude. Assuming the
pose of omniscience, it leaves its readers to
infer that it has all the time been in favor
of a local navy, but at the same time warns
Australia that, although the fleet unit which
is being constructed is an excellent thing for
Port Jackson, and useful in that it removes
a burden from the British taxpayer, it really
doesn’t of itself amount to a row of pins in
the matter of defence. Referring to the
recent keel-laying and rivet-driving at
Cockatoo Island, the Tory paper thus de-
livers its soul: —
If the British navy loses its supremacy, for some
time ahead at least, no effort that our population can
make could ensure us more than temporary safety
against even a second-class Power.
This may he true, or it may not. But if it
is only half true it is the kind of implied
challenge to which the Commonwealth can
give but one answer. If our best effort can-
not in certain circumstances insure our
safety, our second-best effort must in most
cases secure our speedy damnation.
• —♦-
Bast week there landed back in Sydney a
grazier whose mouth was full of praise of
the green Argentina fields that are blessed
and fertilised by Private Enterprise. The
S. M. Herald very naturally gave him his
head, and he got off the old gags that “go”
as splendidly as nigger minstrel chestnuts.
His remarks may be compressed into this
form :
(1) Private Enterprise is encouraged, and “great
development” has resulted.
(2) Capital is “pouring” into the country.
(3) There are 5000 miles of railway in course of
construction, and all the lines, with one or two small
exceptions, are privately owned. It is difficult to
make comparisons as regards fares and freights, but
he thinks both are a little cheaper than in Australia.
(4) There is a land tax which is used for Defence
purposes, but “it is not excessive.”
(5) There are not many Englishmen in the country.
But there are over 2,000,000 Italians and large num-
bers of Russians.
(6) The producers told him that “up to the pre-
sent” the Beef Trust had helped and not hindered
them.
(7) Where Australia has “only little patches of rich
land, the Argentine has hundreds of miles. If they
had all their, lands in use, and knew how to farm pro-
perly—the majority of the farmers in the Argentine
are backward in this respect —it would be a bad look-
out for us, for they could almost keep the whole
World going.”
Is the Herald becoming reckless in its old
age, or didn’t it notice that its traveller was
loaded? The average Australian, reading
hurriedly in a jolting tram, would probably
sum it up this way:—
(H Despite all the encouragement to Private En-
terprise and the consequent “great development,” the
country has not attracted the Britisher in quantities.
It has only managed to acquire 2,000,000 Italians
and a large number of Russians.
(2) Capital is “pouring” into the country—-despite
a land tax!
(3) The returned voyager is not certain whether
the P.E. railway fares and freights are cheaper than
on the Government-owned lines of Australia. Either
he is a slinshod investigator, or he has no striking
figures to offer.
(4) The land is taxed to provide for Defence—-and
Argentina’s Defence is not to be despised. Loud
applause if the reader is not directly interested in
land.
(5) Not many Englishmen in the countrv. Then
the Argentine policy is not tending to the enthusiastic
celebration of Empire Day. Impossible place for a
fervent loyalist.
(6) The Beef Trust, up to the present, has helped
and not hindered the producer. This fits in with the
worst stories told about it. The Droducer is slimed
before being swallowed.
(7) When Argentina pours in a few more
millions of people and uses its vast lands to their
utmost capacity, where will far-off Australia stand
when the markets for its raw products are lost?
Won’t it be rather late then for the S. M. Herald to
barrack for Protection and Australian industries?
Still the S. M. Herald can’t be bothered
with such problems. The dear old soul may
be expected to just heave a patient sigh and
get on with the mending of the Liberal
patty’s socks.
From the Stipendiary Corporal:—
In Constantinople Enver Bey sits very tight, lis-
tening to the Allies screaming bluff beyond the lines
of Chataldja. But Enver knows it is bluff, and that
if armed intervention can be stalled off the Allies
are beaten. Bulgaria’s ruler has forbidden the publi-
cation of the returns of killed and wounded. Ac-
cording to Philip Gibbs, of the Graphic., the number
was “semi-officially” announced as approximately
70,000, and he thinks it was more. But even
70,000 means one in every four wounded or killed
and, when Bulgaria realises the awful extent of that
toll, what a wailing there will be! To quote Gibbs
some more, the Bulgarian “victories were io expen-
sive, in human life, that, in spite of their rapidity,
they almost failed in their effect.... Like sprinters
in a race they had run themselves out”—and by the
time they had recovered their wind Chataldja was
well-nigh impregnable, and fresh troops were pouring
in from Asia Minor. If the Turk can hang out a
few more weeks, the Allies will be glad to make peace
on almost any terms.

The last laps of “Milestones” are being
run off at Sydney Criterion, completing a
season of 10 weeks, which shows that the
artistic play has as good a chance in Aus-
tralia as it has anywhere else.
“Faust” will misbehave himself for the
last time in Sydney on the night of the day
after this family journal bursts upon a
gratified Commonwealth. Allen Doone has
announced his intention of presenting, or
“Sweet County Kerry” at the
Theatre Royal, on Saturday, the 15th.
The Doone season of “pretty” plays at
the Palace will end to-morrow night with
the last performance of “Old Donegal.” On
Saturday William Anderson will commence
a five-weeks’ distribution of drama, the first
helping being Jo Smith’s Australian stage
story, “The Bush Girl.” Eugenie Duggan
and Cyril Mackay will share the bulk of the
limelight.

Bitter words from “Henri Singemoine” :—-
In the golden days when all the World rode
bicycles, and ladies affected small straw hats tilted
rakishly over their noses, , and everyone was many
years younger, the vogue .was for the Beautiful in
dancing, as in so many other things. Letty Lind,
sister of Australia’s inimitable Fan Dango, discov-
ered the uses of the voluminous skirt, and in one dis-
tracting act slew the business of the conventional
ballerina dead. Every second well-to-do female,
with decent feet and ankles, proceeded to acquire the
new allurement. Thousands of drawing-rooms re-
sounded daily and nightly fo the ponderous boundings
and sickening falls of maidens absorbed in well-
meant strivings to be swan-like. Willowy bendings,
now to this side, now to that; graceful little runs
here and there; demure, though somewhat purpose-
less, leaps in the air; a waving of white arms
and a constant exposure of even whiter teeth —these
were the lovelinesses that charmed us in Home and
Theatre alike during the ’9o’s.
But who aspires to be ‘swan-like in these stark
times ? No , one! Our women golf in any old
clothes and the thickest of boots; drive motor-cars;
surf-bathe till they are brown, if not black, in the
face; and as a result of it all they are adding to
their stature and brawn with awful speed. The
The baboon-like Gaby Glide.
4
stage, as always, reflects the tendency of the period.
Consider, for example, the *Gaby Glide, now being
perpetrated nightly at Her Majesty's Theatre, Syd-
ney. What would the genteel critics of 20 years
ago have sa-'d about it ? What indeed! As a mat-
ter of fact, it is calculated to cause even one hard-
ened by scores of visits to Coogee and Manly, and
inured to the ways of ballrooms where the practices
of London’s Grafton Galleries are imitated, to sit up
and take a certain amount of notice. Its dominant
note is hideousness. It suggests a corroboree at La
Perouse, a “oush” revel at a harbor-side pleasure
resort, the aanse du ventre of old Port Said or
modern Paris, and a mult'tude of other ungraceful
movements. It is enough to make Terpsichore turn
in her grave.
Some questions that occur to the astounded on-
looker are: (1) Why does Gabv elect to glide in this
weirdly unalluring fashion? (*2) Did ex-King Manoel
of Portugal ever see her at it,' and, if so, how is it
he wasn’t immediately “choked off” for life? (3)
What appeal does it make to the public? I have
never seen Gaby Deslys in action. She may execute
her glide in such a fashion as to render it attractive.
Some women (about one in a million) are like all
children, inasmuch as they are unable to look un-
graceful in any circumstances. There are no
females of this sort at Her Majesty’s. The galvanic
grins, the brief, lumbering hand-gallops from place
to place, the preposterous wagglings of those who
commit the Gaby Glide are merely repulsive. They
The Tango.
are such manoeuvres as might have been performed
by the first cave family that discovered the secret
of brewing: strong drink, and, partaking thereof, felt
the necessity of working off the effects.
The Buzzing Bee turn is along similar lines. One
party to the offence canters briskly in the direction
of the other, who thereupon sprints up-stage, and
two or three times round it; both amble towards each
other, close and sing a stave or two—interesting,
possibly, to bee-keepers and apiologists generally,
but carrying no significance to the average member
of the public—after which they perform a clean
break, and repeat the process anew. There is no-
thing beautiful, or quaint, or skilful, or artistically
satisfying about it. Anyone possessed of a fair
amount of nerve or hide, and a sound pair of legs,
could do it after a couple of hours’ practice. Like
the One-step, the Bunny Hug, the Turkey Trot, and
all their offshoots, it is anarchistic. It knows no
definite laws. The only essential is to keep moving.
For the rest, if one of the principals climbed into
the flies, and leapt therefrom through a trap-door
in the stage, and so out to the nearest bar, in pur-
suit of drink, the procedure would arouse no com-
ment. He would return to find his partner still
buzzing, and the audience satisfied that all was well.
And probably, as he resumed his duties, the lady
herself would not suspect that anything unusual had
happened to their dance but for the wanderer’s
newly-acquired odor of alcohol. ;
The Tango is our old, old friend, the Cachuca,
plus a certain amount of grotesquerie. It is a new
The Buzzing Bee.
dance in the sense only that a waltz would be new
if the parties interrupted the conventional steps by
throwing hand-springs or kicking each other. The
lady swathes herself in the customary shawl-like gar-
ment of old Madrid, bashes a gigantic black velvet
hat over her eyes, and strikes one of those aloof,
repellent attitudes which characterise the stage senor-
hita. Her accomplice does his best to look like a
hidalgo. They paw the boards in unison, while the band
supplies music of the “Toreador"’ brand. Then the
Tango proper begins. Thrusting one leg forward the
male executes a scride of outrageous length. The
female backs on the same lines. The movement
brings them to their knees. They thereupon get up
and repeat the feat, and continue to do so, witn
brief intervals of dancing, till the orchestra cries
enough, or the audience yawns, or throws missiles.
If it is a dance, then the writer is prepared to hear
serious argument in favor of the view that a stout
duck fleeing from the poulterer is the performer of
steps which are worthy of human imitation.
The dead finish of repulsiveness is reached, how-
ever, in the Political Ballet. Boiled down to its
first essentials it simply amounts to, the melancholy
spectacle of four elderly men publicly debasing them-
selves. That there is no vestige of attractiveness
about their gyrations the degraded quartet would
probably be themselves the first to admit. Nor is
there any real novelty in their turn. The humor,
such as it is, consists in the unnaturally clumsy exe-
cution of time-dishonored movements which a pretty
and graceful woman finds it hard, even after earnest
practice, to render tolerable to modern eyes. The
people who are diverted by the sight of Bantock
crashing down-stage, in brief skirts and a full-
bottomed wig, would be highly amused to see a stout
man falling heavily off a tram. And one had imag-
ined that the catastrophic variety of jest—the sort
that reposes broad-based on the public humiliation
of some inoffensive fellow-mortal—had perished
with the decay of the zest for practical-joking l
Presumably these demented burlesques of an art
which, during upwards of 30 centuries, has aimed
chieflv at grace and allurement, meet some deep-
seated public need. In that case the need should
be inquired into, as one would investigate an out-
break of measles or chicken-pox. A Liberal friend
of mine attributes it to the Labor party, the onrush
of Socialism, and indifferentism as regards the cele-
bration of Empire Day. I only hope and pray that
h:s diagnosis may prove faulty.

Miss Rosie Lloyd, comedienne, who com-
mands a slice of the Afternoon Teavoli pro-
gramme this week, is one of those ladies who
have a celebrated relative, and prop them-
selves up against her name. She is billed
as “sister of the famous Marie Lloyd,” though
the lady might be her grand-aunt for all the
difference it makes. As with most of “Eng-
land’s Most Celebrated Comediennes” and
there are hundreds of them—her repertoire
consists of a few songs (all or most about
kissing) and a set of teeth; and the teeth
are the biggest success. On the other hand
Will Poluski, jun., another English come-
dian, is the real thing, and about tops the
poll of recent comedians who have labored
under the “famous English” label. Welsh
and Louise Carbasse—who shine in a new
sketch, “A Terrible Fix”—and Marshall
Crossby, a baritone with a good voice, are
enjoying a second time on Mclntosh’s earth.
Prominent among the also-rans are the
Staley and Birkbeck musicians, the Lanca-
shire Opera Co.. Maxini Bros., the Pattv
Franks and My Fancy.
The earth tremors and upheavals in vari-
ous parts of the Crystal Palace have not
affected the picture show, and it flicks on
just the same. This week an eye-opener in
the form of a Panama Canal film is un-
wound ; although it takes well over the hour,
it is most interesting throughout. The huge
steam shovels, the grabs, the tremendous
concrete works and the locks land- Uncle
Sam’s new canal a length or two in front of
the seven wonders. At the Colonial, Hussein
the Pirate has been providing a passable
film; and the mirror show at the Palace
annexe is still proving a success.
The five Olracs have arrived at the
National Amphitheatre. They are expert
fallers. They fall over everything with the
completest impartiality. They are capable
of falling over any article, obstruction or
other circumstance, dead or alive, from a
worm to a cathedral. There has been no-
thing like their turn since Lord Brassey
ceased to represent his Sovereign in these
parts. The Delinos are different, inasmuch
as they do not fall off anything. They walk
on wire, perform tricks on bicycles, and so
on. Always the audience watches expectant
that one of them may break his neck, or at
least a leg. In vain. However, it is possi-
ble to ask too much of our hired entertain-
ers. . The Era Comedy Four are still being
received kindly, and the Prunes and Miss
Nellie Morrison merit a subdued shriek of
praise.
“The Taming of the Shrew” is Shak-
epeare after the own inner heart of human-
kind : boisterous broad comedy to suit both
sexes ; full of action; spiced with exaggera-
tion ; dressed in the raiment that (for all
its pompous superfluities and “class” con-
trasts) remains in sympathy with modern
fashions; and strong in touches of eternal
nature. Petruchio is the typical masterful
brute , whom women secretly adore, whilst
rejoicing that his methods are out of date.
Katherine stands for the hot-blooded she
whom every man would conquer if he could.
The self-assertion of Bianca and the Widow
as soon as they are married strikes the four
sections of the adult population in their
respective senses of humor. The hopeful
spinster giggles, the fatuous bachelor laughs,
the matron holds her tongue in her cheek,
and the old man grins reminiscently. The
arrangement of “The Shrew” which Asche
staged again at Melbourne Royal last Sat-
urday brings, out all, or nearly all. (hat is
best in the disorderly comedy, and dispenses
with the incidental lumber of it. Moreover,
his wonderful make-up and convincing air of
besotted bewilderment as Tinker Sly in the
induction restore Asche to notice in his best
character part. Old Christopher, the malo-
dorous son of Beer, is a revolting person, but
in his. embodiment of the inductory joke he
is sufficiently clean and wholesome.
A few changes in the cast since “The
Shrew was first produced at the Royal, 3 i
years ago, make very little disimprovement
in the result. Warlock is a tame, grave
Lucentio who doesn’t speak his lines as dis-
tinctly as Kay Souper did, yet he is an
admirably nice young man. The Bianca of
Miss Lilian Birtles, although rather weak,
is pleasing enough. And Arthur Trantom,
as Gru.mio, succeeds in talking and looking
v ei T like his more weighty predecessor,
Athol Forde. A moving shadow in the
mummer row, he echoes foggy sounds of
long ago. Oscar Asche is, of course, his own
distinctive self again in the 10-league boots

This is the seventh week of the panto at
Her Majesty’s, and still it prospers. The
brisk young she-boy, the stolid she-dame, the
cat, the stony-broke King and his mild
Princess daughter, continue to bear the heat
and burden of the entertainment what times
it is not otherwise engaged. Seeing that 350
people are alleged to take part in the pro-
duction, the share of heat and burden that
falls upon the principals can hardly be over-
taxing their powers of endurance.
“No Mother to Guide Her” will supply
the managerial want at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s for another week or two, so there is
plenty of time for its burglary interest to
be brought right up to date. Thusfar, the
police who burst upon the scene when rob-
bery and murder are suspected have for-
gotten to ’phone for the finger-print expert,
but this oversight, after all, doesn’t count
for much in comparison with their neglect
to arrest the red-frocked donah who changed
clothes with the heroine whilst she was
waiting to be hanged. The limit of police
inefficiency is reached in the reigning attrac-
tion at the Princess’s. The limit of several
other things is reached, for that matter, and
one might rake the astounding narrative
with a small-tooth comb without finding
aught that would look barely possible under
a microscope.

There is more of Mat Gabbett and less of
the Rev. John North in the new version of
“The Term of His Natural Life” which
transpired at Melbourne King’s on Satur-
day. The rev. gent, is an unusually fitful
apparition in the painful story, but Gabbett
is early introduced into the first act, and is
the moyipg spirit in a plot to capture the
ship—very well painted, by the way, by
Rege Robins and his assistants. Gabbett is
really almost as important as good Rufus
Dawes or bad Captain Maurice Frere, who
wallops the convict population beyond the
limit of forbearance. Charlie Taylor plays
the man-chewing Gabbett, and Charlie’s
habit of looking on his weaker brethren as
prospective ingredients in a stew or a
rissole gives him a very fair standing as an
animal impersonator. Captain Maurice
Frere, the private rotter and public calam-
ity, is acted by Herbert Lee, who supplies a
mere stencilling of other people’s work in
the part. Kenneth Brampton’s portrait of
the Rev. North is rather the frame without
the picture, and “good Mr. Dawes” is one
of Roy Redgrave’s classic bloques given to
Hamlet-like brooding and self-analysis. The
convincing Redgrave, however, does not for-
get the claims of bellowdrama, for he has a
manner of revealing his sufferings by grease-
painting his visage with bruises of black
and weals of red until his countenance looks
kc the plan of a Bulgar battlefield. John
xtex, tne had egg who slays his parent, is in
the hands of Walter Dalgleish; Cannam is
the same insistent libel on Mr. Meakin; and
iiiss Helen Fergus is a decorative Lady
As Sarah Purfoy and Sylvia Vick-
ei s, Alisses Florence Itichter and Vera liemee
are respectively passable, though both are
sweetly ineffective when the situation calls
tor an emotional demonstration. Though
the play dates back to the infant years
or last century, both ladies also gown
themselves in the latest fashions, and even
in scenes when no display is allowable they
still indulge in sartorial swank.
At the Opera House the last nights of De
Biere have lasted a fortnight, and on Satur-
day they will finish their course. Which re-
mark applies also to the farewells of Nella
Webb. _ The bounding Arabs continue to

zoug-zouging in pyramidal forms; Kitchen
and loy are still shedding their outer gar-

ber backbone with unmerited contempt.
Last week the P’ran Lyric (Vic.) was
still uncurling “The Count of Monte Cristo”
and giving a very good display of the popu-
lar story. Among other films in the run-
ning was “The New York Hat,” wherein
the presentation of v a chapeau to a lovely
maiden leads to. the local parson being
charged with giddy behavior. To The
Bulletin’s great relief, the pilot was found
not guilty of any wicked intention. “Mr.
Up’s Trip Tripped Up” was a comedy with
a plot as clear as Jokook’s policy; while
“Fraud at the Hope Mine” related a yarn
of robbery and malice with a nice girl to
provide the emotional trimmings. Also,
there was a picture of Mahaddin (Africa),
wherein the screen was sprinkled with be-
nighted Sambos disfigured by wrinkles, dirt
and obesity.
Last Saturday West’s Melbourne support-
ers were entertained by “The Girl Without
a Country,” a damsel who attempted to pinch
a nation’s .secret at the behest of a gentle-
manly foreign person. How the girl got
on in her appointed espionage is well nar-
rated in a film which, without showing
genius as a piece of fiction, is yet nearly a
masterpiece in emotional particulars. The
hugger-mugger throughout is cutely por-
trayed by a Danish actress, Asta Nielsen,
who uses the incendiary glance with deadly
effect. “A Noise Like a Fortune” was
merely the din of money talking at the top
of its voice; while “The Water Rats” re-
vealed a burglarious rodent with a strong
strain of piety and a good, kind ’eart. Strange
how all the burglars in motion plays have
the inconvenience called Conscience and a
tendency to wowse on the slightest provo-
cation !
Quantities of the population continue to
stroll into the enclosure at St. Kilda (Vic.)
where the Smart Setters are giving their
vocal displays at prices of an extremely
popular character. The programme was
changed as usual last Friday, a feature of
the new bill being the sketch, “When They
Go On the Stage,” by Walter George and
Miss Georgie Martin, who Spoke as if they
were talking by well-oiled machinery. Among
those who are graciously permitted to stray
into this par. are Oswald Preston, who
warbled “The Young Carbineer”; Miss
Emily Kroll, who sang “My Moon”; and
Edgar Hulland. who attacked Liszt’s Pol-
onaise with a placid contemnt for its techni-
cal difficulties. Edward Elliott, the ven-
triloauial comic, also hawked forth “Mr.
Sunshine James,” and made the object con-
verse with Cockney impertinence.

The Anderson season of melodrama at
Adelaide Royal unexpectedly ran into five
weeks, and was so successful in a quiet way
that Herbert Myers entertained tbe whole
crowd at the finish and said pleasant things
about them. The Royal is now taking a
holiday, and Mabel Hardinge has the dra-
matic field to herself with “East Lynne” at
the Tivoli. The King’s has the variety field
to itself. Paul Gorden, wonder of the wire,
has gone; but Miss Brooke is still causing
Woman to gasp at the monstrous fashions
she was content to wear day-before-yester-
day, and great chief Lone Eagle has come
in with his war-dance and wampum and
other assets. Weather slightly warmer has
been good for the open-air Dandies and
Wondergraph pictures, which show a re-
markably-faked Biblical deluge. West’s also
revel in Death, with the grim Retreat from
Moscow. The Empire trumps the other
houses’ death-pictures. It puts up “Resurrec-
tion.” But not Gabriel’s variety. Not even

AT POVERTY POINT.
Frank Musgrove has bought Sydney Criterion
Theatre freehold for, it is said, £60,000. This in-
cludes the hotel, shops and some frontage in Park-
street. The sale is subject to a lease, with three
years to run, to J. C. Williamson, Ltd. The Bank
of N. S. Wales was the vendor. The theatre was
built originally by the Hill estate, the family of
which Dan Cooper’s wife was a member. It occu-
pied the site of the old Glasgow Inn, at the rear of
which was a fine old house, the private residence in
years past of the Coopers and the Hills.
Melba’s alleged fee of £40,000 for a series of
concerts in ’Murka and Canada next autumn is likely
to be the last of her very big hauls. The great Aus-
tralian Voice had lost some of its original force,
compass and fluency, and even a little of its beauti-
ful color, before Melba left here for Europe a few
months ago. When it has gone further on the down
grade the English-speaking world will hardly con-
tinue to adore Melba as old Patti was adored long
after the glory of her warble had departed. For
that matter Patti’s final American engagement was a
heavy loss to the speculators, and the little woman
released them from their contract.
“Monkbarns”: “Taming the Shrew” is put on
again by Asche. Until Benson did better, for about
a century the stage had been held by Garrick’s
mangled version, the uproarious farce in two acts
called “Katherine and Petruchio.” (Maybe Phelps
staged the right thing.) Irving and Ellen Terry first
acted together as Petruchio and Katherine. Irving
got £7 a week, and Ellen £l5. Petruchio was the
last character acted by Brooke at the Melbourne
Olympic. But the Induction was only once given in
that city before Asche did it. This once was by the
Garrick Club, in 1864. For an after-piece the club gave
“The Merchant of Venice,” with Sam Banks, the
comp., as Shylock.
“Melb”: Asche rehearses “Midsummer Night’s
Dream.” Though popular now, it was not much
acted till almost in our own time. Pepys wrote of
it that it was the sorriest stuff he ever saw or heard.
Probably half was cut out, and the other half
gagged. The first Melbourne production traceable
was at the Princess’s: Bottom, Jefferson; Theseus,
Fitzgerald; Lysander. Heir; Demetrius, Ireland;
Puck, Julia Edouin; Egeus, Harwood ; Quince, Lam-
bert ; Flute. Stewart; Oberon, Emma Neville ; Titania,
Dolly Green; Hermia, Mrs. Heir; Helena, Ro3a
Dunn. It was not seen again until Rignold’s pro-
duction, in which George acted Bottom, with Hilda
Spong as Titania, and Miss Russell as Puck. Other
parts were taken by Keightley, Sweeney, Whyte,
Hazlitt and Rignold’s other well-known myrmidons.
The third and last production was by Musgrove and
Courtneidge: Bottom, Denny; Theseus, Bucklaw;
Oberon, Dora Rignold; Titania, Nora Kerin.
From London: “Captain Penfold,” described as
“an Australian aeronaut,” went up in a balloon in
London on Christmas Eve, to advertise Sandow’s
cocoa and chocolates. Dressed as Old Father Christ-
mas, he sat on the edge of the basket, and nearly
came to grief at the start, only missing a collision
with a gasometer by a few inches. He intended to
descend by parachute in Hyde Park, and distribute
chocolates free among the crowd. But the wind blew
him right out of London into the county of Essex.
At the alleged height of 3000 ft. he parted company
with the balloon and dropned 400 ft. before the para-
chute opened. When he landed—in a field —he was
unconscious for 10 minutes. After that he presented
his chocolates to a little crowd of agricultural
kiddies.
