“Jerong”: Bulletin (9/l/’l3) records the death
at Wanganui (M.L.) of W. H. Thornton, Sophie
Vaughan’s father. I first knew Thornton and his
wife on Lambing Flat (N.S.W.). She was a Red-
fern girl. This was in the early part of 1861. In
those days Thornton was known among the diggers
as “the "scenic artist.” There were no theatres on
the field at this time ; but he could get any amount
of sign-painting to do, for every little one-eyed store
wanted to buy, in big letters, 10,000 oz. of gold,
highest price given. About this time commenced the
roaring days on the Flat. Tipperary Gully broke
out, and the Chinese riots followed. And, thank
Heaven, we had no Miss-the-’Bus McGowen for a
leader, or the Chows would never have Received their
quietus! Tipperary and Chance Gully gave Lambing
Flat a £ooct start. Two theatres sprang.,up, the Em-
pire and Diggers, and one in Tipperary Gully. They
all boomed. Any amount of work offered in the
scenic artist’s line, and Thornton did gilt-edged
business. He was a very estimable man, respected
all round. There are but few of the old Lambing
Flat rioters left to regret him ; Time must soon call
the last of us to the final roll up.
“F.J.8.”: “Monkbarns” (B. 2/l/’l3) is altoge-
ther too positive. The identity of Faust is not his-
torically on ail fours with that of Balsamo or
Mesmer. The Socialist, Ernest Belfort Bax, has made
a very complete and exhaustive study of the ques-
tion (“Doctor Faustus and his Contemporaries”).
Bax examines all the evidence, and can find no genu-
inely historical Faust, the necromancer. He is in-
clined, therefore, to take him as a type, rather than
as an individual. He finds the material for his
making in two real persons, namely, Phillippus
Aureolus Theophrastus Bombast von Hohenheim or
Hoehener (“Paracelsus”) and Heinrich Cornelius
Agrippa von Nettesheim (“Agrippa”), whose doings
and claims were fused, and fastened on that Faust
who invented printing, and whom the monks hated
for discounting their value as copyists. “Monk-
barns,” by the way, makes a childish remark in say-
ing that Faust “managed to impress the public that
he was sold to Lucifer.” In that age of demonology
and witchcraft every man a little better educated
than his neighbors was, on the prima facie evidence
of superior knowledge, already a Devil-thrall. Luther
devoutly feared the Devil, and held daily arguments
with him, honoring him on one occasion by throwing
an inkpot at his head. The sneer at Cagliostro is
also beside the mark ; for, pretender though he was
in many things, he was undoubtedly a telepathist.
Franz Mesmer, who gave his name to mesmerism,
was no quack either, and it is stupid to associate his
name with that of Joseph Balsamo. By the way,
“Paracelsus,” from one of his names, gave Europe a
derivation for the terms “bombastic,” “bombastical”
and “bombast.”
“Hullo”: J.C.W., in an interview with S. M.
Heralsj, states, as usual, that he would only be too
glad to find a really good Australian play. And he
instances the really good Australian plays that the
firm has already produced: a melodrama adapted
from Wagner’s “Parsifal,” and two pantomimes! “1
had in mind two or three years ago to produce a
drama founded on Steele Rudd’s ‘On Our Selection,’
and I asked the author to try his hand at writing the
play, but. . . .it did not suit. I then suggested to an
able young friend of mine that he should collaborate
with Steele Rudd. . . .but he let a fine opportunity go
by.” It was the Firm that let that fine opportunity go
by—to be picked up by Bert Bailey. The Firm stands
accused of a lack of business acumen in not being able
to find on its staff, or off, the right Australian writer to
build from that excellent foundation the successful
and money-making play that “On Our Selection” now
is.
“Hullo”: J. C. Williamson has returned from his
quest of the paying play, and reports that “it is
difficult to secure plays.” And Sydney Telegraph
moans that the good play “has ceased, with a few
rare exceptions, to be produced in Great Britain.”
Apparently the only English dramatists that the
Telegraph has heard of are Pinero, Jones, Haddon
Chambers, Wilde and Shaw. There are, it 'may be
mentioned for the information of the Telegraph
and the Firm, others. Here are three plays that
have, from a box-office point of view, been strikingly
successful: “Hindle Wakes,” “Bunty Pulls the
Strings,” both by new authors, and Shaw’s “Fanny’s
First Play.” We might have these to begin with.
Then there is Bennett’s “What the Public Wants,”
which is exactly what the box-office wants. And if
the real thing is good enough for Australian audi-
ences (and judging from the success of “Mile-
stones,” it should succeed), there are Galsworthy’s
“Justice” and “Strife,” Elizabeth Baker’s “Chains,”
Barker’s “The Voysey Inheritance,” Synge’s “The
Playboy of the Western World” and —but these are
enough to go on with.
The Australian Kinematograpii Journal, a trade
paper which deals with the moving-picture business,
takes exception (30/l/’l3) to an extract from the
Sydney Daily Telegraph which The Bulletin
quoted recently. The gist of the quotation was (1)
that the head of Clement Mason, Ltd., had remarked
that a Picture Trust had been formed which would
mean to the organisers an additional income of
£250,000 a year ; (2) that the directors of a suburban
firm had said that, in that event, the price of films
would go up. The A. K. Journal appears to doubt
the truth of these statements. In any case it
denies that a monopoly is possible in the flicker
business. Listen to it: “How any arrangement can
be construed into a monopoly when anyone else
can undertake a precisely similar business is not
easily conceivable. There is nothing to prevent any-
one—provided he has a sufficiency of capital to stand
the racket —importing pictures and opening a film
exchange. This being the case, how can such an
arrangement, as has been entered into, be termed a
monopoly?” It is the argument of every apologist
for every trust on earth. There is, of course,
nothing to prevent anyone—provided he has enough
capital to stand the racket —from building theatres
and importing theatrical cos. with such vigor and
skill as to burst the existing theatrical combine.
The happening is, however, highly improbable. A
trust is not necessarily a concern which no one on
earth can possibly overthrow: it gets there just as
effectively, in most cases, if it is so situated that
the chances of successful opposition are reduced to
(sav) 1000 to 1.
“Star”: Von Biene, whose death the cable has just
reported, may not have taken his ’cello to bed with
him, but certainly it was never far from his downy
couch; and Auguste was liable to take it to his
breast at any hour of the night or morn, and rake
melancholy music out of it. At an hotel in one Vic.
provincial town three other residents burgled the
room, and pinched Von Biene’s ’cello from his bed-
side and hid it in the cellar, thinking to have at
least one night’s sleep. But Auguste discovered his
loss at two in the morning, and, getting out on the
stairs in a long nightshirt, howled like a dismal dog,
until the conspirators were glad to restore the fiddle
to its woolly and woeful owner. “Now,” said the
grateful Auguste, “I play ze kin’ shentlemen to
sleep.” And he played till daylight.

THE PURSUER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I witnessed oft the big man’s flight,
And his pursuer’s weary plight.
The manager, with lowered head,
Ducked into darkness, and had fled;
The thin shape stole from out a crack,
And followed fast upon his track.
And when the jovial fellow sat,
With nice dark suit, and high silk hat
And took his drink with jovial cheer,
The thin and shabby one drew near ;
And then the former’s visage threw
Off ruddy joy, and faded blue.
From room to room within, without,
From street to street they dodge about
The stout man shows a troubled soul;
The thin one bears a paper roll.
One runs a theatre, they say ;
The other bloke has made a play !

HUBBY : "Hardly."
Apropos sentences for “life.” During the
last 10 years six “lifers,” previously con-
demned to the gallows, have been discharged
from Victorian gaols. Only one underwent
less than 15 years ; he was the old Chinaman
who got his freedom after six years. Of
the others, one, a woman, convicted of wilful
murder, served 16 years. Three male mur-
derers served, respectively, 15 years and nine
months, 16 years and eight months, and 17
years. The sixth, a man, was convicted of
wounding with intent to murder, and served
18 years and seven months. There are now
in Victorian gaols 12 “lifers,” seven of whom
are being given the benefit of the “Carlisle
expectation of life” rule, under which the
Chinaman was released. According to pre-
sent designs, the others will remain in prison
for the term of their natural lives.
“Jersey” writes: —
lie the inability of Private Enterprise to supply
infants with more or less pure milk (B. 30/l/’l3),
I know something of the filthy habits of the average
farmer and his dairy assistants, and a truthful de-
scription of even the cleanest cocky’s methods of
milk production would cause a commotion in the
healthiest reader’s Stomach. 1 have seen obviously
diseased cows milked, and the fluid forwarded to
Sydney twice a day— and the owners of the beasts
knew that tubercular perms were being trucked.
Cancerous and tubercular cattle would soon be exter-
minated if the State would undertake to buy ttiem
all; but the cocky’s freed will not let him stand
the loss. So, unless an inspector happens on him
unawares, the poor emaciated beast is ' literally
milked to death. Every animal in a dairy herd
should be subjected to tubercular test. It is not
sufficient for an inspector to take a cursory glance
at a beast, as some cattle do not develop the usual
symptoms, even though they be rotten with disease.
Last week I saw a cow, scarcely able to stand in the
bails, be.ng milked, and its racking cough could
be heard half a mile down the paddock. A few
minutes later its milk was being put through the
separator, and later on was handed out to city eaters of
best factory butter. Ugh ! But the milking of diseased
cattle does not constitute the whole beastliness of the
dairy farmer. Watch the cows as they are driven
into the yard. Half of them are suffering from
sunburnt "teats. The filthy hands of the milker
grasp the sore and tender teats, and, with no at-
tempt at washing the udder with warm water, he
proceeds to squirt blood and milk into the bucket.
Private Enterprise then forwards the mixture to the
metropolis as pure country milk. Like thousands
of others who know, I don’t use milk, thanks. I
am waiting for the business to be run by some
authority whose main purpose in life will be to
supply pure milk, and not to make profits.
Some of the infants on whom Australia is
relying for its defence went out to La
Perouse (N.S.W.) last week for an encamp-
ment, and on the first Sunday a horde of
mammas, maiden aunts and elder sisters
descended upon the picnic and had hysterics
when they saw how poor Percy had to feed
himself, "if the food was coarse, the boots
were just terrible —great heavy boots, of
rough leather, with thick soles. Apparently
the female relation expected to find Percy
being fed on clotted cream, asparagus and
fresh oysters from Kurnell beach, with his
feet encased in patent leather shoon with
soles as thick as those of dancing pumps.
Probably the boys would not have known
they were “badly fed and shod” had the
female relation been kept at a distance. As
she was not, he now finds himself a dread-
fully misused youth who had to pay two-
pence per glass for soft drinks. These boys
were out to learn the hard business of war,
so as to be able when the time comes to
defend those maiden aunts ; and if the maiden
aunts imagine that the ice-cream cart will
meander up and down the firing line, and
that afternobn tea will be laid on, they are
making a ghastly mistake. The best thing
that can happen to Percy just now is to be
left alone with his instructor to be hardened
up and taught to take care of himself under
any and every circumstance whatever ;<and in
the long run that may be a lot better for the
maiden aunt, too. If Percy isn’t hardened
up, some very unpleasant things may yet
happen to the maiden aunt.
“Marloo” : The occasional fatal snake-
bite in Australia leads me to ask why the
much-abused Morrissey is so idiotically
ignored by persons in authority, such as
Boards of Health. During the first fort-
night of January four people were bitten at
Lismore (N.S.W.), and in every
case were cured with the Morrissey
antidote. It happened to be handy,
they applied it, and the job was
done. On the other hand, in the
same period, an eight-year-old child
died in Victoria from the poison of
a tiger-snake. If Morrissey had
happened to discover his antidote
while wearing a ton-hat and a flog-
ger coat, a whole Science Congress
would have advertised its virtue by this time.
The plain tale of a serious girl:—
The Bulletin of recent weeks has dealt with the
Red Plague. The churches have shirked the subject,
the State has shirked it, and parents do likewise —•
all shockingly ; the press, as a rule, the most shock-
ingly of all. As the subject doesn’t get discussed in
the ordinary course of events, the public will want a
lot of waking up to the dreadfulness of it. Com-
pulsory reporting of the disease is about the only
sane thing to be done; and when a license for
marriage is applied for, there should be an official
search of the registered list of those suffering from
the beastliness before the permit is issued. The
public, which won’t protect itself, won’t submit to
a medical examination as to fitness for marriage, it
says ; but it should be made to declare its unfitness.
I write strongly because of this unpleasant little
incident. A certain man was spoken of very freely
as my probable future husband. A nurse, a friend
of mine, hearing of this, wrote and asked me to
come and see her at the hosoital where she is em-
ployed. There, both she and the matron, ignoring
the precious professional etiquette which has al-
lowed more disease to spread than enough, told
me that the man in question was suffering from a
particularly nasty form of the disease. Later, I
taxed a relative of his, who had been urging the
marriage, with knowledge of this fact, and received
the Staggering reply that I ought to be ashamed of
myself for knowing anything at all about such a
thing at my age (I am 23). After I had protested
against this theory, I was told that no woman
should know anything about it at all until she was
married. I’m glad I wasn’t as ignorant as I should
have been.

THE PROSPECTOR.
"Rooking for your overdraft , darling?" “ No , merely trying to trace the lost lode."
Northcote (Vic.) has become possessed
of a devil, and, like certain Biblical persons
similarly afflicted, it doesn’t know what to
do with it. The evil spirit in question is a
weapon for teasing flock, and has been in-
stalled bv an enterprising hat-maker. The
trouble about it is that it makes a noise,
described by a councillor as a “sort of weird
misery, calculated to drive a man to drink” ;
and as it creates this disturbance all day,
and can be heard nearly two miles distant,
it is rapidly getting itself disliked. The local
potentates have been asked to muzzle the
nuisance ; but they realise that it is a neces-
sity to any progressive hat-maker, and that
local industry is hampered enough already ;
so they have finally decided to order the
owner to build a high wall round the offence
so that its uproar may ascend to Heaven.
While this work is being done the public
might ask itself what its own responsibility
is in such matters. Everywhere there is a
growing disinclination to go more than a few
hundred yards to toil; wherefore the
harassed factory man who starts a new busi-
ness squeezes himself into a back lane so
that he may be no worse off, as far as labor
is concerned, than the rival factory boss
whose people have squatted down around
their work.
Badger, of the Brisbane Tramways, who
fled to his English directors after the strike,
probably to be out of earshot of Justice
Higgins’s compliments, got back last week.
He is an American by birth and training,
and hunted up Yankee notions while he was
away. The idea that hit him hardest was
the so-called “stepless” car —a vehicle sup-
plied with a sort of folding ladder, which
doubles itself up when the juggernaut is in
motion, and lets itself down when the thing
stops. Badger is getting one of them to
try on the dog. The advantage of it is that,
since the steps are down only' when the
vehicle is quiet, they can be carried to
within a few inches of the roadway. The
general use of the machine would probably
send the hot-hosiery store into bankruptcy.
Badger also prodded the “pay as you enter”
car; but it isn’t applicable to any but a
one-section line, so Badger wasn’t inter-
ested. It would have to be a short line that
the Brisbane Tramway Co. couldn’t cut into
two sections.
Large squads of seamen from the two
Japanese armored cruisers are let loose at
every Australian port the vessels touch, and
the press notices are always the same —“A re-
markably well-behaved body of men,” or
words to that effect.
Those little Japs
In peaked caps
Bear long and wicked knives;
The which they might
Employ in fight
To cut short human lives.
They march ashore,
But shed no gore ;
Polite ar,e they and grave.
And truly we
Are glad to see
How nicely they behave.
The tale of Australia's “first church”;—
On February 3, 1788, it being the first Sunday
after Phillip's landing at Sydney Cove, Chaplain
j c p, ar d Johnson conducted divine service under a bia
gum-tree, which stood on the site of the present flaip
in Macquarie-street. This year the Anglican
& contingent of be ii- t o PP ered aldermen, a bat
talion of choristers and a number of radiantly-attired
“‘“X ” vSSSnLe* the S
u 8 anniversary with the more hilarious rum-christened
< est j va i which commemorates Phillip’s official land-
ing How old Johnson’s ghost must have chuckled
at the celebration! He was described in a Govern-
ment despatch as belonging to those persons who
“call themselves dissenters.” He was made chaplain
to the settlement because nobody else was available,
and he did his duty according to his lights, without
any recorded fear, and certainly with precious little
favor from anybody. The Pioneers (whose descend-
ants are conspicuous on such anniversaries) were not
noted for marked manifestations of piety, and when
they were not wrangling with the Governor, and
doing their best to upset the administrative apple-
cart, they were selling rum. Poor old Johnson was a
misfit in a convict distillery. He pinched and
grubbed and saved, and built the first church, some-
where near the corner of Bligh and Hunter streets,
Sydney, out of his own pocket. It was a sorry
affair; but, like Touchstone’s Audrey, his own—a
proprietary church, built of wattle-and-dab. It cost
the poor chaplain a full hundred pounds sterling, and
then some ruffians burnt it down to escape compul-
sory attendance on Sundays. Poor old ghost of
Richard Johnson, penny-starved chaplain, what
amazement he must have experienced in contemplat-
ing the importance given to his first service on Feb-
ruary 3, 1788, before a lot of rum-soddened con-
victs, slowly suffering a recovery, and a number of
military and civil officers of the settlement who never
suffered anything else!

THE BAG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dustyville, the new suburb, is going ahead by leaps
and bounds. Every morning the incoming trains set
down crowds of city-bound business men, armed with
morning papers and the customary brown kit bag.
—Press whoop.
With shoulders bowed and knees that sag
I stumble in and out of town,
The slave and porter of a Bag
That I, perspiring, haul and drag.
That demon made of leather brown
Is mostly with sad rubbish packed.
(I blush while I admit the fact.) '
The books I cannot hope to read
I keep in there and carry far;
Some papers I will never need,
A sample of canary seed,
A section of an iron bar.
Life will be hard if I should gain
A length of rusty anchor chain.
Before I went and bought the Brute
With which I stagger to and fro,
With paper bags of fragrant fruit —
Frail sacks of squashy Dago loot—
Quite light and free I used to go.
I loathed the cheap and crackling fuss,
So went and bought the Incubus.
And now I totter, as I’ve said.
The maddest sort of galley slave
Who toiled and wished that he was dead
At least was forced to sweat for bread,
His hide from whistling thongs to save.
No such excuse have I, who sag
And sigh and totter with the Bag.
I often wonder if I’m sane,
With such a heavy load to trudge;
But half the travellers in my train
With similar Curses grunt and strain;
Each is a voluntary drudge.
I recognise with bitter mirth
The burthen-carriers of the earth.
N.B.W.
E. O’F.

Adelaide University has been opening its
mouth wide of late, saying that it is starved
for both land and money. A helping of the
latter has been supplied by a Government
grant of £12.000 for new buildings—all
promptly expended—and by an Act increas-
ing its annual subsidy of 5 p.c. on endow-
ments by a steady £4OOO more. Now an
anonymous donor has thrown in £lO,OOO to
start a residential college—which Adelaide
has never had—and other additions. But
there are two conditions : Government must
give an equal sum and a lot of land at th, :
hack, on the slope to the river. The Desti-
tute Asylum is certainly going to be moved
into the suburbs ; and the Agricultural Soci-
ety, the Military headquarters, and probably
the police barracks, will follow. The spaces
left vacant are likely to be the subject of a
scramble by University, School of Mines,
Public Library and Museum, it being Ade-
laide’s boast to have all these educational
buildings along North Terrace, in a row. with
frontage, of course, restricted. The Verran
Government was anxious to send the Univer-
sity out into the suburbs, too, but the idea
was howled down as undemocratic, an
immense proportion of the lectures and
Masses being attended by young people who
pee in city offices.

“A.E.P.”: He came from the North of
England, and was having a turn round Syd-
ney before looking for a job at Hunter’s Hill
of some other remote country place. “Pretty
dull iot,” he confided to me, talking on the
Mamy boat about Sydney girls. “How’s
that?” said I. “Well, when I’d stowed my
things away I went for a walk. Down
George-street I met a girl, and she said:
‘Hullo, Jabk!’ I said ‘Hullo!’ and pulled
up. She kept on calling me Jack. I took
her and gave her some refreshments, and then
we went to the picture show. Then I saw
her home — and she still thinks Pm Jack!”

One of the uninformed critics of Northern
Australia is the Rev. David J. Garland, at
present in Maoriland. Years back he was
in charge of a parish which included the
whole north-west portion of the continent,
and was at the time probably the only
gospel-agent in that great stretch of terri-
tory. Till Garland (travelling by himself
except for the company of a black boy)
blew along, herds of aborigines had never
seen a white man. But the reverend person
pins this yarn :
I was then impressed with the immense nossibili-
ties and the great fertility of the country. Except
on the coast, a wonderfully healthy climate, dry heat
in the day time and much of the year quite cold at

“De Yid” : Of, S. Australia’s early big
estates the Da Gosta has had the best his-
tory. Bit by bit it has been cut up and
given away, frequently to the people who
had been tenants of the Trust; and when
two old ladies in England die, there will be
another division. This Da Costa was a
Hebrew who years and years ago poured
boiling oil on the Synagogue by giving a
considerable Dortion of Hindley-street to the
Anglican Church. Much
of the original estate thus
devised has been cut up,
but Jewish pawnbrokers
still pay a heavy rent to
the church for some of it.
The 20ft. frontage of the
Kalgoorlie Hotel, on the
corner of the once famous
or infamous Rosina-
street, still belongs to the
trusteees of St. Peter's
College. The rest of the
land occupied by the pub
is held on building lease
from an old brewery firm.
The land 'on which St.
Peter’s College stands
was left by Da Costa to
the two aforementioned
old ladies, but at their
death the college will
come into its own, so to
speak. Apropos Jew
gifts, one Solomon, father
of Vabien, famous for
being Premier in the
shortest-lived Ministry on
S. Aus. record, presented
a large block of land on
North Terrace to the
R.C.’s. This has a front-
age to three streets, and
a large convent and a
church are built thereon.
/One of Solomon’s three wives was an R.C. —
which accounts perhaps for his generosity.
At Mallacoota Inlet (Vic.) the other day
a person who had shuffled off from mortal
toil was. hurried ter the grave with musical
honors provided by a tourist’s phonograph.
“Abide With Me” and “Rock of Ages” were
the records used. If this sort of thing con-
tinues. death soon won’t be worth dying.
The problem of the indigent consumptive is
a dreadful one, but sometimes it is more
dreadful than usual. A Victorian mayor re-
cently made a public appeal on behalf of a
man “permanently incapacitated with tuber-
culosis.” The sufferer has a family of five
children —and the youngest is five months
old. Bad enough in all conscience, but still
a shade better than a case the writer recently
heard of. In a sleepy country town, a plucky
woman toils at a cottage washtub, rinsing
and wringing the clothes of healthy residents
and visitors. Close by, her husband lies
dying of the White Death. He has not got
long to live, but still hopes to see the expected
child. Even the careless, steady-nerved coun-
try people seem uneasy about it.
“Parisine”: It is not
often ttiat a Quixotic-
looking thing like the
Eiffel Tower proves to be
of enormous benefit. For
many years the Tower
was regarded as some-
thing of a practical joke,
of no use except that the
cheerful jesters said it
would be grand for recon-
noitring the Germans
next time they came to
Paris. Now Eiffel Tower
is the very nerve centre
of French “wireless.”
Under favorable circum-
stances, a message has
even been received there
from Canada. It places
the War Minister in un-
broken communication
with Belfort, Luneville,
Verdun, Besancon and
all fortresses on the
German and Italian fron-
tiers. Also, with the
naval stations of Toulon,
Brest. Cherbourg, etc.,
and the ships in the At-
-lan t i c and Mediter-
ranean, and even with
Algiers and the Sahara.
In case of war, there is
communication with the
mast above the London
Admiralty. However, it
i s doubtful whether i n
case of war “wireless”
will be of very much ser-
vice. Apart from the
fact that masts will cer-
tainly he shot away at the first opportun-
ity, there is the fact that the multiplicity of
wireless stations makes what is calked a
“jamb” very easy. When you want to pre-
vent a station talking to another station, all
you have to do is to send out your own radi-
ating currents and make a jangle of the
whole business.
The Rev. Edward Lyttleton. head-master
of Eton, has been urging compulsory mili-
tary training as an antidote to the idleness
and loafing of youths among both rich and
poor. Coming from the head of J. Bull’s
best-known public school, this must cause
acute pain to journals like the S. M.
Herald, which attempted to annihilate the
idea in Australia by remarking coldly that
it Was “un-English.”
“F.C.”: Observant travellers journeying
from Melbourne city to the Port may have
noticed, in a yard alongside the line, huge
heaps of old tins, and wondered what they
were for. They are a tribute to the protective
policy that encourages the German to use
his brains. Years ago the peculiar “rustle”
of silken garments used to be given to non-
silken dress materials by means of spun
glass ; then Hans discovered that chloride of
tin was equally satisfactory and less expen-
sive^—if he could get enough old tin. So the
agents of an Essen firm buy up all the old
tins they can get, at the rate of 6s. per ton,
provided the tin face isn’t rusted off the iron
sheet underneath. When enough have been
collected, they are tossed into hydraulic
presses, and shipped away as solid blocks to
the Fatherland, there to be smelted, treated
and afterwards returned to the Common-
wealth in some of the “creations” that adorn
the lawn on Cup day.
“Full Astern”: The most excited ship’s
crew that has sailed into Sydney harbor for
some time belonged to a foreign vessel which
arrived last week. Feeling its way carefully
through the heavy traffic of the congested
waterway, the ship anchored temporarily, off
Miller’s Point; but when it came to raising
the anchor again, the hook seemed to have in-
creased its weight a hundred fold. Slowly
and with a strain that nearly pulled the don-
key-engine out by the roots, the anchor ap-
peared over the bows, and then was seen
clinging to it what appeared to be the father
of all devil-fish. The crew promptly scat-
tered over the fo’c’sle head, some to escape,
others to find axes and circular saws and
dynamite to put up the fight of their lives
against the monster. Fortunately, the pilot
in charge took a hand in time to save the
under-sea cables linking the North Shore
with the city. After a strenuous time the ob-
stacle was cleared, and, curiously enough, no
damage seems to have been occasioned to the
tram system on the Shore or the telephone
and telegraph services. Probably the lifted
lines belonged to the telephone communica-
tion. which is so bad that if it were wrecked
in 171 places nobody would notice the
difference.
“Looking Backward” on the accusing
finger:—
lie finger prints, which are to figure in the Fitzrov
(Vic.) murder charge. It was Sir William Hershel
who first used the system in the detection of crime.
That was in the ’so’s or ’6o’s, when the Government
of India caught a number of Baboo forgers in its
toils. Galton perfected the system, and in his book
said that the chances of one print being identical
with another were 64,000,000 to one—a very hand
some starting price. But the use and classification
of finger marks goes much farther back. The present
system is based on a book written in 1823 by one
Purkenje, who was, I think, a Pole. His was
rather a scientific thesis than a system for identify-
ing cracksmen. The oldest known finger print in the
world is in the British Museum. It was found in
Suffolk, in a flint pit. on a pick made of the horn
of an elk. It was left by a palaeolithic person, and

Extract from a letter:-“Dearest Mother , —
I have been doing a good deal of dancing
here. I love it—it is such good exercise."
the only thing established by it was that his finger-
nails were thicker than the modern manicurist al-
lows. Its age is estimated at 20,000,000 years or
so, Moses and other early historians notwithstanding.
It was probably later than this that the ancient
Egyptians—long before the Ptolemies and Cleopatras
- —made use of the individual character in finger
prints. In some of the mummy wrappings in the
British Museum napyri have been found with the
impression of finger tips on them. It apparently was
used as a signature, or an addition to a signature,
and was intended to block any Jim the Penman of
the day. The thumb was seemingly not used in
those times, but any of the fingers except the little
one.
Burns, Philp and Co.’s recently-issued
guide book, “Picturesque Travel,” offers
handy information of the interesting spots
of the world, and how to get there. The
Australian about to make a voyage, whether
for business or pleasure, will find the job
made easier at the start by perusing its
lucid pages.
SAW YOU LAST NIGHT!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Oh, I saw you last night!”
“Eh! I didn’t see y0u!”....
above ! What a fright!
Says she saw me last night!
Just suppose my wife ! Phew !
Hope there wasn’t much light!
“Dear —where were we last night ?”
N.S.W.
DECREE NISI.
THEIR MAJESTIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In placing the old stamp on the envelope one tried
to be careful, out of respect to his Majesty, to put
the stamp on properly. Now it is a matter of no
moment how the stamp is put on. In fact, it looks
best broadside on.— Argus correspondent.
Whilst George's head adorned our stamp
(The head that wears the Crown)
We did not dare when it was damp
(By “it” is meant the postage stamp)
To stick it upside down.
With loyal care and courtly grace
We gummed it to the proper place
Where stamps should tightly cling ;
And then, for just a little space,
We gazed on George’s regal face,
And sang “Gorsave the King.”
But now we have a kangaroo
Instead of George’s conk ;
And little caring what we do
(It is an awful kangaroo)
We stick the stamp on cronk.
Turned upside down, or anyhow,
The animal, you must allow,
Is very like a whale.
There should be a proposal now
To change the ’roo into a cow,
Arid introduce a pail.
Ah ! if the blessed cow were there
Beside a milking shed,
Would any Tory farmer dare.
If his Anointed Cow were there,
To stand it on its head?
The stamp would then respected be ;
To lick its back on bended knee
He’d reverently bow ;
And having fixed it carefully
He’d sing “Gorsave Her Majesty”—
Her Majesty the Cow !
VICTORIA.

BOOTH MEMORIAL.
LOAN OF A MOTOR-CAR.
(To the Editor of the “Argus.")
Sir, —In connection with the Wil-
liam Booth memorial fund, we are
anxious to call upori a number of
country people of Victoria, and I ap-
peal to some sympathetic friend, who,
appreciating the life and labors of the
late General Booth, will be prepared
to lend a motor to take me through
the country for six days. Blankets
and food Will be supplied for chauf-
feur and self. We would of course
take the greatest care of the motor,
and the trip might net £5OO to the
fund.—-I am, etc.,
ARTHUR S. ARNOTT.
Salvation Army Headquarters, Sec. to
the memorial fund.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Bill O’C.”: Always interesting is the
art of signalling, from that of the lighthouse keeper
to the methods of the mute, inglorious cobber “keep-
ing nit” for the coming of. John Dunn. A guard on
an Adelaide suburban line is developing a neat code
of his own. There are several small stations along
the line, at which the trains only stop on the requisi-
tion of passengers. The guard doesn’t bellow him-
self- hoarse to make his instructions to the distant
engine-driver, but manages it by silent work from
the other end of the platform. A golf links is
situated half-way down the line. If the train is
to stop there, he catches the driver’s eye and plays
a correct “approach” or “bunker” or something. On
another branch is the Port Racecourse. If that is
wanted, he rides an unseen “also-ran.” Near the
golf course is a siding sacred to a dairy farm. When
a stay there is demanded, he pulls a pint or so from
a supposititious Daisy. And if the train is to run
right through to the beach, he swims through a
breaker which isn’t there.
Coola ’: Sydney surfers have not so much
to complain of in the way of “bluebottles” on the
beaches as those who dip in N.S.W. North Coast
waters. One beach in particular, near Fingal light-
house, seems to be the congregating ground for the
beasts. Went in for a dip there one morning with a
mate, and we were stung worse than a pair of flats
at a church bazaar. We rubbed with wet sand to
get relief. Saw a couple of Binghis on the same
beach use the leaves of a small shrub. Don’t know
the name of the shrub; in truth, I know only that
the leaves were oval-shaped. Perhaps somebody else
knows the remedy. If so, the time is ripe for the
information.

Dear Bulletin, — The contribution of
“Niuafo’ou,” from Tonga (B. 26/12/’l2), wafted me
back in fancy to the palm-shaded, surf belted
dream islands of the South Pacific. It came some-
what as a shock that an imaginative female should
be branded as f. Baroness Munchausen for a few
slight addition and padding of facts solemnly told
her by one who, unfortunately, is now beyond refut-
ing or confirming his information. He is taking his
last, long rest in the Friendly Isles he so dearly loved
and so long labored for. The writer did not say the
lava statue of the woman, babe and pig was a work
of art. Possibly it would need a little sketching in
to make it recognisable. With regard to the malau
bird, I have a box of the eggs. Perhaps they are
rather large specimens; they certainly outrival the
product of the turkey, and that is somewhat of a
feat for a bird “nearly as big as a pigeon.” The
man from Niuafo’ou surely does not think that the
writer was so infantinely guileless as to believe that
the hen sat down and laid 100 eggs at a sitting.
It seemed hardly necessary to state that she laid one
a day for 100 days. The ordinary domestic fowl
often does that much, without being considered a
feathered prodigy.— One of the Glad Gihls on
the Mailboats;
A FAMILIAR TASTE.
In his report on the Trentham Cemetery, Mr. Evan stated that it required special irrigation. It
appeared to be an alluvial ledge, as the inhabitants sank into this drift for water, which was used for
domestic purposes. —News item.
THE WIDOW : “Every time I drink this water it reminds me of your pore dear father.”
“R.J.8.”: Re “K. O. Gill’s” curse on the
barb-wire fence. As far as cheapness is concerned,
the plain costs slightly less than the barb; but no
sane cocky considers that four plain wires make as
efficient a fence as four barbs. In fact, six plain
wires won’t keep stock out of a crop paddock unless
they have a barb on top. As for the alleged whole-
sale mutilation of stock —rabbits! Barb wire is not
dangerous if the wires are kept tightly strained. For
any mutilation there is, blame the fiend who lets
loose stuff lie around, and never strains his fences.
He’s the cove I’ve got it in for.
“Vagabond”: Can anyone recommend me
a cure for the cockroach curse on board ship ? I’m
getting crowded out of my bunk at night, and we all
have to stand guard over our tucker at grub time or
they’d snatch it off the plate. We’re edging along
into colder latitudes, and the only crumb of consola-
tion I’ve got is that some of the beasts may pre-
sently be frostbitten. We’ve tried all sorts of cures,
but none of ’em are worth mentioning. We have
various insect powders, which are blown through a
bellows into various cracks, but that’s no good. A
boot is the best remedy so far, but one can’t spend
all the watch below banging round with a boot. It’s
fatiguing, and the proportion of misses to hits is de-
pressing. Besides, “what is one among so many?” as
Shakspeare or Will Lawson says. So, if any Abo-
insecter can produce a cure, he’ll bring down the
blessings of a whole ship’s company on his head.
* * * *
“N. or M.”: “Leo Lear’s” starling (B.
9/1/’l3) is more swaggering than the one I am ac-
customed to down south, in the Apple Island. In
our district the starlings don’t think of interfering
with parrots and such. They wouldn’t dare. More-
over, the imported pest has, locally, dwindled of late
years—a thing to wonder at, as the countryside is
thick-set with harboring hedges. One of the theories
advanced is that the sparrows have fallen upon their
fellow-nuisances at nesting time, and turned them
and their bits of sticks out into the cold world; but
I won’t swear to this. I know, however, that the
starling is a variable beast. On Bruny Island, for in-
stance, the flocks of him that may be seen pervading
the atmosphere live on pickings from the beaches and
other unconsidered trifles, and are strictly honorable
as far as fruit is concerned. Incidentally, I note that
the Tasman minah —a little grey quaker of a bird
that lives on a staple of insects with a dessert of soft
fruit—has been acquiring unseemly habits of late. If
a skinned sheep is left hanging up for a few hours in
the open, the carcase will be found to be pecked all
over. The minah is a soft-billed bird, too.
“Bob Em”: The Marathon efforts men-
tioned by “Mimosa” and “Pick” (B. 9/l/’l3) are
nothing to perform about. Charlie Bates rode a
Come-by-Chance station horse (right off the grass)
from Come-by-Chance to within four miles of Wal-
gett and back, a distance of 65 miles, in five hours.
Also, one Arthur Colless, of the same section of
N. S. Wales, rode from , Brewarrina to Come-by-
Chance, with but one spell of an hour at Walgett,
in 15 hours —which works out at 140 miles in 14
hours. He used the one horse throughout, and his
riding weight was 14st.

“Mynza Bere”: Re the uarramundi of the
Wide Bay (Q.) streams. Was recently holidaying on
the upper reaches of the Mary River, where the
“barra” were like shoals of mullet. They have the
pleasant habit of every now and then rising to the
surface, and remaining there for about 20 seconds,
and I used to stroll along the bank and bang into
them with a gun. I saw, on several occasions, as
many as seven or eight, each a good sft. long, lying
so close that a shrimp couldn’t have crawled between
them. By way of a change they would swim along
in single file, each about a foot apart. One day we
cast covetous eyes on a big cove, lying in sft. of
water straight under our noses; but he refused to
budge till half a dozen fair-sized rocks had been
dropped on him. Then he came slowly to the top
to see what all the noise was about. He was im-
mediately honored with a royal salute of two guns,
and captured. On one memorable day I saw at least
40 or 50 of the big fellows in a space of 100 yards.
A man with a harpoon would have had more sport
than the tarpon fishers of Florida get. I can’t ex-
plain why they were so numerous and quiet, but the
drv weather at that time may have had something to
do with it.
• « • *
“Mallarapoonie”: The giant yam of the
Northern Territory takes some beating when it comes
to depth of root. The vegetable is not unlike an
attenuated horse radish, and its leaf is somewhat
similar to that of an arum lily. It frequents sandy
flats and the margins of swamps, is a hardy dweller
in arid regions, and a staple commodity of food with
the wandering bands of blacks. In flavor and texture
it would pass for a very rank and fibrous parsnip.
Have followed it down with a pick and shovel for
6ft., and then given up in disgust. A feature of the
beast is its covering of thin slimy skin, like an
onion’s. The Binghis exercise amazing patience in
digging it out. With pointed sticks about 4ft. long
they grub down to surprising depths in pursuit of
it. Some of the big yam flats in the Territory have
supplied vegetables to a tribe for generations; and
the native yam-workings, with their mounds of earth
thrown up in all directions, resemble an old alluvial
gold diggings.

“Eddyson” : Barnby was seated in an old
tarred punt, with a prop in his hands. On the sub-
merged end of the prop he had tied three ordinary
meat hooks, and with these he was pottering about
at the bottom of the river. Bamby commenced
operations at daybreak, and it was now after eight.
His punt was almost waterlogged, and a scathing
sun was slanting blinding rays from the face of the
stream. But Barnby fished on. He was a man about
48, thin and brown, all hair and wrinkles. At half-
past eight Jonas came slouching down the east bank
in unlaced bluchers. Discovering Barnby he forgot
where he was going, or decided that whatever he was
going to do could be done just as well to-morrow, or
next week. After 20 minutes Jonas said: “Fishin’ ?”
Barnby grunted. Jonas remained standing,for an-
other quarter of an hour and then sat down. Barnby
continued his angling operations. At about 10 Tan-
ner came up from the bend, driving a black cow.
He forgot the cow on discovering Barnby, and stood
on the river’s brim, watching, open-mouthed, till 18
minutes past 10. Then he said to Barnby: “Fishin’?”
Barnby grunted. Ten minutes later Tanner said to
Jonas: “Fishin’ ?” Jonas waited fully a minute, and
then answered, “Fishin’.” Tanner sat on the bank
near Jonas. Barnby went on fishing. At a little
after 11 Tanner said: “Well, I mus’ be goin’.” At
half-past 11 Jonas said: “S’pose I’ll haveter be goin’.”
At 12 Tanner went. At half-past 12 Jonas said:
“I’ll be goin’ now.” Twenty-five minutes later Jonas
went. Barnby continued to probe the bottom of the
river with his hooked stick, but it was close upon
three in the afternoon before he recovered the body
of his wife, who had drowned herself at 11 o’clock
on the previous night.
THE SKITE OF LONG AGO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am an easy-going bloke, and have no axe to grind ;
I’m satisfied with -tucker, doss and beer ;
It takes a lot to get me riled ; so tolerant my mind,
With other blokes I never interfere.
But there’s a time when even saints get up to say a
piece,
When things above the limit overflow ;
And I arise and do affirm that we should cause to
cease
The irritating skites of long ago.
You meet them in the whare and you meet them in
the camp;
They sour the trip by coach and ship and train ;
They blow in on the bush track, and they give a man
the cramp
With that persistent cow of a refrain
That follows every mention of the things you saw, or
did,
Or dreamed, or thought, or absolutely know ;
“Aw, yairs; it’s not so rotten, but”—and then they
lift the lid
And empty out the skite of long ago.
Their liquor ran like water, yet they seldom turned a
hair
(Their cheques, of course, were cheques in those
glad days) ;
But when they did have jamborees (they had, at .least,
their share),
The trimmings, now, would fill us with amaze;
And all these blokes had rung the sheds, were saddle
kings at home,
The girls they loved were not too (blanky) slow ;
They beat the pug from Sydney-side where’er he
chanced to roam—
According to the skite of long ago.
They used to coach like blazes round the rim of
Devil’s Hill
(The leaders throwing handsprings as they flew) ;
But Hell Fire Jack, the driver, he was always cool
and still,
Excepting that he sometimes bent and drew
The team sharp round a corner with the sparking
brakes a-squeal
(A thousand feet the usual drop below),
With the (blanky) coach cavorting on a- single
(blanky) wheel —
They say so when they skite of long ago.
They kept a dark horse sometimes for the benefit of
mugs,
And entered it for races at the town
(Where the lambs are mostly tethered for coves to
bite their lugs
When they’re tired of One Tree Gully, and come
down).
And, of course, the (blanky) neddv always won the
(blanky) Cup,
And the One Tree mob collected all the dough ;
It was, you see, a monty with the One Tree jockey
up—
At least, it was by skite of long ago.
The way they used to bushwhack is an art long since
forgot
(Likewise the few remaining trees are small).
The bushies worked in couples and they made the
pace so hot
It’s lucky any timber’s left at all!
While one bloke whirled the (blanky) axe the other
soaked the butt
(To stop the tree from burning down, you know) ;
My oath, there used to be some blokes who did
know how to cut—
That is, they did in skite of long ago.
And it’s the same in everything from morning until
night—
There’s always some old geezer waiting by
To horn into the argument and loosen up his skite,
And jaw serenely on from lie to lie.
When all the sons of man arise to meet the Judgment
Day,
I hope that geezer's battened down Below,
Or else he’ll interrupt the show to have his (blanky)
.say,
And §fcite about the (blanky) long ago!
N.S.W.

In 1878, at the Bombay Gas Co.’s annual meeting,
a shareholder, just returned from an exhibition in
Europe of the then new electric light, condemned it
saying “There could be no storage for electricity and
....the lights went out.” Another shareholder
readily admitted “that at some distant day electric
lighting might become general; but it might be
that at some remote period men might f1y....A1l
these things might be possible but they were a long
way off.” Phillips Ormonde & Co., 17 Queen St.,

SOUTH A US. TRAVELLER’S OPINION.
Change of water and food often cause colic and
diarrhoea, and when Mr. F. Bannister, traveller for
Menz and Co., Adelaide (S.A.), is attacked with
either of these complaints he takes Chamberlain’s
Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy. In a recent letter he
states: “Having suffered severely from colic, I have
much pleasure in informing you that Chamberlain’s
Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy has on several occasions
given me instant relief, and I always carry it with
me.”*

“A.L.8.”: “J.G.” (B. 16/1/T3) says that
Binghi isn’t in it with white men at robbing bees’
nests. But this is so only when Binghi tackles the job
in the white man’s way. It is questionable whether
one of us could work the trick as well as he when he
goes about in his own old fashion. A glance at
some of the trees he has burgled leaves the impression
that he possesses grit. It isn’t every person can
climb 80ft. per medium of toe-holds 3ft. apart, then
hang on with one hand, hew a fist-hole through into
the nest and calmly feast.

“Nym” : Re “Ginger’s” par(B. 19/12/T2).
All coastal tribes of the N.T. practise circumcision,
and so probably do most inland tribes. Many of the
tribes also practise sub-incision, keeping a few of
the best type of blackfellow to breed from, but gel-
ding all inferior males. Some N.T. blacks have a
practice that might be followed with advantage by
white folk in the bush. Waste matter of every
description—ordure, rags, bones, etc. —is carefully
collected, and either burned or buried. They believe
that the debbil-debbil will inhabit all rubbish, and
plague the original owners thereof. This custom is
also a relic of Israel, and lends color to the supposi-
tion that Binghi belongs to one of the lost tribes. A
peculiar fact governing circumcision may be noted.
In very rare cases are orphans circumcised, though no
reason is ever advanced for the omission. It seems
necessary for the father to be present at the cere-
mony, and 1 have known of several cases being post-
poned till that parent could be present. After the
ceremony, which is always performed at sunset,
and to the accompaniment of a peculiar corroboree,
the boy is held sacred for 12 mouths, during which
he must abstain from certain foods, and the wearing
of a naga (loincloth) is compulsory. After that
period is over he is entitled to a wife, though some
“blackfellers” remain bachelors all their lives, on the
ground that “ole feller lubra no more good; young
feller lubra too much silly-feller.” Another “Jewish”
rule of conduct is that when a blackfellow dies, leav-
ing a widow and young children, his brother or near-
est male relation marries her, and adds her to his
family. Marriage with a deceased husband’s brother
is not only necessary but often compulsory in the
N.T. This particular marriage is always settled by
the kings or elders of the tribes, though, within cer-
tain lines, the blacks are permitted to pick and
choose their wives freely.

“Nym”: A few years back, a par appeared
in The Bulletin alleging that blacks train dogs by
giving them a few hairs from the armpit. I didn’t
believe it. Recently, however, I handed a
particularly- vicious brute to a nigger in my
employ. The nigger tied the dog up to my
house and fed him, mixing a few hairs from his
armpit with the food. At the first meal the dog
nearly had a fit in endeavoring to bite the nigger.
It had calmed down considerably at the next meal,
and after the third the nigger untied the brute, which
followed him meekly. Narcotic?
“Chloe” : The aboriginal widow of the Nor’-
west makes a great demonstration at the graveside of
a dead husband, cutting and gashing herself about
the arms and breast. The wounds are only skin-
deep, however, but the
blood flows freely, while
the lady throws herself on
the ground, and refuses to
be comforted until the
chief stops up her mouth
with clay. This is called
making her “tadje,” and
the other gins thereupon
bind her hair with clay.
The rolls of chtyed-up hair
stand out like pipe stems
from the head. While
tadje, the widow may not
eat any flesh, even though
she may be immediately
given to a fresh husband ;
nor must she remove the
clay wads from her hair
till a certain time has
passed. Then the unfor-
tunate creature has to lie
in a pool till the sun-
baked ornaments are dis-
solved. Sometimes even a
week’s soaking makes
them as pliable as bricks,
and she is forced to saw
off the hair with bits of
glass. When her hair is
free, the tadje is removed ;
the merry widow is seized
upon by some of the
women, who hold her
down, while the chief rubs
her mouth with fat or
flesh. The victim screams
and protests that she is
unwilling to give up her
mourning, but, of course,
nobody believes her, es-
pecially when they see her chorus-girl appetite at
the feast which has been prepared by the s'sterhood.

“Mallarapoonie”: “Vigilans” (B. 2/1/T3)
is mostly right when he says. the Australian estuar-
ine crocodile (Crocodilvs porosus) is invariably an
arrant coward on land, but that is in the light of
day. The list of depredations committed on the
north coast of Australia by C.P. under cover of
darkness provides a long and dreadful roll of blood
and mutilation of man and beast. Eight years or
so ago, in Wyndham (W.A.), the record for that
settlement was three whites taken at different times,
the last a police trooper. The night being hot he
had probably laid down in the cool of the river
bank. Then there was the more daring ease of the
Malay who was taken out of a boat on the Victoria
River. Probably in this case, too, the man was
asleep. Next morning the boat was on the old spot,
in mid-channel, half-full of water, and the Malay
was gone. The manner of his going was never ques-
tioned. In almost exactly the same locality, un-
aware of this happening, my mate and I passed a
night in an open boat with crocs, swirling and
grunting all round us. We had two half-Myall
blacks aboard, and apparently they considered the
characters of the saurians particularly untrust-
worthy. I judged that from the fact that they took
turn about till daylight waiting with an axe for the
first croc, that offered the paw of welcome.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — May I, from the safe
distance of Somerset (Eng.), be permitted to exchange
stoush with you on the recent par. (B. 5/9/’l2)
anent the vegetarian ? I wear neither hair (to my
sorrow) nor spectacles, yet for a long time now have
foresworn the ghoul-like habit of corpse-tearing. Nor
can I say that various revelations from time to time,
on the methods adopted by the gentle butcher, offer
me much inducement to return to the fleshpot. I
contend: —
(1) If man was meant to be carnivorous he would
have teeth resembling those of other carnivorous
animals, which he has not.
(2) That all nutriment is primarily derived from
the vegetable kingdom, and in taking flesh we only
get what the animal we eat has not used.
(3) That at the moment of death the animal has
many poisons in his carcase (in process of being
eliminated) which its devourer gets into his own
system, willy nilly.
(4) That the work of the world is done by non-
carnivorous animals —horse, donkey, camel, ox, etc.
(5) That flesh at the best is only a stimulant (bet-
ter than whisky, worse than beer) and is useless for
giving what we all most require—endurance. Wit-
ness the placid vegetarian horse trotting along in
front of a heavy cart, while behind him hoofs the
carnivorous dog, carrying nothing but its own fleas,
yet with protruding tongue, glassy eyes, and general
all-in appearance.
(6) That flesh-eating, more than anything else, is
responsible for cancer.
Finally, if anyone can tell me a more effectual
method of getting the better of the Yankee or any
other Beef Trust, then the drinks are on me.—Yours,
Aldridge Evelyn.
“8.J.”: “Native Born” (B. 16/1/T3)
asks for an abo. name for a home. There are
Toora, a house; Carinya, a happy, peaceful home;
Wahroonga, our home; Yaralla or Uralla, camp or
home; Elimatta, my home; Lumeah, here I rest;
Worm, hut; Langi, nest or home; Dandaraga, a
good place; Naygoondy, this is my camp. If the
humpy is in a valley a really euphonious name
would be Bukkuraepanyul, between the hills.

“Snell”: “The greatest set-back I ever
had happened this trip,” said Azgen, the surveyor.
“Our camp was pitched by a creek at a place called
Pea Flats, and on the first Sunday morning I saun-
tered over to our nearest neighbor, whose name I
believed was Glass. Glass lived in a two-roomed
skillion stuck on in front of a chock-and-log hut. I
was rounding the corner of the hut when a sturdy
boy darted away from the front, followed hot-foot
by a long, lean, iron-framed female who reached an
arm like a spoon dredge, hooked the runaway,
tucked him over a knee and under an arm, and be-
gan to give him what-for with a large wooden but-
ter-pat. I forebore to interfere while the punish-
ment seemed within reason, but when it was pro-
longed to monotony I said: ‘Come, come, be reason-
able, madam. The boy has’ —I said no more, for
right there a red face covered with a scrub of brown
whisker pulled from the retaining arm, and a
coarse, grown-up voice said: ‘Look here, whoever-
you-are, if I don’t complain, what’s it gotter do
with you? Go on, Annie, if yer wanter.’ But Annie
did not go on. She set Glass, sen., on his feet and
retired to the house, grumbling about ‘interferences.’
I never made good with the Glasses after that.”
“G.T.” : The course of true love runs no
smoother in Papua than in more civilised spots.
Y’ama, of the Public Works Department, was build-
ing a beautifully exact wooden trellis for my veran-
dah. Little and old and ugly was Y’ama. His
short blue rami was innocent of conceit, and his dun-
colored top-gear unconscious of coquetry. Seem-
ingly, his whole soul was put into the sticks he was
working on. Ivikennys, passing along the road
below, gave an extra swing to their swirling grass
skirts, and called to him cheekily, but were un-
heeded. A man drifted into tea one day and shot a
glance of recognition at the old carpenter. “Well?”
I queried. And this was the yarn he told me. The
lady, an old sweetheart of Y’ama’s, had married an-
other man. Tiring of him, she sent word to Y’ama,
who lived three days’ walk to the west, to come and
kill him. He, taking with him a friend to assist,
obeyed. This took him to gaol for five years. He
was out scarcely one moon when another message
came requesting him to kill the man she had married
in the meanwhile. Again Y’ama obeyed. That time
both he and the lady became “belong Gubiment,”
Y’ama for seven years, the lady for life. His years
finished, he “signed on” to work in Port Moresby,
where the prison is. Every evening after his swim
he wraps him in a gay red rami and decks his hair
with frangipani flowers; then sits on the hillside
overhanging the gaol, and sings to his ladylove. (His
song is something like a dog’s howl, which makes
no matter.) His ladylove sits within and smiles.
* * * *
“Te Babarantiko” writes about an old
sea game:—
For a joy-time hand me a harpoon and let me
stand on the martingale back stays of a fair-sized
windjammer, waiting a chance to let fly at one of the
50 or so “sea pigs” running about the forefoot and
ahead of the rushing ship. The thrill that runs down
one’s veins as the iron thuds home and the porpoise
foams out of the deeps of blue and goes swinging up
under the cat-head to the heave-ho of the watch on
deck! Porpoise flesh, though very dark and rich
in blood, is very good eating indeed, and when a
herd of sea pigs enter an Island lagoon great is the
anticipation and excitement of the natives. As a
rule, the porkers are easily approached in a small
boat, and I’ve more than once taken a canoe into the
middle of a herd. In a “drive” in the fairly shallow
water on the coast reef off Otua village, a native
dived off my canoe, clasped his arms around a por-
poise and swam him to the beach, where the fish was
killed. Formerly, porpoise teeth, with holes driven
through the roots and strung on fine coir twine, were
used in several groups in the Pacific as currency and
as ornaments for the body. In the Fiji and Solomon
Groups such strings commanded high prices, accord-
ing to the size and quality of the teeth. Now ordin-
ary silver coins are in use, and Brum, jewellery has
superseded most of the old native ornaments. Por-
poise molars have practically ceased to be of any
value in the Fiji Group, and will soon lose their
value in the Solomons. Vella Lavella, in that group,
is now the only considerable user of the ivories. But
even there the demand is waning, though the taste for
the meat remains.
THE BALLAD OF A TRUST BETRAYED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The fat and fussy diner sat him down in doubt to
feed
In a hash-house that he’d never tried before;
The weary, worried waiter ambled up to know his
need,
And, smirking, slid the menu to the fore.
The old gent conned the items with consideration
keen,
Then moved his lips and marked his opening
course: —
“I think I’ll try a little lamb —I’d like it very lean —
It must be tender, too —with fresh mint sauce.
“And peas—ah, yes—some peas; some good, green
tender peas—-
e You’ll see they’re young and tender, will you not?
And don’t forget the lamb must be the knuckle cut —
and please
Be sure you see the gravy’s stemming hot!”;
m
The waiter murmured “Yessir,” in the old accus-
tomed way, 0
And slithered off as waiters e’er have d6ne;
He reached the kitchen counter, where he banged an
empty tray,
And bellowed briefly: “Bleat an’ bullets one!”

PERSONAL
ITEMS
“High-flier” : McDonald, the aviator whose
death is reported in the cables (he died the
way aviators mostly do), was here with
Hammond, of the Bristol Co., a couple of
years back. He it was who flew that gallant
old warrior, General Maria Gordon, over the
city and harbor of Sydney. His untimely
passing recalls an incident illustrative of
England’s innate distaste for things that are
not imported. He was booked to fly at
Hendon on a monoplane—an
English machine. The Vicars-Maxim people
make a superb asro engine—the AA r olseley.
The continent of Europe can show nothing
better, yet the employers of McDonald per-
versely insisted on installing a French engine
for the occasion. In the end, the contrap-
tion never left the ground, the foreign engine
being defective in about 14 different direc-
tions. Had the ’plane flown under the cir-
cumstances, its pilot would probably not have
lived to die in 1913.
PUBLIC WORKS
MINISTER
JOHNSON (W.A.).
The cable tells that the corporation of
Bath (Eng.) the other day decorated with
wattle blossoms a bronze tablet affixed to the
house where Governor Phillip resided in that
village. Phillip retired there on a pension
of £5OO a year, and, having no other means,
put no frills on the Bath cottage. Tenants
of the old place maintain that the ad-
miral in cocked hat still haunts the premises,
and has been observed climbing through a
manhole on to the roof at midnight.
The retirement of Dr. Mercer, of Tas-
mania, and the rumored probable early re-
tirement of Dr. Clarke, of Melbourne, are
reminders that imported Anglican bishops are
not the stickers they used to be. Broughton
Happed his apron about Sydney for 17 years,
and Barker for 28; Nixon prayed among
Tasmanian apples for 21 years, and Bromby
tor 19 ; Short bore up with the piety of Ade-
laide for 34 years, Perry reigned 'in sinful
Melbourne for 29, and Tyrrell for longer
than Newcastle coalminers remember.
,J^ g ,ll can Bish °P Long* of Bathurst
(N.S.W.), and Father M. J. O’Reilly, the
V mcentian, who have been duelling before
the public over the hoary question
of Anglican continuity, are pretty tire-
some as controversialists, but both are
really good fellows when not out
for show. The Principal of the Bathurst
college is one of the raciest wits in Aus-
tralia, while Bishop Long, the handsome,
clean-shaven, erect bush naturalist and
enthusiastic surfer, is simply wasting his
time in chopping into little lengths the use-
less old brambles of doctrinal controversy.
“Motley” : A Melbourne paper notices the
demise, in the Old Colonists’ Home, of John
Birt, aged 83, professionally known as Frank
Weston, whereupon the paragrapher gives an
entertaining account of Frank Weston, of
Wizard Oil. Birt, who arrived in Melbourne
as early as 1854. was a performer with dogs
and monkeys. Frank Weston came first in
1866 or thereabouts. He brought the AVizard
Oil, and popularised it all over Australia by
means which fat Jack Bryan vainly tried to
emulate with his Styptic; AA 7 eston would
appear on the platform with his banjo, and
give rattling good songs. Then came “Now
I will speak a piece.” An Artemus AA r ard
lecture smoothly led on to the AA 7 izard Oil,
and he wound up by convincing everybody
that they must purchase a bottle. Frank
made so much money that he returned to
America and brought Weston and Hussey’s
Minstrels, including Frank Hussey and
Harry Kelly, who forestalled Billy Emerson.
THE COOKA BURRA.
The oldest living annual almanac in N. S.
Wales, and possibly in Australia, is
“Moore's.” It was started by “Jerry” Moore
in 1852. Jerry J. Moore was a cordwainer
by trade, who in the first ’4o’s commenced
dealing in second-hand books, at a stall
against the brick wall of St. Andrew’s old
cemetery in George-street. He mended shoes
while his customers waited, and gave them a
book to read to fill in time. Jerry used to
visit the immigrant ships as they arrived, and
bought for a song all the books the passen-
gers wanted to get rid of.
John Carr, probably the oldest legislator
in Australia, is on the brink of the grave.
Born in Yorkshire 94 years ago, he went to S.
Aus. at the age of 43, and two years later
was in Parliament. Not long afterwards
he was a Minister. He and the late Pre-
mier John Colton were “the Noarlunga
twins” —both were J.C.’s, both Wesleyans
and local preachers, both sat for Noarlunga.
they were together in several Cabinets, and
both were ardent social reformers. Carr
who was seen about the city until the last
week or two, has put in his spare time for
several years in writing letters to the papers.
“I was told off,” said the Victorian official,
“to show the Talbot Excellency over a new
irrigation work. I got into the State rail-
way carriage at Spencer-street. His Ex.
was to join at Toorak. When the train
stopped at Richmond, a workman of sorts,
apparently a bit drunk, recognised the car,
put his head in at the window, no doubt be-
lieving Talbot to be on board, and roared :
‘Get work !’ It struck me as a joke, and I
thought Talbot would see it that wav, so
told him about it. He just stared for a
minute, and then remarked, in a shocked
manner, ‘How rude !’ ”
That tireless black tracker of Australa-
sian historical records, Robert McNab, of
Maoriland, has another volume, dealing with
the colonisation of the North Island, almost
ready for publication. McNab has given
years of his life and bags of money to the
job of finding out things that very few people
are interested in. The average man would
sooner know a cert for the Hurdles than be
sure that Malaspina’s Spanish expedition
touched at M.L. in 1793, and the average
woman would rather hear that potatoes were
down a penny than that the original chart
made by Tasman’s pilot had been found by
McNab in Amsterdam. It is a thankless
job, in many ways, but the true enthusiast
doesn’t worry about thanks. All the same,
Maoriland might very well knock-off for five
minutes to ask McNab to have a drop of
something—especially as he would take milk
and sugar with it.
A London evening paper is responsible
for the information that the favorite tree
hereabouts is the wattle —“named after Mr.
Watt, the Prime Minister of the Common-
wealth.”
“ LOVES LABOR LOST.”
The State Government is favorably considering a
suggestion that the Sydney premises from which the
Governor-General was “evicted ” shall be used as a
State Government House. This has given pause to
some members of the party who proposed to fight the
eviction. —News item.

hair , and I dunno who to fight."
When in Adelaide the other day Denham,
Premier of Queensland, claimed to be a
South Australian because he lived for six
years (1881-7) at Mallala —the township
which also produced Works Minister Dick
Butler.
Justice Ferguson, who has been elected
unopposed to the late Justice O’Connor’s
seat in the Senate of Sydney University,
got his degree while working as a Hansard
reporter. His predecessor also earned his
first wages as a Sydney pressman.
Sturdy William Jamieson, the N.S.W.
Government suiweyor who threw down
chain and theodolite in 1884 to become the
first manager of Broken Hill, then a despised
I mountain, sustained the sorrow of his life
the other day—-his 21-year-old son, who was
so. suited to his father that he was more
friendly than paternal, died at Jamieson’s
Central Queensland station.
The Adelaide statue to C. C. Kingston is
taking shape at last. Chief Justice Way,
Langdon Bonython and Denny, M.L.A., are
looking after the fund in Adelaide, and Dr.
Cockburn has charge of things in London,
where Alfred Drury, A..R.A., is preparing
the bronze. The Adelaide Corporation has
promised a central site for it in Victoria
Square.

A few disjointed remarks from “Ancient” :
The death is cabled from London of Susan, wife of
Hamilton Osborne—about the last of the pioneer
brothers who founded the fortunes of this vast and
mostly affluent Australian family. Hamilton Osborne
aoes not resemble a pioneer. He more suggests an
intensely sporting London clubman. Some 10 years
ago a son fell over a low-railed balcony at the Aus-
trahan C!ub (Sydney) and was killed. Shortly after-
wards Bangaroo, the family place near Canowindra,
was cut up and sold, and this branch of the Osbornes
moved to England, and have there abided ever since.

-Mrs. Binney, who died last week in Syd-
ney, was the mother of Dr. E. H. Binnev of
Macquane-street and Captain Hubert Bin-
ey, of the U.S.S. Co. Her husband, G
Nixon Binney, was the second son of one of
the most famous of all English Noncomform-
ist masters. The Rev. Thomas Binney is
still remembered as the greatest preacher of
.^ a U. and one of the most striking person-
alities in the Cold Country. Parker tells
A 3 Yu ? n , one occ asion. finding St. Paul's
Cathedral crowded, and only a few vacant
seats in the portion reserved for royafty

court or ecclesiastical flunkey ventured to
stop him. On his looks he was the Englies?
man in London, and his immense stature and
strong, intellectual face do not seem to have
been belied by his character.

Henry William Northeroft
man with a somewhat picturesnue
record, was recently selected by
the Maoriland Government as Com
missioner for the Cook Islands i n ~
eluding that gem of the South
Pacific, Raratonga. By birth an
Englishman, he arrived in M.L as
a boy with his parents. Early, he
joined the colonial forces at Tara
naki and saw enough fighting with
brother Henare to satisfy most
men. But it was meat and medi-
cine to Northeroft, and in about
50 engagements, to say nothing 0 f
mere bickerings, he was wherever
he could most impress Henare with
the necessity of going quietly. il e
gained the M.L. Cross for con-
spicuous gallantry in rescuing
comrades, and rose to be a captain
of militia. In later years he took
to the law, and sat on the Bench of
the Stipendiary Magistrates’ Court in vari-
ous parts of the Loanly Islands.
Admiral Bedford, one time Governor of
Westralia, is dead, aged 75. His first active
service was in the Black Sea during the
Crimean War.
H. D. L. Woods, of N.S.W. Crcwn Law
Office, has been promoted to the job of Chief
Clerk of the Supreme Court. ‘‘Little Woods”
was one of the popular football idols in the
days when ’Varsity and Randwick competed
for the Rugby football championship. Woods
was the Uni. fullback, and has also repre-
sented the State in the same capacity.
The new Master of S. Aus. Supreme Court
is W. L. Stuart, 37, a son of the late Local
Court Judge and Insolvency Commissioner.
The new man has stood on pretty nearly
every step on the way up, and has been
acting-Master since Buchanan, so long the
holder of the post, became acting-judge
and president of the Industrial Arbitra-
tion Court. In his spare time, Stuart is a
major in the Defence forces.

Marie d’Este, wife of the new Regent of
Bavaria, ought, according to a lot of people,
to be Queen of England. Charles I. is among
her ancestors, and she may or may not be
ashamed of the fact. Anyhow. George seems
to have a pretty firm seat on his gilded chair,
and even if the job were vacant a Stuart
need hardly apply. But it must comfort Marie
a lot to know that there is an organisation
in Maoriland formed with the special object
of upholding her claims.
DARE TO BE A WOWSER!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Don’t be disheartened if worldlings call you
“Wowser.” Fight on ! —Encouraging battle shriek.
Dare to be a Wowser and shame the scoffing world !
Fight the sooty Devil with a stout gamp bravely
furled !
Pray him down, and sing him down, and vent your
rage and spite,
For he’s nothing but the Devil, and it serves the
Devil right!
Dare to be a Wowser and make your wowsing felt!
Kick the Devil from behind, and punch below the
belt!
Put the boot into the Fiend, and let him plainly see
You are not hampered by the rule of any referee!
Dare to be a Wowser, and gouge and kick and
throttle!
Use your head, and elbows, too, and smash him with
a bottle !
Try a toe-hold now and then, and trip him on the
level,
Then strangle him until he croaks, “I’m sorry I’m
the Devil!”
Dare to be a Wowser, and jamb your bonv knee
Into the Devil’s neck and pant, “You won’t play Hell
with me !”
But don’t make him a Wowser, when holiness you’ve
gleaned,
For he’s our only Devil and a necessary Fiend.
N.S.W.

The profits are better; but the balance-sheet figures
don’t differ much from those of 12 months ago.
We might as well get to them at once:—
1911. 1912.
Notes.. .. £79,843 £37,058 Dec. £42,785
Bills . . . . 1,332,056 1,310,309 Dec. 21,747
Deposits . . 20,948,705 20,589,544 Dec. 359,161
Liquid assets. . 11,207,796 10,772,116 Dec. 435,680
Advances . . 13,762,776 14,091,956 Inc. 329,180
Premises . . 510,588 538,943 Inc. 28,345
There is not an unexpected movement among the lot.
Deposits have naturally shrunk a trifle. The Com-
mercial of Sydney does most of its business in
N. S. Wales, where its figures (deposits, for in-
stance) are greater than those of any other bank.
Its only Australian branches outside that State are
in Queensland. And both N. S. Wales and Queens-
land have been living in a furious riot of extrava-
gance. Australia’s imports of merchandise last year
amounted to £76,412,308. To pay for that lot
and the vast interest bill on old borrowings, it
exported merchandise valued at £66,391,293. The
foreigner didn’t give us the balance. We have to
pay for it with the gold we had accumulated or are
digging up (and there is very little of the latter),
or we must give our lOU. The banks have thus
been steadily drawn upon, and it is surprising that
the Commercial of Sydney doesn’t show a reduction
of more than £359,161 in deposits. It was also
inevitable that, though deposits and other resources
would shrink, more assistance would be called for
by many clients —in connection with uncompleted
land-purchases, and buildings and business exten-
sions, etc.
Fortunately this bank is able to meet such a de-
mand. For many years past it has held over 50 p.c.
■of its liabilities liquid, and even now it has over
49 p.c.—an extraordinarily strong position, and at-
tractive alike to borrowers and lenders. This policy
as without a doubt largely responsible for the ex-
traordinary growth of the bank’s figures. Look at
it for a 10-year period :
Dec., ’O2. Dec., ’l2.
Notes and bills £1,181,086 £1,347,367 Inc. 14.0 p.c.
Deposits .. 11,804,568 20,589,544 Inc. 74.4 „
Liquid assets. 5,363,347 10,772,116 Inc. 100.8 ~
Advances .. 9,269,271 14,091,956 Inc. 51.9 ~
Premises . . 447,100 538,943 Inc. 20.5 „
The paid capital is now £1,739,553; when the
final instalment of the last lot of new capital is
paid in March, the total will be £1,750,000. Twelve
months ago it was £1,500,000. With capital coming
in at odd times during the past few years, it is
impossible to say exactly what the earnings have
been in proportion to the average amount of the
shareholders’ funds, that average amount not being
ascertainable. This, however, gets somewhere near
the facts of the case:—
June, 1908 £7 5 7 p.c. I Dec., 1910 £8 1 10 p.c.
Dec., 1908 6 18 7 „ | June, 1911 7 19 8 „
June, 1909 7 9 0 „ Dec., 1911 7 19 10 „
Dec., 1909 8 4 7 „ June, 1912 8 211 „
June, 1910 8 2 11 „ | Dec., 1912 7 18 4 „
The figures are not exceptionally high; in fact, they
are only moderate. But the quality of the assets
must be considered; what the £14,091,956 of ad-
vances are like one can’t tell, of course, by merely
looking at the imposing figures in the balance-sheet,
but the £10,772,116 of liquid assets tell their own
tale. They don’t produce much immediate profit,
however; but they add enormously to the strength of
the concern. They add, also, to its prestige, and if
a bank keeps its head, prestige means future profit.

(Sydney), formerly the A.J.S. Bank: —
