For a bank as big as the A.B.C. the profit is small.
In searching for an explanation, the size of the
inscribed deposits, which are a sort of pref. capital,
has to be kept in mind. Add the interest on that
capital, and the profits for the half-year were
£60,092. Also there is the other fact that the dog
had earned a bad name, and it takes a good while to
get the public to believe that he is a converted dog,
even though you do bury him as Snap and resurrect
him as Fido. Still, time is working on the side of
the A. 8.0. So far as new business is concerned
its position is uncommonly good. A bank depositor,
for instance, wants to know above everything what
security a bank offers him. That security depends
on the size of the bank’s own capital and reserves.
The bigger the capital and reserves in proportion to
the borrowed money, the better the security, other
things being equal. A further point to be considered
by the depositor is the quality of the assets. Are
they good ? In other words, are the capital and
reserves, which guarantee the deposits, as big as they
pretend to be? Finally, he needs to know what
is the proportion of liquid assets to liabilities. For,
obviously, a bank might be quite sound, and yet be
thrown into most serious trouble through being
unable to meet a sudden demand (a mad demand, it
might be) for deposit money. Now, how does this
bank answers those tests? Consider, first, these
figures:—
1911. 1912.
Paid capital £1,193,048 £1,195,275
Inscribed denosits . . . . 1,730,198 1,711,448
All other debts . . . . 2,743,160 2,691,287
Liquid assets .. . . 1,203,623 1,328,416
Advances, etc. .. .. 4,109,223 3,915,748
Premises, etc. .. .. 369,783 375,393
As mentioned above, the inscribed deposits are to all
intents and purposes pref. capital. The entire capital
is, therefore, £2,906,723. AH other debts (deposits,
notes and bills) amount to £2,691,287. Every £1
of the public’s money is, therefore, protected by about
£1 2s. of fixed money. Excluding the inscribed de-
posits altogether, the liabilities amount to less than
£2 ss. for every £1 of capital and reserves. In
ordinary circumstances it is not thought out of the
way in Australia if a bank takes £7 of the public
money for every £1 of its own. In England the
figures run even higher. As to the other tests, the
bank has £1,328,416 liquid, and £3,915,748 in ad-
vances and other assets apart from premises. Its new
liabilities are £2,691,286. Thus, it could pay just
on 50 p.c. of its liabilities with its liquid assets ; and
its other assets are worth, according to the books,
three times as much as the balance of the liabilities
No depositor could want better than that. And the
cash position is so strong that the borrower ought to
feel fairly satisfied that if his securities are all right
he won’t be harassed to pay up at the most awkward
time—a very important consideration. There is no
visible reason why the bank shouldn’t push ahead.
To all appearances it has gone through the fire and
been thoroughly purified. Big dividends are certainly
out of the question for very many years to come. The
size of the capital stands in the way of anything of
that sort. But that very feature makes the A.B.C.
attractive—or ought to make it attractive—to the
client.

June, 1912 .. 19,023 10 ~ ~ 15,000 4,023 117,182
Dec., 1912 .. 16,782 10 „ „ 15,417 1,365 118,547
It has never been possible to take this co.’s pub-
lished figures seriously. It has shown a certain
amount of profit; but the amount it has made
and not shown can only be guessed at. Go
back seven years, to the end of 1905. At
that time the paid capital was £140,000.
it is now £324,389, or £184,389 more. Of this
amount £50,000 was transferred from reserves,
leaving £134,389 of the new capital actually sup-
plied by shareholders. Now, during those seven
years the following vessels have been added to the
fleet: —Kanimbla, Kubu, Kirawa, Kaikai, Kailoa,
Kirrule, Kiandra and Kosciusko, along with a few
horse punts and small ferries ; and four others have
either just left the stocks or are building. It is safe
to say that these boats have cost at least £150,000.
Yet, as shown above, only £134,389 of new capital
has been got in. Further, as at Dec., 1905, the
landed property was valued at £88,408. l.arge
purchases have been made around the Harbor, and
the item stands now at £131,092, an increase cf
£42,684. Cash balances, less liabilities, on Dec.
31, 1905, were £18,700. They are now £64,962.
Ihus in these two items alone-—landed property and
cash balances—there is a visible £88,946 more than
there was seven years ago. That leaves only
£45,443 of the new capital to account, for. Ap-
proximately £15,000 of that has gone into new
wharves, waiting rooms, machinery, stores, etc.
Therefore, except for about £30,000, the whole of
the money put into ferries during the past seven
years has been provided by the co.’s customers.
Those customers have not only provided the co. with
a dividend equal to about 14 p.c. on the actually
paid capital, but they have also supplied the co.
with the new fleet mentioned above. This is over
the odds, and a co. which demands a profit of this
nature for supplying a public necessity is just about
the worst enemy of Private Enterprise that could
be conceived. Now, on the pretext that Wages
Board awards have put up the running costs, the co.
sends up a kite to find out how the wind blows with
regard to an all-round increase of season fares. Two
years ago, when the McGowen Labor Government
was comparatively new to office, such a thing would
not have been thought of. Indeed, eager to show
the public that it was quite a fair concern, the co.
about that time reduced the rates. But the directors
are not afraid of the McGowen Government any
longer. After two vears’ experience they know that
it is on the side of the boomster. In addition to
throwing this bomb at the public, the directors
calmly announce a further increase of capital. A
Government which had any sense of its obligations
to the public would take the bull by the horns
promptly. The co. is in precisely the same position
as the Gas Cos. The goods the latter supply are
no more of a public necessity than the service the
Sydney Ferries, Ltd., supplies. The Gas Cos. have
been told by Act that future issues of capital must
not carry more than 7 p.c.; future issues of pref.
capital are limited to 5 p.c. The same provision
should be made in this case. And the investor who
it tempted to buy at extravagant prices can rest
assured that sooner or later something of this sort —
if not something more drastic—will happen.

Big changes have been taking place in connection with
N.S.W. gas cos. ; and as one result this table has had
to be recast. The Gas Bill was the earthquake. As
originally introduced, it proposed to allow gas cos. to
pay 4 p.c. as a standard div. on their capital. A co.
might have got from its shareholders more premiums
than capital; but premiums were not to count. Also
a co. might have providently curtailed its dividends
and thus increased its reserves ; but that wasn’t to
count either. Of course, the Bill didn’t go through like
that. As passed, it allowed the cos. six months in which
to capitalise their reserves, and on the total capital
thus arrived at they may pay 10 p.c. If thev reduce
the price of gas below 3s. 6d. per 1000 ft. they may
for every Id. reduction in price pay an extra \ p.c.
of dividend. The Australian Gaslight Co. was not
treated any too well in the new law. Others were
not limited as to the amount of reserves they might
capitalise ; this particular co. was told that it must
not capitalise more than £425,000. This, as Bruce
Smith would say when he wanted to cut things short
was an injurious differentiation, and it was an unfair
one. It would have been a proper thing to have pro-
vided for the appointment of a valuator to represent
the public interests in every case and prevent the
over-valuation of assets and the consequent inflation
of reserves and over-capitalisation of cos. ; but that
wasn’t done. Indeed, the whole business was utterly
discreditable; the men in charge of it seem not to
have known the most elementary things about it; and
they proposed such patently monstrous things that the
whole business was taken out of their hands, and,
except for a line here and there (mostly poor ones),
it was seized by people who were very little concerned
about safeguarding the public.
The capital was £849,996 in 141,666 shares of £6
each fully paid. The co. has now issued one new
share for every two old ones, making the capital
£1,274,994. The Act says that “the amount of pre-
mium capital and reserves which may be capitalised
by the Australian Gaslight Co. shall not exceed the
sum of £425,000.” No further division of bonus
shares can, therefore, take place, no matter what
the assets may be worth. And the chances are that
they are worth far more than they stand at in the
books. In other words, it is practically certain that
a large amount of undisclosed profit has in past years
been put into works, etc. However, this investment is
not wasted; it will at least make it easier for the co.
to pay the 10 p.c. div. on the new capital (= 15 p.c.
on the old capital) under any adverse trade conditions
that may come along. To entitle the co. to pay 11
p.c. it must reduce the price to 3s. 2d. And as it
will not hereafter drop undisclosed profits into works,
it is quite probable that that price will comte along
before very long. Any new capital that may be raised
can carry only 7 p.c. as a standard ; and the shares
representing such capital must be offered by public
auction or tender in lots of no more than £lOO worth.
The directors may fix a reserve price beforehand for
these new shares. If the public does not take them
they may be offered at the reserve price to existing
shareholders, consumers and employees as the direct-
ors think fit. If they don’t go then, they shall be put
up to auction or tender again. And, finally, any
remnant may be disposed of as the directors please.
The premium capital got for any such shares is to go
to a special purposes fund ; but this fund is never to
exceed 10 p.c. of the capital. This fund is really a
contingency reserve. The idea throughout is to pre-
vent the accumulation of reserves, visible or invisible,
in order, of course, that the consumer may get the
benefit of a reduced price of gas when the circum-
stances allow of it.

Of the profit made during the past half-year it will be
seen that rather more than one-third was put into new
works. Taking the full 12 months past, the follow-
ing shows what has been done with the profit:—
Dividend (111 p.c.) .. .. £98,580
Put into new works . . . . 59,212
Reconstructing works, written off,
etc. .. 35,693
Added to formal Reserve.. .. 8,183
Total profit for the year . . £201,668
The amount shown as spent on reconstructions, etc.,
is additional to the sums ordinarily spent on renewals,
replacements, etc. The latter are charged up with
working expenses. The sums spent on new works are
in direct relief of capital expenditure. As at Decem-
ber, 1904, the capital expenditure on works was
£2,149,842. The total now shown as charged to
capital account is £2,263,170. Thus, of all the ad-
ditions and extensions effected during the past eight
years, only £113,328 has been charged to capital;
the rest has been paid for out of profits, and not
shown in the balance-sheet. In the past four years
alone, £180,092 of profit has gone that way. So, be-
sides getting their dividend, shareholders have had
5i p.c. p.a. dropped into their assets. It is an amaz-
ingly fine performance—from the shareholders’ point
of view. From the consumers’ point of view the thing
is not quite so satisfactory.
Nor does it look as though the co. will be dis-
turbed in its field of clover. It is rather different
in that respect from the big Sydney co. In the lat-
ter’s Act there was no provision for public purchase.
The Melbourne co. arranged things better—4t pro-
vided a plan of purchase which practically compels
the public authority to either leave it alone or pay an
exorbitant price. The price stipulated for by the Act
is six times the total of the last three years’ profits ;
and, though there is always some doubt about the
meaning of “profits,” it doesn’t look as though the
co. could be got rid of for under £3,000,000. Of course,
Parliament might amend the Act and make the terms
less onerous ; but the money having been subscribed on
these specific terms, it is very doubtful whether any
Legislature would repudiate and alter the terms now.
The only other protection the public was offered by
the Act was through the limitation of dividends ; the
co. might pay 10 p.c. if it sold gas at 7s. per 1000 ft.
and i p.c. more for every l£d. reduction. The pre-
sent price (4s. 7d. per 1000) thus permits the co. to
pay 15$ p.c. p.a. Consequently, the real protection
given to the public by the Act isn’t worth speaking
about. Nor does the electric light and power enter-
prise of the City Council seem to give the co. any
trouble. That concern ought to be a strong competi-
tor ; but the council, instead of using it to secure
cheap light and power for the people, seems chiefly
eager that it should pay money into the municipal
funds, and thus relieve Property of some of its ordin-
ary rates.

This concern is getting the results which its long-
headed management deserves. There was a time
when the situation looked pretty hopeless. The Ash-
croft sulphide-treating process had been bought by an
English co. for the usual extravagant sum, and the
Central mine at Broken Hill was secured to furnish
an ore supply. The mine had been much over-valued
and when the process failed utterly, Ashcroft excused
himself and it on the. plea of that over-valuation,
'pe co. was thus left with a most expensive plant, fit
for little but the scrap heap, and a mine from which
no substantial profit could be made by any mode of
treatment known at the time. Then C. F. Courtney
was brought in as general manager, and he schemed
and planned, with the results shown above. Not, of
course, that he did everything. The mine helped, and
lately the markets have helped ; and the co. has had
a fine staff. To-day there is not a more perfectlj
equipped mine in Australia—probably not in the
world. Broken Hill sulphides are a pretty hard nut
to crack. Not so many years ago a co. 'thought it
was doing very well if it got out 70 p.c. of the lead
and 65 p.c. of the silver ; the zinc wasn’t anything
but a curse. Last year this co. recovered 92.9 p.c. of
the silver, 93.1 p.c. of the lead and 89.9 p.c. of the
zinc in concentrates ready for the furnaces. And
“whilst this extraction and classification is most satis-
factory, it is expected that in the future some further
improvements will be made, raising the grade of the
zinc concentrates, and increasing the quantity of lead
concentrates recovered from them.” The process is
practically continuous. The ore is fed into the lead
mill, and with the help of water a mechanical separa-
tion is made there, the bulk of the lead and silver
being got out. The “tailings” go on to the zinc
mill, where oil and acid and water get to work in
the flotation or froth process. This gets out the bulk
of the zinc and a little more silver and lead. The
product goes to the de-leading plant, for the separa-
tion of the silver and lead from the zinc. The silver-
lead concentrates join the concentrates from the lead
mill, and mostly go on to the lead furnaces at New-
castle ; the zinc concentrates still go to Europe. The
co. tried to produce metallic spelter at Newcastle
and succeeded ; but there is, unfortunately, more
money to be made by exporting the stuff, arid giving
the Belgians and Germans the work that Australians
should be doing.
The table shows that £63,870 was written off for
the year. This includes £33,870 written off for de-
preciation—say, 3£ p.c. all round, which doesn’t seem
enough—and £30,000 put to a contingency fund.
A few thousands more ought, perhaps, to be added to
the profits, because Profit and Loss has been charged
with £2958, which was payable as premium on the
debenture stock ( £59,161) redeemable on December
31, 1912. The co. had plenty of means to pay off the
debt. This shows how things have moved along:

The output of ore last year (to June 30) was

opments at the bottom level (1200 ft.) have been so
favorable that the co. is prodding the earth another
100 ft. forthwith. There is an end ; but it is a long
way off. The course of the metal markets is of more

The metal contents of the concentrates were as fol-
lows: Lead, 32,616 tons; silver, 2,401,9520 z. ; spelter,
35,451 tons. On those figures one can work out ap-
proximately what difference a fall of Id. for silver
and £1 for lead and zinc would make in the profits.
Put it at £59,000, and you will probably not be out-
side the mark —you may be a bit inside it.
The causes which led up to the smash in
the J. D. Williams market are to get into the
courts—some of the causes, anyway. First, one of
the co.’s managers was arrested on a forgery charge
in connection with scrip. Then J. D. Williams, having
first been sacked from the office of managing direc-
tor, was arrested on a charge of harboring the
aforesaid manager. The action against Williams
was taken on information sworn by one of the
directors of the co. There are, or were, two factions
on the board. Williams bluntly accuses the other
faction of declaring at least one interim dividend
for the express purpose of boosting shares; and his
protest against the dividend is on record in the
minute book. It is a nasty allegation, and it will
have to be answered. The only reply so far is the
arrest of Williams on a charge which has no bearing
whatever on this point. Meantime it is to the
credit of pretty well the whole sharebroking frater-
nity of Sydney that they have consistently advised
their investing clients to stand off this stock.
Nearly all the boosting has been done from outside.
* * * *
Ardlethan tinfield (N.S.W.), though un-
questionably rich at surface, has yet to prove itself at
depth, and speculators ought to carefully read Geolo-
gist Pittman’s report on the nature of the granite
formation in which the lodes occur. Take, as a
sample, the Homeward Bound mine. It reports that
No. 2 shaft is down 38ft. At that depth the lode
stuff is about sft. wide. The lode is split up and the
tin is very fine. The estimate is 15 p.c., which, of
course, is a splendid prospect. Probablv it means 15
p.c. of tin oxide, in which case, taking oxide at £lOO,
the assay is equivalent to £l5 per ton. The report,
however, doesn’t state that 15 p.c. tin oxide, is the
average across a lode sft. wide ; indeed, as the lode
is “split up,” the average may be less than 5 p.c.
The Carpathia has its shaft down 27ft. The sinking
is in disturbed country. The lode stuff, according to
Geologist Pittman, contains granite, mica, quartz and
felspar, with a sprinkling of tin oxide. Blocks of
ore, weighing about scwt., have been found, estimated
to assay 60 p.c. These lumps occur in patches. Four
distinct classes of ore have been obtained from the
open cut. So far, no solid lode with defined walls
has been disclosed, and they are still searching in the
open-cut for a rich spot to put down a shaft. The
mine is favorably situated with regard to transport to
the Wyalong battery, and the high-grade ore men-
tioned so frequently would give a handsome return if
large quantities were available. There are 60,000
paid shares and 60,000 contributing. The former
have been exchanged at 10s. and the latter at Bs.,
making a total of £54,000 for the lot. It doesn’t
take very much tin ore as rich as that exposed in the
Carpathia to give a profit of £54,000, with the
metal at its present price; but it is one thing to
make profit and another thing for the shareholders to
ffetit. Big credit balances, derived from surface
stuff, frequently disappear in the search for perma-
nent bodies and in providing plant to treat ore that
is never found.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. Sd. per cub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd.— a saving of 2s. 5d.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

It was announced some time ago that a return of 20
carats per 100 loads would be a fair thing, so
presumably the first run is satisfactory, allowing for
poor and valueless stones. The price per carat, in
the rough, has been moving up of late. Taking 20s.
as an average for the lot, the yield works out at 17s.
6d. per load, not counting the sweetening of tin
oxide in the wash. The leads are patchy, and require
a good deal of testing before their value can be
hazarded. The deposits at Copeton are alluvial.
The wash generally lies in granite, and is overlain
by basalt; erosion has left it in scattered hills and
ridges. Tunnelling has been the usual form of pros-
pecting, but as the wash often dips towards the centre
of the hills, the prospectors soon lose level. Not
having the capital to drive low-level tunnels in
barren country beneath the wash, they are then
compelled to have a shot elsewhere. The chances
are that a crowd with a bit of money may happen
on a few decent pickings. At the Streak of Luck
mine a shaft was sunk some years ago. After going
140 ft. it met gravel up to 9ft. in thickness, averaging
8 to 10 carats of diamonds per load, and about
151 b. of stream tin. Attention, however, is
now being turned more in the direction of dis-
covering the original matrix or source, and the
recent finds of diamond-bearing dolerite are said to
have been confirmed by reliable authorities.
Great Cobar, though it is apparently not
suffering from shortage of labor now, has another
threatened trouble. Drought hangs on to the dis-
trict, and the mine hasn’t water enough to last much
more than a month. And at this time of year it is
often a good deal more than a month between drinks
in the Far West.
* * * *
Mount Drysdale and Eldorado (N.S.W.),
hand in hand, have once again presented their weari-
some supplication for more time to mature that long-
threatened, but never-ready, plan of mutual support.
After repeatedly granting suspension of labor condi-
tions, the warden began to think it was getting be-
yond the joke stage. The last suspension expired
in November ; but as the cos. haven’t shown any signs
of animation since then, they have been asked to
show reason why they should be allowed to exist and
worry the official mind any further.
• * * *
The much messed-about Queen Bee, near
Cobar (N.S.W.), had a swag of forfeited shares sold
by auction a few days since at 3d. per share. A
little lot of 98 tons has lately been sent to the Great
Cobar smelter. This policy of carting ore to Cobar
for treatment is certainly better than trying to smelt
small quantities under difficulties.
* * * *
Lloyd Copper Co., with a nominal capital
of £280,000 in £1 shares, has been listed in Syd-
ney. The subscribed capital is £235,000, in fully
paid-up £1 shares; reserve shares, 45,000. Previous
returns show the extraction from crude ore varied
from 2 p.c. to 2$ p.c. copper. Taking the latter
assay, it will require 40 tons of crude ore to run out
a ton of copper. With costs at from 255. to 30s. per
ton of ore, copper will cost from £5O to £6O to pro-
duce ; so Lloyd’s is up against a problem in economy.
Previously copper was produced for £56 per ton, and
with cheaper firewood this cost may be reduced some-
what —always assuming the average extraction is
maintained at 2£ p.c.
* * * *
“Collins Street” : The Australian “guinea
pig” director is becoming more voracious. With
the aid of proxies sent to himself, he has been
raising his fees gradually, taking a little more now
and a little more next time at the half-yearly meet-
ings, which in the case of successful cos. are never
attended bv more than a handful of shareholders.
Mount Lyell led the way ; then the B. H. Proprietary
directors got their fees raised; the North Broken
Hill Co. and other smaller fry followed. The latest
coterie to raise board fees from £lOOO to £l5OO is
the Broken Hill South, and for the work the directors
do they would be well paid at £5OO a year divided
amongst them. For, after all, the manager and the
secretary are the people who really run the com-
panies’ affairs. The directors are merely the chaps
who sign the cheques.
* * * *
Phillips River (W.A.) gold-copper shows,
with which Germany was to show Australia its
complete ignorance of smelting practice and metal-
lurgy, are evidently at their last gasp. Hopes were
prolonged by the Whim Well Copper Co.’s controllers,
who held out hopes a while back that they could
procure some necessary cash; but an appeal by
Phillips River debenture holders for tributing parties
evidently reflects the failure of that financial scheme.
In reality, the district is better suited for wheat
growing and sheep raising than for profitable mining
on anything like a big scale. #
Associated Northern continues a sparkling
attraction in the Westralian market, a run of high
values having culminated in the richest face seen in
W.A. for some years. The shoot at No. 4 level in the
company’s Ora Banda property shows 82s. for 30ft.,
2405. for 45ft., and 2305. for 20ft. Then the drills
broke into specimen ore worth three figures in
ounces per ton. The best feature of the shoot is the
continuance of values on rising from the drive. At
the first point opened on, 20ft. shows 90s. per tom
while 2605. is recorded for 21ft. in a rise 50ft. off
the first exploration work. Values have been de-
clared conservatively, and as £6OOO profit per month
was within easv reach before the present bonanza
was kliown of. the task of the millman should be
still easier now. Without latest developments a
profit of £lOO.OOO was claimed as being in sight.
The co. (in 350.000 shares) has also a lot of cash
in hand, and a property in Mexico, now held up on
account of revolutionary troubles From the Golden
Mile lease a small revenue is still secured with the
help of tributors. *

Pavne’s Find (W.A.) shareholders have
been told bv the manager that, after careful sampling
their allegedly wonderful show has a visible value of
£BOOO. Consolation is offered in the managerial
faith that some rich patches may be got. A more
comforting message would have been some assurance
that the gold was likely to go down. Any balance on
the ore in sight will at least be useful in breaking
a most significant silence on this point.
* * * *
After manv months of tongue-wagging,
the mine workers of the Golden Mile have passed the
01, t w ith a loud thud, deciding by 2364
”? ’ 5 " votes to go to arbitration. The real facts are
that labor is so short on the Mile and elsewhere
underground, and is having so free a time, that the
foolishness of even talking strike dawned on all but
the most crimson-d.yed of the red-raggers. It is quite
likely that a mutual agreement may be come to be-
fore machinery of the court is set in motion.
Great Victoria, Southern Cross (W.A.),
is being pierced fore and aft and in every other direc-
tion to gauge values. Quantity calculated at about four
million tons is admitted, and if evidence of quality
bears out prospectors’ claims, the price asked will be
P ai s| by the Great Boulder, and a big mill will be in-
stalled. Expectations are restricted solely to the
oxidised zone. Below this is a conglomeration, men-
tioned by metallurgists with horror.
A look at Edna May (W.A.) showed (1)
that the reef is strong and free milling, and enters
the property on the eastern boundary, and (2) that
the main workings consist of a level "driven 240 ft. at
70ft., with two shafts, one vertical to 70ft., and the
other on the underlay to about 40ft. below that
depth On the 70ft. level values are officially esti-
mated to average between 35 and 45 dwt., while in
the underlay shaft they are stated to go as high as
•Hoz. A little water was struck just below 70ft.,
but the country remains soft in the bottom, and so
far there is no sign of refractory ore. Between the
eastern boundary and the underlay shaft the reef
trends approximately east and west, and is inter-
sected by two crosscourses, which, however, do not
appear to have disturbed the reef to any extent. West
of the same shaft, where the quartz is over 10ft.
wide, the reef assumes a boomerang shape, turning
approximately south; in the end of the level it
appears badly faulted and thrown. At this point, the
reef seems to have had its course further altered, and
the underlay looks as though it might also turn from
west to east. Moderate exploration is needed to de-
termine these matters. Where the fault occurs,
values are stated to be 25dwt. As the cap of the
reef has been exposed at only one point, ore re-
serves are hard to estimate, but they may be safely
put down at several thousand tons. Except for the
disturbance in the south end of the 70ft. level the
outlook is bright.
* * * *
The much reconstructed Gwalia Consoli-
dated, Wiluna (W.A.), has had another reincarnation,
a new co. having been formed with a capital of
£125,000, in half-crown shares, to give the mine an-
other run. The inspiration in this instance is not so
much the mine as a volatilisation treatment process,
by which it is hoped not only to secure extraction
from the excessively slimy arsenical Wiluna ore, but
also to win possible royalties by extending the pro-
cess to the Golden Mile and elsewhere.
“M.C.”: Gwalia Consolidated has a new
roasting and volatilising process, from which it is
expecting great things. Superintendent Ben Howe
invented the process. He roasts the ore with a small
quantity of salt at a temperature of 1000° centigrade.
The metallic vapors are led through chambers and
drenched with water, and the gold is finally recovered
by filter pressing. The trouble with a process of this
kind is to maintain a high enough temperature and
yet prevent the ore from sintering. Sintering pre-
vents metal passing off as fume, and it is proving of
great benefit to copper and lead smelters on that ac-
count. The Ben Howe process, however, aims at pro-
moting volatilisation, and its success depends mainly
on a careful regulation of very high temperatures.
Something is evidently very wrong with
Hampden (Q.). Or if it isn’t wrong yet, there is
danger in the distance. The impending trouble ap-
pears to be in connection with the supply of power
from the gas plant.
* * * *
Apparently the re-formed Mount Oxide
Co. is to have a capital of £475,000 in £1 shares,
cut up as follows: 200,000 to the old co. (share for
share) ; 275.000 to the English people in return for
£150,000 of cash. The least satisfactory feature of
the Oxide throughout has been the amount of
“paper” in the flotations ; and this latest reconstruc-
tion, though it supplies the necessary cash, does it
at a big cost in underwriting commission, bonus
shares, etc. Fortunately the mine is developing very
well, and it needs to in order to carry what it is
loaded with.
• * * *
Major Charley and Captain Osborne have
a working option over Mount Warminster copper
mine, near Mount Rockhampton, and not far from
Great Fitzroy. Ned Trickett, the sculler of old, was
one of the original prospectors; but Trickett’s objec-
tive was silver. The quartz veins round about Mount
Warminster carry argentiferous galena. Trickett’s
shaft is probably there to this day, and so are the
old excavations in search of copper ore. Some of the
ore was left on the surface until the bags got rotten ;
the owner of the land prevented its removal until
payment had been made. He took no risk, and
lived many years to watch over his barren freehold.
* ♦ * *
“Platman” : There is a kick left in some
of the old Far Northern (Q.) goldfields yet. For the
first time for years a crushing is reported from the
Wandercloud, reopened by one Morton. It was only
seven tons, but it went 4oz. of gold, worth £4 per oz.,
to the ton. Another resurrect’on is the Minnie
Moxham, at Northcote, between Mareeba and Thorn-
borough. Five tons went soz. of gold, worth £3
17s. 6d. per oz. Northcote is the only absolutely
deserted goldfield I have seen; all the others have
had one or two fossickers whose faith, plus an odd
dwt. or so, enabled them to hang on. But there
wasn’t a soul in Northcote. The remnants of the
furniture of the pub were packed into a spare
room. They were all spare, as a matter of fact. The
trouble with Northcote is that the stone carries a
lot of antimony, and antimony and quicksilver don t
agree. Another Northern mine that deserves men-
tion is the Just in Time. It is chiefly remarkable
for its prospectus, in which promoters and vendors
ask for neither cash nor paid-up shares. All they
ask for is a bunch of contributings and the cost of
taking up the lease. The holder is Keating, of the
Tyrconnel, Hodgkinson, and he is backing his faith
in the show by announcing his intention to take up
1000 cons. North Queenslanders can’t believe he is
well.
* * * *
At Charters Towers the New Queen
Cross has been providing a mild gamble. If the
crushings maintain the excellent average of £7 14s.
4d. per ton got from the first lot, the Queen Cross
may figure again on the dividend list. Still, there
are 200,000 shares, the number having been doubled
during a wild boom some years ago. Past history is
glorious, and it is on record that 140,000 tons gave
£7 16s. per ton. But the Moonstone lease is another
bird altogether, and up to date only 100 ft. or so
along a shallow level has been proved. On the other
side of the shaft there are a few inches of nice look-
ing brown stone, but the reef is very variable^
* *
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, has
just crushed 466 tons for £2500 (£5 11s. 7d. per
ton) The half-year closes in March, but no definite
information has leaked out about the long-expected
dividend, which, if it is Is., would cost £7500 The
first crushing this half-year was 402 tons for £2696,
which compares with £2500 for the last fortnightly
lot. Returns like that should give a good profit; but
the co. started the half-year with a debt of £4170,
and has had to spend money freely on mine develop-
ment. The work done has had a double objective—im-
proved ventilation and increased ore reserves—and as
a result No. 5 level (2600ft.j now shows a payable
reef for 170 ft., and in places there is sft. of stone.
Still, the stoping average isn’t anything like sft.,
or the tonnage crushed would have increased more
rapidly. The Deeps are anxious to make the Brilliant
Block shaft, next door, an upcast, so as to improve
the ventilation, and are willing to spend a large sum
of money with that end in view. That is another
reason for delaying the dividend.
Chairman Plant, of the Brilliant Block,
Charters Towers, gave shareholders an interesting ac-
count of the agreement entered into with its more
fortunate neighbor, the Brilliant Deeps. One of the
great difficulties on the Towers is the high rock tem-
perature, which at 2600 ft. sometimes reaches 120°
Fahr. The only remedy is a large volume of air.
B is with the object of securing this air that the
Brilliant Deeps are willing to pay 70s. per foot to-
wards the cost of sinking a shaft from the end of the
Brilliant Block’s crosscut at the 2000 ft. level. The
shaft will be 700 ft. deep, and will be sunk in the
granite at an angle of 45 degrees. The Brilliant
Deeps also undertake to drive some 400 ft. in a direct
line to the shaft from their boundary at the 2600 ft.
level, all of which work is being done to improve the
ventilation. How much reef they will develop for
the Brilliant Block depends on the course of the
formation, which is somewhat erratic, and has de-
viated more to the south than was expected.
A gentle shove is being given to Bendigo
by the twin scribes who push the pen in the Bee-
hive for the Melbourne dailies. They have picked
out an old stager in Carlisle as the dainty puss of
the moment. If what has been taken out of a mine
be a commendation, Carlisle is all right, for it has
punched out 350,0000 z. of precious metal; but its
div. return is a long way below those of such giants
as Garden Gully, Great Extended Hustlers, John-
son’s, and, for that matter, the Catherine Reef. Still,
it has its splashes of luck, which have put
£48,00,0 of profits into the pockets of the unco’
guid. To-day the flurry is over an east back at
1140 ft. Here good gold has begun to make, and
the rise from 1200 ft. reveals a system of spurs with
rich stuff. Thus there is a run of crushing ground
20ft. in width to be developed along the big slide.
In olden times Carlisle’s neighbors to the north got
real stingo above this slide; but the market must
wait on slow Bendigo ways to prove whether the
claim is to have the same slabs of luck. And that
is tantalising. Spurs and expectations combined
have doubled the price of scrip, and the co. has got
back to the dividend list.
* * * *
Poseidon Alluvial has got into the deep
gutter without being flooded. Officially it is told
that 2oz. prospects have been collected by an able
and energetic manager. But the plunger somehow
isn’t putting much on Poseidons. There is always
a wobbly sort of feeling that the 2oz. prospects may
be cut out by a band of clay, or swept away by a
gush of water. Still a 2oz. prospect gives a nice
lead to the deep ground, which up to that time had
produced much talk, but very little to go on to the
scale. Now with the water forked off the shallow
gutter and the deep ground ahead, the co. has the
chance to, prove that it owns a mine.
» * * #
Powlett'North, Wonthaggi (Vic.), is the
last remaining bit of the Powlett coal ground which
was held by speculators before the State elected to
squat down in the Basin. Peter Mcßride, then Min-
ister for Mines, grabbed half of the original block
for the Crown, and left the rest to Private Enter-
prise, giving the latter a chance to show whether it
could boot the State mine out. A trifle of £48,000
has been spent during the fight, and the State mine
is still top-dog. The first bad lead of the private
co. was to tunnel through. There was no excuse
for this. Now, with the water following up the
drives, the onlv hope is to sink a shaft. That means
another £5OOO. But even now the fatmen aren’t at
all sure they have struck a mine, so at the eleventh
hour they are borrowing a drill to find out where
they are. They ought to have done that at first.
* * * *
Manager Miller, of the doomed Long Tun-
nel (Vic.), is getting a good deal of sympathy. It
is a deadly job trying to keep life in a tottering,
gasping old mine. The trouble in Miller’s case was
all the worse because he had only a very small mining
population to draw on. When this one mine went,
the whole thing went. Naturally, therefore, a good
many men didn’t wait for the end. There was plenty
of work offering at big prices in other places, and
they cleared off to get it—and usuallv it was the
men who felt surest of getting a job elsewhere who
went. This naturally didn’t improve local condi-
tions or make it easier for the tottering old con-
cern to pull through.
* * * *
Mathinna (Tas.) is in the throes of a
gold discovery. Assays have run up to close on 60oz.
of gold per ton. The find was made on the Old Jubi-
lee, a mine worked some years ago. The main shaft
is 300 ft. deep, but the recent discoveries were made
at the surface and at the 60ft. level. _ Government
Geologist Twelvetrees thinks that the line of reef is
a continuation of the Golden Gate, and that the
crosscut at the 260 ft. level must have passed through
the reef without recognising it.
* * * *
“Waihi,” answering a correspondent who
asked which mine had the biggest lift-pump in the
Commonwealth, the Waihi or the Tasmania, says that
the Waihi has a 28in. Hathorn Davey, while the Tas-
mania has a 24in. machine of the same Cornish type.
» * * *
“Vigilans” : Dr. W. G. Woolnough’s report
on the geology of the Northern Territory is a heart-
breaking account of chances thrown away. Says
Woolnough: “The richest shoots of ore at Pine Creek,
the Union and other auriferous fields have been
scooped out. The poorer but still valuable material
has been left without proper support, so that it would
be expensive, if not impossible, to. extract it. Some
of the excavations made by the Chinese had about as
much symmetry as a rabbit warren.... Nowhere are
the evils more apparent than around Pine Creek.
The reefs have been worked without the slightest
attempt at system.... Mount Diamond is another of
those depressing places where thousands of pounds
worth of mining machinery is standing idle, although
report states that when work ceased the ore values
were most promising. At Brock’s Creek there has been
considerable development, but, as in most parts, the
mining is confined to a few Chinamen. The Cosmo-
politan, Howley, is another depressing spot to visit.
It has been the scene of vigorous mining industry m
the past but is now almost completely deserted.
The Chinese have been the curse" of Northern Ter-
ritory mining. A Chinaman rarely does any dead
work • he just follows rich shoots and veins. Fields
such as Wandi, Driffield, Union, Pine Creek and
Brock’s Creek have been ruined by this pot-hole min-
ing. The reefs are now almost imlocatable, and you
must get right away from the old workings to do any
safe mining. And it is the same at Bynoe Harbor
and the West Arm tinfields. The system of tribute
in vogue in the Territory for over 30 years was also
vile. There was no supervision over the Mongols.
They were allowed to pig-root the country as they
liked And everyone exacted toll from John —-“com-
shaw!” it was called. If it were virgin country to-
day, what a boom there would be!
* * * *
“W.N.” : The Commonwealth’s Insurance
Act ought to have something to say about the rate
of interest charged to policyholders for the use of
their own money. As much as 8 p.c. is charged by
some offices on loans against the security of policies.
The rate chargeable for such loans, or in respect of
overdue premiums should be fixed by law at not
more than (say) 1 p.c. above the average rate of in-
terest earned by the investments of the office.
Last year was rather a lean one for min-
ing finance concerns, and the London, Australian and
General Exploration, Ltd., had its reverses like the
rest. Nevertheless, chairman Moreing cheered share-
holders up at the annual meeting. About the hither-
to unprofitable Burma mines he took a hopeful view,
while as regards the co.’s big interest in Lancefield
(W.A.), he said that there were 300,000 tons of
Bdwt. ore in sight ready for extraction. The problem
of treatment, he added, had been solved, and he was
convinced that profits would soon be earned. The
remarks would be much more comforting if they were
not so beastly familiar.
The Jupiter shaft, on the Rand, is now
5040 ft. deep, and is being followed by a number
of other shafts which are already below 4000 ft.
At 8000 ft. it is calculated that the temperature will
be 102.35 degrees, an extra one degree being added
for each 250 ft.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, February 3, 1913.
My Deai: Arini. —
The old tradition that made the outgoing
Excellency pass the incoming Cocked Hat on
mid-seas, or some other unsuitable place for
a confidential parley, is breaking down.
Governor Chelmsford is timed to meet Sir
Gerald Strickland in Melbourne, or there-
abouts, early in March. With the discreet
Victor Hood at his elbow, the newcomer
won’t need many wrinkles from his
predecessor. Lady Chelmsford, however,
can put Lady Edeline up to the Right Way
of giving large donations of Good Advice,
and the correct manner of suppressing com-
mittee ladies. A few mutineers in the National
Council have writhed under the autocratic
rule of Lady Chelmsford, and the peace-
makers often had as lively a time as the
Conference which tried to stroke Turkey the
right way.
Archbishop Wright and his black-eyed
lady are making a swift departure for Eng-
land. “Church business” is the spur that
goads his Gaiters to leave his Australian
flock for six months or so. We can only
pray fervently that handing round the cleri-
cal hat in John Bull’s already over-taxed
island isn’t part of the mission to London.
The everlasting Cadge abroad is a bad ad-
vertisement for our prosperous continent.
His Gaiters, looking very sleek and well-
groomed, made a stately entrance into a
leather-bag and trunk shop the other morn-
■ ing ; so I suppose his silk stockings and lawn
draperies were about to be packed for
the voyage. I wonder if a heavy-weight
Archbishop ever sits on the head of a cabin
trunk when it is too full for words, or does
he hire a mere verger to do it for him?
Henley-on-Lane-Cove was rather worried
by a squally wind on Saturday evening.
When the dusk first began to fall upon the
river, the lighting of the lanterns exhausted
large supplies of matches. Nothing, it
seemed, less than a whole factory would
have kept one set of bobbing lanterns aglow.
Then the wind calmed down, and glorified
gardens gleamed with swinging balls of
light beyond the Lane Cove bridge and
down to the water’s edge. Patches of re-
claimed river, turned into grassy lawns by
sods and manual labor, made bold outposts
of illuminated color. A dim red haze from
bush fires formed a handsome backcloth for
a charming stage picture.
The carnival got much of its decorative
effect from the small and saucy row-boats
that rolled along under much top-hamper of
dancing Chinese lanterns. Mr. E. Bennett’s
big house had a clever scheme of lights—
stowed away in deep balconies and tossing
amongst surprised birds in the garden. The
Pasquils’ lawn, stolen from the river by the
ingenuity of man. was aflame with crimson
fire-flies- —probably mere lanterns that flung
long lines of dancing light into the dark
water below. Captain Wright’s nest was a
grand fandango of red and orange. And
every little bungalow kept its candle-ends
up, and strewed the earth with matches to
help the demonstration.
J. C. Williamson, a little slimmer than he
used to be, strolled into his Sydney office the
other afternoon on his return from foreign
climes. Henry Bracy was waiting on the
doorstep, and a stray head or two peered
round an adjacent door. Otherwise there
was nothing to show that J.C.W., who hates
a fuss, had been more than an hour away
from his official den. He brings back his
usual careful habit of giving no important
information to the press in the most attrac-
tive interviews.
J. C. Williamson broke his journey at Cey-
lon, and was the guest for a week of the Gar-
ricks, a few miles out from Kandy. Mrs.
Garrick used to be Rose Musgrove. Her
husband is reputed to be one of the wealthiest
tea and rubber planters in Ceylon, and their
homestead is run on lavish lines. The front
name of David has been plunked on to the
three-and-a-half-months-old heir to all the
tea and rubber. It poured rain for the
seven days J.C.W. stayed with them, but the
big man declares the week to have been one
of the happiest holidays of his life.
A slim white wedding card has come this
way. It has a decorative D and H on its
demure outside. Silver letters in its interior
convey the compliments of Mr. and Mrs.
August Hermann. She was Lillian Delany.
daughter of the late John Delany, one of
Sydney’s most accomplished musicians. The
wedding occurred in Berlin on December 21.
The summer sale is the chief thrill of the
moment. There are sales and sales, of
course; but, in any case, the struggle to buy
something you don’t want, because its price
has been chopped across the middle with a
blue pencil, seems to be worth twopence
halfpenny as a recreation. Mountains of
white gowns are more or less given away,
says a shopping enthusiast, and the summer
sales are genuine price-reductions; hut you
must be waiting on the shop mat when the
doors open if you want to get near the bar-
gain of your dreams.
For some weeks vivid purple millinery has
surmounted the summer tan of the outdoor
girl’s warm-weather complexion. A vista
of the afternoon shopping area in Pitt-street
looks like a raging epidemic of purple fever
stamping about the congested pavements.
But the last state of the Hat is worse than
the first. Its purple crown is now top-
dressed with blazing scarlet bows, and the
combined glare goes to the head of the
passer-by like a whiff from Sheol.
Winthrop Hackett, of the West Aus-
tralian, dropped into Sydney the other day.
He has a unique record for a newspaper
man: he was elected to the Legislative Coun-
cil of his State at last elections, and has
thus sat continuously since that body was
created in 1890. He is an M.A. of Trinity,
Dublin, and was chosen as first Chancellor
when his State set up a University the other
day. Years and years ago the offer of a
knighthood came his way, and was declined.
Then he married, and—well, the offer wasn’t
declined when it was made again.
_ A. B. Triggs, the large sheepman, who has
his headquarters at Linton (Yass), reached
the 45th milestone this week, and various
men of wool and finance lunched him to cele-
brate it. Later on the same day a quite-
family party said “Many happy returns” at
the Pacific, Manly. A. B. Triggs was a
bank clerk at Yass when he made his first
sheep deal at the tail of a drought, backing
an opinion formed on a long study of wea-
ther facts. The first deal made good, and
now he specialises in weather. The most vital
of his many business departments is his
weather office, where Australian rainfalls
are collated month by month. His big
success has been built on this knowledge of
general rainfall and weather averages, good
selection of lieutenants and pluck. He has
grass and sheep in many places, and when
the grass goes off duty in one region he can
shift the sheep to another. And buyers
trust him, purchasing hundreds of thou-
sands of pounds’ worth of stock on the re-
ports of his own inspectors. He is a man
of many addresses —among them Wirch-
illeba, Tara, Moonagee, Merri Merrigal,
Yarrandala, Willieploma, Wee Jasper, Long-
plain, Currangorambla, Uabba, North Mer-
owie, North Whoey and Ballandry, If the
poor aboriginal did little else, it helped Mr.
Triggs to names for the properties which
are the outward and visible signs of his
phenomenal success.
The Rollestons’ departure occurs in a
week or two. Captain Rolleston has been
the naval man behind the gun at Garden
Island for about four years. He belongs to
the old school of British bluejacket, and is
suspected of shying at anything Australian
with the same fervor as sheep-farmer A. A.
Dangar and other mutton magnates.
Ladies excepted, of course, for Mrs. Rolles-
ton is Australian born.
Mrs. George O’Regan, who died the other
day in Forbes-street, Darlinghurst, where
she had lived for 55 years, was an interest-
ing link with old Sydney. A sister of the
late J. P. Garvan, who founded the Citizens’
Life Assurance Co., the late Mrs. O’Regan
remembered the Sydney Domain when the
blacks frequented it. Her stock of old-time
memories included the wreck of the Dun-
bar. Going down t<- the Gap with her hus-
band very early on the morning after the
wreck, she saw the sole survivor hauled up
over the cliffs. A woman of bright person-
ality and benevolent disposition, the late Mrs.
O’Regan, who was a native of Limerick,
had a wide circle pf friends in this town.
One of Sydney’s best-known business
men, James Stedman, sen., passed out in
Hobart last Saturday. Appendicitis was
the first cause. Mr. Stedman has left a
large reputation for good deeds in the cause
of charity. I don’t know how long he had
been in business in Sydney; the Oldest In-
habitant can scarcelv remember when the
Stedman signboard first went up. He had
spent all his years beside the Harbor—was
born in Parramatta, learned his business in
Sydney (his start was where the Strand
now is, in a confectioner’s shop in George-
street), and for many years past his home
had been in Manly.
A Bulletin reader drops in a line to
mirror the shining virtues of the Australian
girl. “A recent accident on 1 the Orient
Wharf, Circular Quay, showed 'the Sydney
girl at her best. A tramguard was render-
ing first-aid to a man who had just been in-
jured. —leg broken, I think. The girl, who
was in a motor near the wharf, jumped out
and ran up to offer her help and the use of
the car. Then she went aboard the Orient boat
and returned with the doctor.. A few min-
utes later she drove patient and doctor off
to Sydney Hospital. Incidentally, I got a
tip from the doctor. ‘Use old newspapers
rolled up for temporary splints,’ he said.”
Pianist Lawrence G. Smith sent a few lines
to a friend in this city to say he’s steering
his course for Australian waters. For three
or four months Lawrence G. has been camped
on Jack London’s ranch, somewhere beyond
the wilds of ’Frisco. At date of writing, he
was on a cruise in the Londons’ yacht.
Organising sec. Grace Burrows( of the
Girls’ Realm, has hurried back to town after
a holiday at Wagstaff Point. In this marine
resort the finny tribe are said to dodge the
elusive hook with some success, unless a
veteran fisherman is out for business. Miss
Burrows has been impounded by the Blue
Mountains’ Centenary Crossing Committee
as general organiser of the May celebrations.
Tenor Sid Macdonald took his voice off to
Melbourne the other day for a well-earned
rest.
F. Barron Morley, a Sydney pianist who
has been abroad for about 10 years, threatens
to revisit his native water-front in May. He
lives mostly in Munich, with occasional
digressions to Berlin and Vienna.
Barron Morley, by the way, now clings
to his front name, and is known in Munich
as Frederick B. Morley. He found the Bar-
ron rather top-heavy in Germany, where he
was mistaken for one of the cerulean blue
breed of barons of the hereditary species.
The recently-returned organist Lilian
Frost didn’t add any weight to her long,
willow-wand figure in the 12 months she
spent on the track of foreign music. She
brushed up her practice under the Parisian
organist Widor, and nearly worked her feet
off on British pedals. The luncheon-hour
organ recital is a feature of modern London
life. It doesn’t accompany the midday
prayer, but is usually on its own, a merely
secular help to the one o’clock bun. Miss
Frost has returned to her Sunday perch in
front of the Congregational organ in Pitt-
street.
A year or so ago a Sydney girl named Per-
driau made an unostentatious departure for
the other side. She took a pleasing voice to
the studio of the famous Albani, and sat
down to scales and similar solid diet for 12
months. She returned here in the Orvieto
as noiselessly as she departed. The fact that
Albani should be teaching for a crust is
another glaring example of the old proverb —
the want of money is the root of all trouble.
Albani must have made tons of cash in her
day, though her day was rather before the
glittering fortunes that pursue the modern
prima donna —on paper or in fact.
The youthful Leah Myers was married to
one Roschmidt, in Paddington, the other day.
He’s with a well-known business firm as a
buyer or something of the kind. There were
no frills, and the honeymooners slipped
quietly off to the western highlands after the
ceremony. Whether Leah will lift her tuneful
voice on the concert platform again, or
merely warble to her household gods, is not
mentioned for publication.
Violinist Cyril Monk remarks in joyful
italics that he has just prevailed on Miss
Petersen to accept an engagement ring. She’s
a bright Australian girl, and like Monk him-
self, _ an ex-pupil of Sydney’s wonderful old
music-master Kretschmann. As “Isabel
Varney” she has made her presence felt as a
composer of songs.
Miss Toohey, of the Wahroonga branch
of the clan, went off to Europe the other
day. _ Her chaperon is Mrs. Douglas Mac-
kenzie, the military lady of the massive
jaw.
The Gaby Glide, which Jessie Lonnen
leads to encores in “The Sunshine Girl,”
appears to be an American negro version of
the old Grecian bend —an affliction which
smote English society in the earlier days of
Queen Alexandra’s Princess of Walesdom.
There’s some 19th century legend of the
beauteous Alex, having sciatica or some-
thing that made her limp for a time. Of
course the Court went dead lame, too, and the
Grecian bend (wny Grecian, by the way?)
gave the caricaturist lots of joy. The young
man who started this Gaby freak-hop in
Noo Yark must have seen a larky negress
carry home his washing. Even a stage
nigger always lets himself go at the knees,
and keeps on bumping the air behind to give
the impression of a bustle on the move. The
Gaby Glide is nearly all bustle, and I expect
the local parson will go to see it in order
to say how often it ought to be smacked for
its frivolous deportment.
The. Montessori method of injecting arith-
metic into the infantile cranium hasn’t come
along too soon. At a big State school the
other morning a very young class was
wrestling with a new sum. The teacher
observed the smallest scholar vigorously
writing a pencilled note, a suspicious thing
in school hours. “Give me that note,” says
the lady pedagogue. The youngster hesi-
tated, then laid her small crime on the altar
of authority. It was a mixture of Faith
and Works, and contained this line, “Pleas,
God, help me do this som” !
The Sydney Grammar School Old Boys’
Union has issued a stack of cards for a
smoke concert on February 7. Some of the
old boys have been grown up for 30 years or
so, but they mean to give a frolicsome wel-
come to the new headmaster, H. N. Sloman.
He is not ancient enough to have had a vote
fo~ very long, being 28 years young. He took
to himself a wife just before leaving Eng-
land. The smoked music will waft him into
his new scholastic den in College-street.
Another good old superstition has re-
cently died hard in the vicinity of a batch
of bush-fires in the Campbelltown district.
A travelled Hayseed lady returned home
with a plucking of peacock’s feathers amongst
other trophies. A few local acquaintances
admired and accepted small samples of the
gorgeous fowl’s tail. Others rejected them
with uplifted hands as unlucky. Then
the fires came along and carelessly avoided
the property of the settlers who recklessly
gave house-room to peacocks’ plumage, while,
by an odd coincidence, everyone who had re-
fused the feathers of ill-omen was more or
less scorched by the fire-fiend.
Golf is keeping fairly quiet till the first
week in April, when the caddy will return
in full force to his pedestrian sprint as
A.D.C. to goffers. At Kensington links
there was some mild pursuit of the white
ball on Saturday afternoon, and two Petti-
coats beat two male goffers with some
slaughter. This new digression in mixed
foursomes has inspired the vanquished to
arrange a terrible revenge later on.
The Richard Arthurs (Mosman) have let
their roof-tree, and are going off to Manly
for a surfing campaign. Manly, by the way
is “the Village” where two-thirds of the
male population seem to be hatless, collar-
less and always accompanied by a towel
There never before was such a rush to the
place as there has been this season. At least
one ocean beach house has booked up every
room on the premises until far into April •
and there are probably many more in the
same bulging condition, in spite of fancy
tariffs for sea-front rooms.
The Cecil Darleys, who live mostly in
England, have once more packed up their
chattels for the Land of Freetrade and low
wages.
Re Britannia’s free-food tariff, Lord
Brassey has again plunged into ink. This
time it’s a revised edition of his original
sin, which was a Freetrade pamphlet pub-
lished in 1904. The present volume is
called “Sixty Years of Fiscal Policy.”
When Brassey was Governor of Victoria he
had a careless habit of falling off things,
but he contrives to keep an unsteady seat
on a quaking stack of Freetrade statistics.
Naturally, he bumps against a preferential
tariff between the grey old Mother and her
overseas daughters. If the wealthy old con-
tractor could only fall off the peerage for a
few months, and sample the business of
working-man in a British factory, he might
change his mind about the glory of the
open-door.
The Austral Club (London) is going to
be swallowed, with its own consent, by a
Dominions Club, with larger premises and a
keen hunger for overseas members. Lord
Strathcona will be president of the new
club’s council.
A living replica of the Venus de Milo has
been found among the 400 maidens in a
U.S. girls’ college. She is sft. Tin. in height,
has a chest measurement of 34in. and a
waist of 30in., and weighs 12st. Her tastes
tend to beefsteak (underdone, I presume),
cold water and fresh air. She keeps off tea,
coffee and candy. Details concerning the
number of American millionaires who will
lay their impaired digestions and motor-cars
at the lady’s substantial feet will probably
be head-lined in the U.S. press later on.
Another polite slap in the face to our
sex! An enterprising Italian Miss, named
Teresa Labriola, is a doctor of laws and
professor of philosophy in the University of
Rome. So far, however, and no farther
shall she go her legal way. The Supreme
Court of Rome has firmly remarked that
women do not possess the right to be advo-
cates. So Teresa is sitting back to get her
breath, and may be wondering whose ears
she has the legal right to box for scratching
her name off the rolls before she had time
to gum it on, so to speak.
Yours affectionately,
Vandorian.
“Captain Jack” Evans, who has been one
of the fighters in the Tasmanian general elec-
tions, was four years old when he came to
Tasmania with his father, who was also a
skipper. In 1870 a whole fleet of tea ships
were sent to China and Japan from Tas-
mania. G. M. Evans was in charge of the
clipper Helen, and he had his son J. W.
(Jack) with him. The Helen beat the
others, and its cargo w r as all over Tasmania
before the others blew in. When the little
Evans grew as big as he could, which was
never much, he became a skipper too, and in
that capacity witnessed a tragedy performed
in 15 minutes. He had command of the
Tasma in 1883, and on a run from Sydney
to Hobart was having a cup of coffee when
the Hippolite rock was reported. They
were right on it, though it was broad day-
light and the day was fine. There were six
souls and the skipper aboard. A boat was
lowered and the lot landed at an adjacent
bay. The little skipper was the last to go,
and before his eyes the Tasma went down —
with 40 bullocks, among other things. Evans
reached Hobart that trip on the anniversary
of his birthday. After a while he took to
’long-shore_ jobs and entered the Tasmanian
Assembly in 1896 for Kingsborough, a sea-
side Hobart constituency and he has Deen a
pretty brisk member ever since.
Crosbie Ward, humorist and politician of
early Canterbury (M.L.), had a few ideas
that might have helped the Shickery
Islands a lot if they had been allowed to
breed and multiply. Even to-day a political
party might do worse than pin one of his
old verses in its hat. It occurs in a parody
of Byron’s “Isles of Greece,” written in
1859: —
Trust not for money to the banks!
They make a charge of 10 per cent.
Your land, which owes no stranger thanks
Is money—more than can be spent.
Crosbie Ward kept the early settlement
alive by his contributions to Canterbury
Punch, and was a member of Fox’s Minis-
try, and afterwards of Domett’s. In 1856
he became part-owner of the Lyttelton
Times.
