MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, February 3, ±913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The fresh atmosphere of Upper Macedon
was tainted by petrol fumes when all the
Most Expensive People who were not on the
elevated spot oiled furiously to the Fullers’
garden party. Here and there along the
route a low-hanging branch swashed des-
tructively at a wind-blown plume or light
scarf; but everyone managed to escape
serious damage. His Ex. sported his gay-
est air, and struggled through the handshak-
ing and small talk without anaesthetics, his
smiling lady merely lending him moral sup-
port. Auntie Phipps, Help Curwen and
Secretary Wilberforce fussed gracefully in
the _ vicinity. Norman Brookes, unaccom-
panied by his wife, chaperoned the British
tennis players.
The Japanese invasion continues. From
the two grey cruisers, lying snugly at Port
Melbourne Town Pier, throngs of small,
solemn, brown men stream ashore like
strings of inquisitive ants. They do not
wander aimlessly about the city like most
visiting men-of-warsmen, but penetrate to
all sorts of places that offer no apparent
attraction for mere gazers. One particularly
keen specimeh was detected peering at a
boiling-down establishment from behind a
barrel. They have inspected the sewerage
system, and taken copious notes about dust-
carts, and one was heard to ask a waitress
what she put on her face. The way of
Japan with its visitor is different. A Mel-
bourne girl remembers a dark time when
the descriptive letters from her fiancS in
Japan stopped short. Every day the young
man used to scribble some notes about the
places he had visited. After a little of this,
the Japanese postal authorities became in-
terested, and, without warning, quietly im-
pounded everything as he dropped it into the
post.
There was some astonishment when a
group of the neatly uniformed and deadly
serious caJets presented themselves to the
lift man in the Gas Co.’s building. “We
wish to see. over the premises, please,” they
remarked in a business-like way. The
startled official looked at them hard, but
eventually decided that it wouldn’t do any
harm to give the unconscious humorists a
rise. The cage swallowed the lot, and it
floated up like a departing spirit toward the
top floor and the quarters of the fashionable
Alexandra Club. I haven’t heard what hap-
pened when they arrived on the mat, but it
is quite likely they were received with tea-
cups and applause.
On mail day swarms of the brown cadets
impede the traffic in the vicinity of the
G.P.O. They wait patiently until any sort
of official emerges; then they pour letters
into his arms, smiling meanwhile in a kindly
Asiatic way as if to say, “A letter from my
unworthy self to my degraded grandmother.
Ennoble me by posting it in honorable right
box.”
The men of the Belgian training ship gave
a farewell party last week at the St. Kilda
Town Hall. It was to have been held on
the vessel; but some afternoon-tea visitors
had endured such a rough time on the pre-
vious day that considerate Commander Cor-
neille converted the Friday evening func-
tion into a shore revel. The Mayoral Hen-
nessys were late, but eventually arrived.
(The lady’s heron feather head-dress might
have been the cause of the delay.) When
they had been welcomed, the organ boomed
an overture to a musical programme, and
the' cadets sang their national anthem with
an accompaniment of more organ. (The
Hennessys by this time must have got over
any regrets they may have had about leav-
ing their own vast organ at home.) Alice
Heeley, of the Sheffield Choir, stepped into
Napoleon Boffard’s place and intoned “The
Lost . Chord,” the organ thundering and
growling in the background. Mrs. Jansen
sang a favorite song of the bird-like Blanche
Arral in a deep contralto, and three cadets
got. through an Ave Maria. Then somebody
noticed that the programme was weighing
heavily, and the boys chanted an Opera
House ditty in broken Australian. The
guests laughed until the big hall echoed and
the dead weight of the organ was forgotten.
There was a fluttering, hair-bowed army of
dappers present, and directly the cadets‘had
pt through their hosting duties, they slipped
into chairs near their most adored girls and
sentimentalised. At supper time, all the
bewildering corners of the rambling hall and
the adjoining gardens were filled with fare-
well murmurs, until the boys were rounded
un by their tutor, a grim, grey-haired man
who seems to speak French with an Ameri-
can twang.
Chief Justice Parker, of Westralia. is
being dined nightly by all the Right
People in turn. His eldest daughter and a
grandson called Patsy are with him, but not,
so to speak, at his learned heels. They are
rusticating in the country, while he sacri-
fices his digestion on the wooden altar of
hospitality.
On February 11, the day the Morea sails,
Melbourne will lose a fine batch of spinsters.
All save one are furnished with return
tickets. Auntie Phipps is the lone exception.
After a halt in India, she will journey on to
England. Miss Graham, with niece Edith
Haynes, is off to London by the same ship,
whilst two Power girls and Rita Kiddle will
get off when the gangway is let down at
Colombo.
Dr. Arthur Brebner, with whom London is
acquainted as a journalist and novelist (his
latest work or job is titled “Patches and
Pomander”), is visiting here with his wife.
The lady is a daughter of Dr. Steven, of the
A.M.P. Society. The scribbling doctor, by
the way, claims acquaintance with numerous
Royal foreigners, including Karageorgevitch,
the gentleman who put on the crown of his
butchered Servian cousin. Knowing Karaetc,
I guess, must be rather like knowing the
Kelly Gang.
Surgeon Douglas Shields, who departed
last week to take permanent charge of a
fashionable women’s hospital in London, had
three fine aids to help float his reputation.
They were (i) manner, (2) a vice-regal
patient, and (3) brains. Please note that
brains are placed third on a foolish
worldly valuation. Manner is always
the first thing looked for. It is generally
believed that Shields got his from Heaven.
The Dudley lady’s illness at sea gave the
little medico a chance to earn the needful
big advertisment. And he did not miss it.
Mrs. Shields, who once helped to heal the
sick, will be missed verv much by her circle.
She shunned what is called Society, pre-
ferring the quiet, sane life and a small circle
of real Mends.
Chinaman Chia’s little white bride is hav-
ing a sick honeymoon. Some severe throat
trouble precluded any thought of departure
from Australia, and now she is in hospital
until such time as she can get away to the
hills to convalesce. The poor girl is fret-
ting for her mother, so the trip to distant
China is “off” for the time being.
Grand-opera Quinlan, after many years of
wedded life, has just been presented with his
firstborn. Noel Thomas Quinlan is the
infant’s name, and he is an Australian by
birth. The cable to father announcing his
arrival crossed another from father joyfully
reporting a record success by the co. in
Dublin.
Helen Sousa, the tall, slight girl with the
black hair and the chalk-white face, who
was out here with popper, mommer and the
sparkling band, has discarded the melodi-
ously famous name, and become Mrs. Hamil-
ton Albert, of New York.
The Hodges, Maddens, Powers and Fisk-
ens gathered in force at Mrs. ]Fred Keats’s
send-off to Mrs. Hussey Chomlby. (By the
way, the Chomleys drop all the English
trimmings of their name on ordinary work-
ing occasions.) The tall Miss Baird, who
was to have travelled to London with the
departing lady, has given up the trip, in
order to stay at home and comfort her sister,
Mrs. Goode, whose husband died suddenly
at Warrnambool.
The Mornington Woinarski family have
scarcely had time to straighten up the house
and sweep up the confetti of the last wed-
ding ; and now another orange blossoming
is threatened. Nell Zichy-Woinarski is the
one who has brought it on them, and Dr.
Arthur Nankivell is her helpmate. The date
is Tuesday, February 11.
Dr. Arthur Nankivell practises at Cohuna,
whither he will bear away his bride. He
is a grandson of the late City Coroner
Youl, the quaint old identity whose
last acts on earth were to drink a parting
glass of fizz and nod good-bye to the relatives
seated around his armchair. Strange that
no artist has ever thought of painting a
pleasantly dramatic death-scene on Youl-
tide lines.
A marriage is impending between Stella
Robertson, daughter of the great Mac., of
confectionery fame, and young Bert Brewer,
who was her “sweetheart when a boy,” and
is a neighbor of the Robertsons at Fairfield.
“Bobby” Bruce and Miss Jackson have
decided against any elaborate setting for
their approaching marriage. A chorus of
low moans has gone up from girls who were
looking forward to the conventional fuss
and flurry. The .Jackson and Bruce families
are already allied by marriage; Dr. Jack-
son’s eldest daughter married one of the
Bruces two or three years ago.
There was a gay, good-looking gathering
at the Paris Cafe the other Wednesday
afternoon to celebrate the wedding of Phylli's
Miller and Upton Brown. Prior Kind'elan
had only tied the nuptial knot an hour pre-
viously, but the bride had quite recovered
from the ordeal ere the reception began. Her
dainty trainless gown was of palest pink
trimmed with Limerick lace. Sister Molly
and Eily Miller looked like two blushes in
white ninon over pink satin. Impertinently-
tilted black velvet Beefeater hats, with pink
ostrich feathers, went with the delicious
dresses. The third bridesmaid—kiddie Jean
Dunstan —frisked round in a mob cap. The
bride’s mother sported a white Brussels lace
dress over black and a feathered black hat.
“Rosna” writes from London:—
There was a wedding of much frill and circum-
stance at the Guards’ Chapel on December 21 when
Maude, daughter of the W. H. Turners, of Westralia
was joined to Sir John Swinnerton Dyer, of “the
Scots.” The regiment turned out for decorative pur-
poses, and the regimental band provided what were
alleged to be appropriate noises. There were 10
bridesmaidens —five small girls in maize-colored
ninon frocks, with autumn-tinted wreaths in their
hair; five large girls, in maize-colored charmeuse,
edged with fur, and brown velvet hats wreathed with
ostrich plumes. They all carried sheaves of bronze
chrysanthemums and autumn leaves. Lord Desmond
Fitzgerald upheld the bridegroom, and Admiral Paget
stood beside the bride —a cream-satin-and-Malines-
lace bride —to see fair play. The reception was held
at Claridge’s, and the great invited included half
a column of Peerage and another of Westralians,
including Forrests, Wittenooms, Leakes, Steeres,
(Bishop) Rileys, Irwins, and I don’t know who.
Pianist Una Bourne was gladdened on her
way to England by the news that Melba had
engaged her for two or three concerts, which
is practically a guarantee that Una will be
fairly in the swim during the next London
season. The cablegram met her at Colombo.
Mrs. Chinn, from Westralia, is at present
in Melbourne. She has been so distressed of
late by the unkind things said of hubby that
one of Melbourne’s cleverest oculists is en-
gaged trying to restore brightness to her
pretty eyes. The Chinns and the Justice
Hood family are connected by marriage,
Mrs. Hood having once been Miss Chinn.
Mr. Pleydell, a nephew of George
Edwardes, of London, has been making ex-
pensive fun for the panto girls. He is a tall
young man who isn’t nearly as serious as he
looks. He also wears a monocle —or a mon-
ocle wears him, I forget which. Before de-
parting from Melbourne, he gave a farewell
supper at the Savoy to the principal star
frivollers. The room was festooned with ivy
in honor of the only Schilling, while Violet
Loraine was remembered with violets —arti-
ficial only because the real blooms are out
of season. Captain Rutherford, a cheery
young man from Singapore, assisted at the
revel, and the panto, orchestra made music
after the theatre had shut up for the night.
It was about five o’clock in the morning
when the farewell ended.
By the way, talking of revels, a cheery
youth turned up the other night at a Col-
lins-street hostelry holding in his arms a
dead duck. On attempts being made to
separate him from the corpse he wept bit-
terly. In the best circles, it is rumored,
deceased poultry threatens to take the place
that has so long been held by lobsters in the
hearts of wandering inebriates.
Mrs. Oliphant Shaw’s frivol at Terang
came up to the guests’ high expectations,
despite the fact that a number of young
couples wore a subdued and reverent “We
are engaged!” expression. Broken Hill
Knox’s second son and his fiancee, Miss
McArthur, walked together, a good example
which was closely followed by Willie Mani-
fold and his Beatrice Grey-Smith. The
Manifold girl-cousin, who lately gave her
promise to a sailor, looked rather lonely and
envious all by herself, her mariner being
away. She was consoled, however, by the
thought that he is making his last trip.
Father considers that life on the ocean wave
is too uncertain for his son-in-law, so he is
to shortly turn him into a landsman with a
fine block of Manifold country and a com-
fortable house.
A rabble of invited folks straggled round
the Exhibition on Thursday afternoon, in
search of a tea and bunning given by Mrs.
Robert Harper, as president of the Kinder-
garten League. They finally discovered the
feast set out behind the kitchen, with a
hostess clad in black and white in waiting.
Mrs. T. aßeckett was a pleasing sight in
blue; and Miss Henderson, of the white
hair, looked out from under a becoming hat.
The Burstons and the Robert Garrans took
an active interest in the proceedings, which
included, besides buns and tannin, some in-
teresting games by the youngsters under the
guidance of Miss Luck. Dr. Edith Barrett
headed an approving group of medicine
women.
Last Saturday the Brighton yacht turn-
out started with a torrid north wind, which
induced the flock of feminine watchers to
venture forth in cool and fresh-looking mus-
lins and linens. But when they were all
enjoying _ themselves, the treacherous wind
did a quick-change act, swung round to the
west, and charged down at full gallop. It
staggered the racing boats, upset a dinghy
or so, and whistled coldly round the whited
spectators. Chilled and ruffled women pre-
sently began to desert in herds, and there
was a pathetic show of white legs and scud-
ding shoes. The Club people sheltered those
who could commandeer coats; but most of
the girls fled and left the yachts with wetted
sails butting like desperate marine goats
through the racing seas. Port Phillip seems
to specialise in these unpleasant transfor-
mations.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Foundation Day, the beastliest possible weather
and the A.R.C. Races all came simultaneously. The
distinguishing features of the weather were a tem-
perature of 110°, and a “brave nor’-easter,” as the mis-
guided Kingsley termed it, which hurled dust, pebbles,
bricks and boulders in all directions. In the inter-
vals of dodging wind-flung portions of the Victoria
Park Racecourse, the crowd watched Mr. O Smith’s
Summerfield win the Burnside Handicap. He paid
his backers £ll9 Bs. for £l. Immediately after
the race punters began kicking themselves for not
having taken the winner’s name and the day as an
“omen.” For all I know they are at the same
unprofitable practice still. The race paid three divi-
dends, Uanna and Eager making a dead-heat for
second place. If the number of starters had not
warranted the second dividend, Summerfield’s money
would have been about £l5O for £l. The totalisa-
tor returns for the day are setting racing men talking
of bigger prize money; £ 20,000-odd is a fair in-
vestment for one day’s sport. Mr. R. M Hawker
secured the Turretfield Stakes with Tabangai, and a
second in the Tramway Trial with Rallina.

POLITICAL
POINTS
In proposing the toast of “The Guests” at
the luncheon to the officers of the Japanese
squadron, Labor-leader and Prime Minister
Fisher declared that “he always advocated
the interchange of people and ideas.” Natur-
ally the Admiral replied that such a wel-
come had made a “deep and lasting impres-
sion on our hearts and minds.” It was all
very pretty and Sunday-schooly. but how the
Jap tongue must have strained the muscles
of Asia’s placid cheek! Still, the beautiful
sham is conducted in the name of Empire
and the British flag, and some other matters
which bulk largely in the morning papers,
and there are people in Australia, as the
Asiatic knows, who mistake such things for
holy friendship. That, no doubt, is one of
the reasons why the Japanese training squad-
ron is so often sent to these waters.
BON VOYAGE!
Melbourne Harbor Trust, some months
ago, imported a dredge from Holland, because
the long-headed Commissioners reckoned it
was better for the country to send 2d. out of
it than to keep 3d. in it. The unwieldy craft
staggered out to Australia, and had such a
bad time that, at one part of the voyage, the
sidelights were rolled right under. One of
the unfortunate crew likened it at this stage
to a half-tide rock. However, unlike the
dredge Manchester, which tried to cross from
Maoriland and was lost with all hands, it
managed to wallow to its destination, and
was duly put to work by its proud owners.
But, unfortunately, the brilliant economists
had forgotten to also order mud barges; so
the precious importation is standing idle half
its time for lack of anything to empty its dirt
into. Perhaps the Commissioners could help
by eating a little; they’ve fairly earned it.
No wonder Works Minister Edgar (Vic.)
used strong language the other day about
the multiplication of tinpot shires, and
Bumble’s consequent wastefulness. In Ray-
wood 137 ratepayers elect nine councillors.
Every fifteenth person on the roll is a coun-
cillor. In eight other shires the proportion
of dignitaries ranges from l-17th to l-28th
of the entire hayseed crowd. You can hardly
chuck a turnip at the population without
hitting a councillor.
Wienliolt, M.L.A. for Fassifern (Q.), is
to oppose Andrew Fisher at the next Federal
election. Wienholt is a staunch old Tory
(otherwise “Liberal”) who owes much to his
family connections, his long purse and the
fact that he gives his whole Parliamentary
salary to a lucky hospital in his constitu-
ency—his own constituency, mark! His dis-
trict (Fassifern) is largely settled by Ger-
mans, many of whom vote for him in the
fond delusion that he hails from the Father-
land. The Wienholts arc, however, a family
of Welsh origin, the father having been a
railway contractor in Bananaland’s early
days.
The “front” of these municipal mendicants
is amazing. Last year the Victorian Govern-
ment granted £4OO for the erection of what
is known as Kirwin’s bridge, over the Goul-
burn. The other day the Goulburn and
Waranga shires went hat in hand to the
Minister for another £2OO towards the job.
Seeing that both shires are working on a
shilling rate, this second attempt at extor-
tion was turned down. The urban taxpayer
is dead tired of contributing to the support
of these cadging bodies. In Victoria there
are 72 shires which have persistently refused
to strike a rate higher than a shilling, _ and
most of them work on out-of-date valuations.
This year the Public Works Minister has
been allowed £40,000 for doles to municipal
mendicants, and up to now he has received
begging applications for £250,000.
“H.S.” on a matter of machinery:—
There is likely to be a row soon amongst some
N.S.W. Labor Leagues. On them has hitherto rested
the onus oi keeping the flag flying in the off season
and doing Labor’s work generally. In return for this,
as it were, they selected Parliamentary candidates,
and Labor voters have generally accepted their
choice. At the instance of the Typographical Asso-
ciation this has been altered by the conference just
concluded, and an affiliated union may now foist the
whole of its members resident in an electorate on the
local League by the short-cut method of paying 6d.
a head to the executive body, when they will imme-
diately become eligible to deal with League funds,
select candidates, and do any other odd jobs knocking
about. The possibilities for “packing” are glorious.
It may happen that McGowen, just before selection,
runs foul of (say) the wharf laborers, amongst whom
are many red-raggers. By a simple resolution of the
union, two or three hundred of them can be landed
into the Redfern League, and McGowen’s old guard
will be outvoted four to one. Good unionists are
usually in the Leagues already, and don’t wait to be
poured in. To be a member of a union means no-
thing to-day. Everyone is a “unionist” now. Mem-
bership is practically compulsory, has no significance
and carries no virtue ; but the conference apparently
thought otherwise. Some of the best “Liberals” this
writer has known were A.W.U. men, who did a little
shearing the while they were accumulating a few
sheep and making their C.P. holding freehold. Even
the typographicals, who sent along the proposal, have
had a “Liberal” president. It is claimed for the
departure that it works smoothly in Victoria. But
where are the results? Has anybody heard of a Vic.
Labor Government yet?
“Sine’s” view of the horse-doctor: —
Administrator Gi 1-
ruth has reported on
the Northern Terri-
tory, and, as was to
be expected, has taken
a vet.’s view of Aus-
tralia’s empty back-
yard. Not a word is
said about encouraging
mining or prospecting,
the only way Austra-
lia’s buffalo preserve
will be populated.
Here is a vast region
holding gold, copper,
silver, tin, wolfram,
oil, coal and rare
earths, which only
needs tracks, boring
rigs and water wells
to make it a good
mining district, and all
we can do with it is
to use it as an experi-
mental farm for rais-
ing weedy horses and
anaemic pigs. Nor does
the Administration
learn as it goes. Thus,
the mining ordinance
just formulated, regarding the mode of getting
leases to nrospect for oil, shows that neither the
Spring-street officers of the Department nor Dr. Gil-
ruth have the faintest idea how this industry should
be developed. The Territory will be a dead loss to
the country until a mining Administrator, who doesn’t
know anthrax from glanders or pig’s nmasles from
mule’s staggers, is appointed, and turns two shiploads
of Charters Towers men loose to go and look for
minerals. Mining alone will pioneer the settlement
of North Australia.
Though the eight-hour day is supposed to be
the general rule in its public services, Victoria
learnt the other morning that no less than
six suburban railway signal-boxes are worked
on the 10-hour system— i.e., 60 hours one
week, with Sunday off; the next 72 hours,
with a straight 12-hour shift on the Sab-
bath. And while it was still shivering over
the possibilities, it also learned, this time
from Willie Watt, that it would be a dread-
ful thing if the railway service was liable
to have its hours discussed by a Judge of the
High Couiwff Australia.
The first line of telegraph opened in S.A.
belonged to a private individual, Mr. James
Mac George. The wire stretched from Ade-
laide to the Port, and business over it
started on November 26, 1855. The Gov-
ernment, however, acquired it after running
in opposition for a while.
UNHEALTHY LODGINGS.
THE LITTLE CHAP: "Good heavens , Alfred , I
should hardly have known you! ”
At its recent conference the Maoriland
Federation of Labor carried a resolution an-
nouncing that on a declaration of war by
any of the Great Powers, the conference is
prepared to recommend a general strike.
That settles everything. Japan and Russia
w|ll never dream of commencing hostilities
now that they know the trade of Wanganui
will thereby be dislocated.
“Oom Bill” Lyne, stalwart ancient and
good Australian, still holds the solid respect
of the Labor party. He is allowed to call
himself by any political name he likes, and
no Labor candidate is to be run against him
for the Hume seat. It is a decent tribute
to a man who did his best in the cause of
Australian industries, and, on the whole,
did it strongly and well.
Willie Watt says that he has worried him-
self nearly bald trying to think how the Aus-
tralian Parliament can regulate prices. The
price of sugar throughout Australasia has
for years been fixed by the C.S.R., and,
instead of losing his valuable hair over it,
Willie should have written to Mr. Knox and
got the information. Or if he doesn’t know
Mr. Knox, and won’t ask any other Ma
Stater, he might have a word or two with
some of the jam manufacturers of his own
Cabbage patch. The only question the public
has to decide is whether it is better to let the
Trusts or the Government fix the prices. It
has come to this —-one or the other must
do it.
Speaking against the proposal, embodied
in the Referenda, to bring State railway ser-
vants within the jurisdiction of the Arbi-
tration Court, Willie Watt asked his Moonee
Ponds supporters: —
Is there any man living of you who thinks there
would not be chaos with a Railway Minister and the
Cabinet determining the policy of management, but
having nothing to say about the wages their servants
shall receive ?
But why should a wages award cause chaos
in a business managed by a Board of Minis-
ters. any more than in one run by a Board
of Directors?
W. D. Johnson, Minister for Works* in
Westralia’s Labor Government, has been
telling the Maoriland people that Massey’s
scheme of elective instead of nominated
Councillors is good goods, especially for
Labor. Yet, long ago, the Labor party in
Australia decided that the abolition of the
Councils was the best thing for Labor and
progress—in fact, that it was about the
only way to get within cooee of real reform.
Perhaps Johnson, like McGowen and Hol-
man and a sackful of others, reckons the
party’s platform is like the presentation
clock that stands on the drawing-room man-
telpiece under the glass case —merely orna-
mental.
The S. M. Herald gave another airing to
a decrepit old gag last week. The Sydney
Ferries Co. makes such prodigious profits
that it not only pays 10 p.c. on capital which
has been repeatedly watered, but has supplied
itself with a whole fleet of ferries out of sur-
plus earnings. It would not be too much to
say that the actual earnings of recent years
have been 25 to 30 p.c. per annum on the
capital actually put into the business by the
shareholders. Tempted by these prodigious
figures and the prospect of more bonuses
in the future, people have been paying ex-
travagant prices for scrip; and when the
bloated co. throws out a feeler about an in-
crease in fares, the Herald bids the public
be calm and remember that, though- the divi-
dends, etc., are big, investors are getting only
4 p.c. or so on their money. Now, supposing
some reporter went to the Herald and told
“Mr. James” that he had bought a house at
an exorbitant price, aud paid three times
more for his furniture than it was worth, and
that his wife was an extravagant piece of
goods who cost twice as much as she ought
to, would “Mr. James” keep calm and tell
the young man that his salary would be
trebled? Or if a paper manufacturer told
“Mr. James” that he had paid three times
the cost of a factory in order to get posses-
sion of it, would “Mr. James” feel called
upon to pay an exorbitant price for that
manufacturer’s paper, on the ground that
otherwise he wouldn’t get a fair return on
his investment?
Anti-Soshers who attribute the rise in the
cost of living to the existence of a Labor
Government will be puzzled to explain away
the latest estimates of the increased cost of
living in various countries. The figures,
extending over a period of 16 years, run as
follows : England, 27 per cent.; France, 18
per cent. ; Canada, 25 per cent.; Germany,
28 per cent.; America, 32 per
cent. Even the most untruth-
ful Tory paper would hardly
accuse Uncle Sam of living
under a Labor Government.
Scientific inquiries think the
principal causes are—(l) In
creased production of gold
(£41,000,000 in 1896, and
£94,193,000 in 1910), thereby
reducing the value of the sove-
reign as against other articles;
(2) combination among pro-
ducers and distributors, includ-
ing the factor known as
“trusts”; (3) higher wages to
workers. On the railways of
England this last item amounts
to something under Is. a week
per worker, so the tremendous
cut some workers are getting
out of the increase can be dis-
cerned at once. Anyway, what-
ever the cause, this is a magni-
ficent time to pay off our debt,
when Cohen will get compara-
tively little for our sov., and a
dashed bad one to borrow, when
we will get very little for
Cohen’s sov. When we wish to
practically borrow a railway
from Cohen, we want to do it
when we will get the most rail-
way possible for £1,000,000.
At present the time seems ripe
for getting the very least.
It was left to State Governor Fuller,
speaking at the A.N.A. luncheon in Mel-
bourne, to make one of the unkindest re-
marks to date about Alfred Deakin. Alfred,
he said, had “for many years been a figure-
head in Australasia.” Webster reckons
that a figurehead is “a person who allows
his name to be used to give standing to en-
terprises in which he has no responsible
interest.” But the Fusion was so hopeless
that even Deakin’s name failed to give it
any standing.
From the Oldest Inhabitant: —
“Old Parliamentarian” (B. 23/1 /’l3) accounts for
only two of the workers in the “free, secular and
compulsory” school agitation in N. S. Wales. Green-
wood did the talking, Dibbs backed him. But the
energetic secretary of the movement is still living—-
Mr. Walter S. Buzacott, the ship chandler of Market-
street. This writer was instructed once to wait upon
Mr. Buzacott and inquire what assistance Parkes gave
in the agitation. The answer was prompt and em-
phatic. “None! lie obstructed all he could. He
was an opportunist. When he found that public
opinion was with us he veered round and took a
hand.”
It isn’t always the ravening Socialist who
hounds the poor widow. English papers re-
port a case of one such woman charged
with shoplifting. She stated that owing to
the extra expense of the Compulsory Insur-
ance Act her employer had discharged her.
and she stole because she was starving. The
Bench expressed its disapproval of her glut-
tony by sentencing her to a month’s hard
labor. The amount of money 'which the
widow's employer was ashed to find under
the Act for her insurance was Sid. per week.

THE REAL ISSUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Orangemen put their Protestantism first, and asked
for neither Protection nor Freetrade. If political
candidates did not stand square to Protestantism they
should be turned down irrespective of the other
opinions they might hold. — Grand-Master Batley at
annual Orange orgy.
In three or four short months from now
Election day’ll be here;
The issues of the contest should
To one and all be clear.
A quaint delusion, held by some,
Is that the coming fight’s
Connected with the matter of
The Nation v. State Frights.
And others cherish the belief
The fiscal question may
Become of some importance long
Before election day.
AVho hold to such opinions show
A lamentable lack
Of knowledge ; for these things are but
As herrings ’cross the track.
The fundamental issue should
Be plain to ev’ry man;
Each candidate must say if he
Supports the Vatican.
A straight-out question should be put
To each would-be M.P.:
“If you’re returned, will you engage
To crush the Holy See?”
No other matter really counts,
On this our future leans ;
We’ll vote for those who pledge themselves
To give Pope Pius beans!
VICTORIA.

“There’s 'ash an’ there’s mutton—but mutton is off!”

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Last Saturday afternoon found a snort'
ing, howling nor’-easter raging over Port Jackson,
and except in a few sheltered nooks the waves were
running high, and the boats were capsizing right and
left. As far as yachting was concerned it was the
day of Prince Alfred Yachting Club, being the sort
of wind and water that yachtsmen revel in. The
Flying Squadron, however, in its open boats, had a
dandy time. The principal yacht race was an open
event, for trophies presented by Mr. Jenny, of Wan-
ganui (M.L.). Only four finished out of io starters,
and of these Petrel, steered by its owner,
Mr. Trebeck, was the greatest, making a mag-
nificent thresh to Manly, and overtaking and
passing the fleet on the way. After that
there was nothing else in it. Sunbeam and old-timer
Thelma had a great fight for second place, and Sun-
beam just managed to get along first. Rawhiti had
hideous luck; one of the crew fell overboard from
aloft on the wav to Manly, and another went over as
the yacht rounded the buoy. Fishing them out
practically put the big yacht out of the race. But
as if that was not enough, the yacht suddenly flew
wild and ran right over the 18-footer Kismet. Most
of the crew of the capsized boat scrambled on board
the Rawhiti, which then retired from the yacht race
and went after the human one in the water. It was
a day of disaster and breakdown.
* * * *
The open-boat racing in Sydney on Sat-
urday was a high-class exhibition of seamanship.
The wind, blowing strongly all the time, came with
savage puffs which must have been trying to the
fellows who were hanging over' the gunwales, heads
down, to keen the jigger right-side up. Kismet’s
sensational adventure with the big yacht Rawhiti
at Shark Island probably changed history, for the
wrecked craft was going great guns when it was
bowled over, and but for being run down would
almost certainly have won the race. Chris Webb,
in Australian, also struck disaster; owing to a
sprung mast he had to lower his canvas and drift
out, a practical wreck. In the first heat nothing
could get near Golding, till right on the finish Scot
came with a rush. But the time and the distance
to go were alike too short, and Golding blew through
with 12 seconds to spare. The final soon developed
; n+o another struggle between Golding and Scot.
After a long fight Scot managed to get about
Bsec. ahead of the persistent rival, and, being the
better boat off the wind, gradually forged ahead
and finally won by 30sec.
Ernest Barry, the English sculler, threat-
ens to retire from the game at the end of this year.
Barry is the only champion sculler the island has
raised within the past 35 years, but nobody can profit
by exploiting him. so his name is practically Mud—-
Thames mud. There is no “gate” to speak of in
sculling matches. All that a Barry can hone to win
is sufficient money and celebrity to carry him into a
decent pub and keen him there. It was the most that
champion nugs could look forward to in the old days,
ere stoushing became a gate money business. Bv the
wav. Bill Lang, having already achieved a pub in
Melbourne, talks casually of taking part in the old
game again.
The Felton-Towns sculling struggle on
the Parramatta yielded something of a record.
Among the barrackers aboard the steamers were four
ex-champions of the world—to wit. Beach, Peter
Kemp, James Stanbury and George Towns —watching
the downfall of C. Towns, another ex-champion.
* # * *
Alf Felton, who defeated ex-champion C.
Towns (never, by the way, much of a champion), is
probably the most promising sculler since the days
the bright eyes of “little Jim” Stanbury first peeped
over the rowing horizon. Felton is just 23, has a
finished style—in fact, one might say a bevelled-edge
style—and the strength of about three young bullocks.
Anything more opposite to the splashing style of
Dick Arnst it is impossible to imagine. Why Towns
was dug up to row against him is a mystery, as he
had absolutely no hope. But the match with burly
Jim Paddon, who resembles an elephant on a spider’s
chest when afloat on an outrigger, will make con-
siderable din on February 15, and should be worth
going far to see.
* * * *
Swimmer Albert W. Barry, who proved
somewhat of a frost at the recent State champion-
ships, came along ae-ain at the carnival at Manly
(N.S.W.) last Saturday, when he broke the Aus-
tralian record for 100yds. in his heat, eoualled it in
his semi-final, and made a mighty fast slither in the
final. His time for the distance was 56 3-ssec.,
which was l-ssec. better than Long-worth’s best, put
up in January last year. However, it seems it wili
not be recognised on account of the conditions,
Barry having had the advantage of a high platform,
and the consequent impetus of a long dive. But it
was a superb swim, and so excellently done that
while he was by himself in the rear of the bunch he
did not seem to be travelling at all fast. But when
he caught his field, it was seen how swiftly he was
gliding through the water. Another good perform-
ance at the same carnival was that of S. C. Wright,
who won the 440 yards’ Championship of the Northern
Suburbs in fimin. 53sec. For an all-round tussle
that swim would take a lot of beating, Adrian and
Mason sprinting again and again. But Wright was
too strong and fast, wore them down, and finished
strongly, with fully 15yds. between him and* Adrian.
On Foundation Day, when hundreds of
boys all over the Commonwealth were trying to get
drowned through their inability to swim, a 12-year-
old Melbourne lad swam three miles in the Yarra.
The V.A.S.A. offered six small trophies for a three-
mile handicap. Out of 63 starters not more than a
dozen retired from the long job, though most of the
others were hopelessly outdistanced before they had
been an hour in the water. The winner took
to get the course, and the nipper aforesaid crawled
home an hour and a half after the race was won. He
had been nearly three hours in the water.
* * * *
Duke Ivahanamoku, the famous Honolulu
swimmer, who made such a splash at the Olympic
gathering at Stockholm, nearly lost the number of
his mess last week. He had returned to his native
isles, and while swimming was attacked by what was
supposed to be the sea-serpent. It turned out to be a
giant conger eel, and was quite bad enough. The eel
meant business, and, finding the Duke was escaping,
it bit off a finger as a souvenir. This is a new and
most unpleasant horror.
* * * *
N.S.W. professional swimming champion
Oscar Dickman put out his fins to some purpose last
week, when he created another world record by swim-
ming over 1000yds in 13min. 33£sec. at Sydney.
He was some potatoes before, but this makes him
bigger than ever. He now holds the world’s pro-
fessional records for 220 yards, 880 yards, 1000 yards
and the mile. Also, he is improving, and every time
he goes into the water he comes out better, to the
worry and grief of the handicappers. At present he
so. overshadows his brother professionals that all
prizes are regarded as mere gifts for Dickman.
Those harmless, unobtrusive persons,
known as “fanciers,” who indulge in the pastime of
pigeon flying, are much exercised over the irregulari-
ties of a well-known breeder who captured several
fair prizes and much kudos for his birds by the
simple expedient of entering them at Adelaide for an
inter-State race, sending them to Melbourne, and then
having them returned per train instead of letting
them come per wing. Of course, sooner or later, some
inquisitive fancier was bound to spot the birds en
route, and this duly happened, and now the South
Aus. Homing Association is considering whether to
merely disqualify the offender for evermore, or to ask
the law to deal with him. To avoid similar bother in
future, this paper would recommend the South Aus.
Association to hold its homing matches from (say)
Darwin to Oodnadatta. From appearances it
will be several years before the Transcontinental ex-
press will outpace the average;“homer.”
* * * »
Motor-cyclist C. Mundy, whose Sydney-
Melbourne record was recently bettered, is on his
way to Darwin, where he hopes to secure a Daly River
farm in the forthcoming ballot.
One complete round of club games having
been played, East Torrens is still easily top of Ade-
laide cricket. This is unexpected, for, of the club’s
eight inter state men, Whitty and Mayne have not
been available much so far ; Crawford has removed to
another district and “Solly” Hill to Sydney; Roy Hill
and Con Chamberlain are now past their best, and
Stirling has not lived up to his early promise. The
three last, however, are still useful, and Len Cham-
berlain is at the top of his form ; also, Middleton is
a new and strong candidate for inter-State honors.
North Adelaide, the club of Gehrs, Rees and Reedman
(still going strong), is a bad second, with Sturt,
Clem Hill’s district—though Clem seldom plays in
club games—a close third.
* * * *
Under ordinary circumstances, another
round of First, Grade cricket would have com-
menced in Sydney on Saturday; but as the coming
week-end will see the Victor Trumper Benefit Match,
the circumstances were voted (extraordinary. So
Grade Cricket was off, and the afternoon was devoted
to friendly matches, the idea of slicing a round in
two with a big fixture being distasteful. Trumper
himself put in the time by raiding Glebe with an
XI which contained a pair of overseas players, one
from England and one from Africa. But Glebe, un-
dismayed, fell upon the invaders and dismissed them
for 143, Freeman taking six wickets for 48 runs,
Watson two for 12. and Cotter one for 40. It fol-
lowed un the repulse by whacking 243 for the loss of
five wickets. A veritable rout.
* * * *
While the “champagne of cricket” was
bein"- put down at Glebe, Queensland and Victoria
were struggling at Brisbane, where the Bananaland
team had accumulated 330 runs on the first attempt.
The men of mark were Thompson (116), Evans (75)
ahd Sheppard (42). Victoria did not shine by con-
trast, and made only 256 in reply, the scorers being
Brown, 63 ; Armstrong. 60: E. V. Carroll, 131 ;
and the other Carroll, 29. When stumps were drawn
Queensland had started on the second round and hit
41 for the loss of two sticks. The bright particular
feature of the game up to that point was the bowl-
ing of Downev, who took seven Victorian wickets
for 97 runs. No one else got near him.
* * * *
Adelaide (S.A.) cricket on Saturday last
was blessed with a hurricane performance by E R
Mayne, who smote 237, not out, in 215 minutes, at
the expense of the bowlers from Sturt. L. Chamber-
lain, with a useful 95, helped Mayne to raise East
Torrens’s score of 417 for three wickets. Other
smiters worthy of a cheer were the colts N. L.
Gooden (140) and H. Bridgman (81), who smacked
most of the West Torrens score of 305 for six
wickets.
* * * •
Sundries has been a good scorer in the
Sheffield Shield matches this season. For Vic the
12th player pocketed 93, for S.A. 86, and for
N.S.W. 63. Time someone said something severe
about this player being allowed such a free leg.
“Blackbut” : Nothing finer has been shown
in wicket-keeping than the work of Victorian stumper
W. Carlceek against N.S.W. Not until 335 runs
were on the board did the first leg-bye happen along,
and Carkeek guarded his position so carefully that
in N.S.W.’s total of 640 only one sundry was credited
against him, the other seven being wides, leg-byes
and no-balls. In fact, only pne whirl of the leather
out of 750 fired at the opposing batsmen missed
Carkeek’s gloves.
Saturday in Melbourne was a day when
it was the business of. everv batsman to bog in if
he wanted his side to win. Few, however, tried to do
it, though in one or two cases they had the excuse
of good bowling against them. St. Kilda was up
against North, and Hotchin (who has been lustily
kicking at the selectors’ door for some time) and
Ainslie spent most of the afternoon at the wickets
on their little lonesomes, the total at six o’clock
being 309 for four wickets —Hotchin 121, Ainslie
135. North has now Buckley’s chance of winning,
though it may make a draw. East, with Seitz and
Baring away in Brisbane, found P’ran’s second-rate
bowling too good for its own third-rate batting, and
laboriously compiled 121 in three dragging hours.
’Roy lost the toss to Carlton, and, with bowler
Cannon away, spent most of the afternoon getting
its opponents out for 173, which score should be
easily beaten next Saturday. Richmond made spas-
modic attempts to put a decent score on the board,
but found bowler Kyle, formerly P’ran’s mainstay, in
rather deadly form. It had raked together 255 for
eight wickets by closing time, of which Stewart ac-
counted for 112. Essendon scraped up 172 against
’Varsity, which little lot Hartkopf should about
beat by himself this week end. ’Wood, having the
bye, filled in the afternoon against the sub-district
of Hawthorn, and had the satisfaction of discovering
that its new wicket-keeper, one Nyman, is also a
batsman of parts. He rattled up 135,; n. 0.; but, of
course, Hawthorn’s bowling, like its: politics, is not
inclined to be progressive.
“A Cricket Crank”: This year’s Sheffield
Shield struggle is making much excitement in cricket
bungalows. The defeat of Victoria at Sydney nar-
rowed things down to the point that Victoria has only
to successfully jump on S.A. to make two wins all
round, and then it will be necessary to hire a cunning
mathematician to pull the winner out of the tangle.

* * * *
“Adelaide” : South Aus. was as interested
as the players themselves in the recent cricket match,
N.S.W. v. Vic. More so, in fact, than Trumper and
Co. were, for, owing to the failure of the side sent
to Adelaide and Melbourne, Ma has no chance of the
Sheffield Shield this year. Her best team just beat
S.A. in Sydney, and now that it has beaten Vic., too,
Clem Hill and Co. are on “a bit to nothing.” That
is, if they beat Vic. in the only match remaining to
be played, they get the Shield easily, and if they lose
the score will be two won and two lost, all round; in
which case S.A. must almost certainly make the best
showing on averages, having won by an innings from
the aforesaid N.S.W. side.
In the course of a cricket match in Albert-
Park (Melbourne), on Foundation Day, a batsman
with no legs was given out “leg before.”
The English tennis four put in part of a
day on the Adelaide Oval, each man getting a single
and a double. Roy Taylor, Bowen (playing in
glasses), Justin O’Dea and R. V. Thomas were not
much use to the visitors in singles, but put up a good
fight in the doubles. Young Thomas was the only
one to increase his reputation ; he always had veteran
Dixon thinking, and took one set singly and one (with
O’Dea) in the double. Those two sets were S.A.’s
only score. Governor Bosanquet presided at the lunch,
and started a controversy by asserting that two hours’
tennis gives as much exercise as four hours’ golf.
Though well on in the 60’s, Bosanquet himself plays
both.
* * * *
The Melbourne Six-Days’ Race is to com-
mence on the night of February 24, and will rage till
the night of March 1. The McNamara-Corry team
which scooped the pool at Sydney is competing, with
seven others who took part in the same tussle, and
with teams representing Vic., Tas. and South Aus.
Applehans and Rosseau did not wait for the event,
but started last Saturday for Paris, taking with
them the Tasmanian rider Keefe, who intends to try
his luck on the big European tracks. His head-
quarters will be Paris.
* * * *
Seven years have come and gone since the
last Motor Reliability Test between Sydney and Mel-
bourne raged through the astonished' continent, and
created almost as much excitement as the Balkan
war, and people had given up the idea of ever seeing
another. But there is to be at least one more, as
the Automobile Club of Australia, as the Sydney
concern magniloquently calls itself, is co-operating
with the Victorian body to run “a sealed-bonnet
reliability touring contest” on March 26, 27, 28 and
29. The only stipulation is that there shall be at
least eight entrants from each State. The affair is
to be controlled by a joint committee representing
the two high contracting parties, and at time of
writing sundry enthusiasts known as the “roads and
tours committee” of the N.S.W. body are at work
drafting a set of rules fqr the government of the
contest, to be submitted to the men of Victoria.
Originally it was proposed that there should be a
teams race, N.S.W. versus Victoria, but that idea
has been dropped, and the struggle will be a straight-
out individualistic one. As proposed, there will be
no great strain on the participants, as the 572 miles
run is to be spread over four days, and will be along
the main southern route through Goulbitrn, German-
ton and Albury. It will be more in the nature of
a picnic than anything else, as it is unlikely that
the contestants will do as they did on the occasion
of the first Test, when they raced for their very
lives and made a speed contest of it. One experience
of that sort was enough. Although the notice is
short, it is possible that cars from Tasmania, South
Aus. and Queensland will take part.
If a recent ropgistrate’s decision nndor
the new Cruelty to Animals Act holds good, Westralia
may set the Commonwealth a good lead. A shooter
at a Perth pigeon-murdering match dropped a bird
outside the boundary; and as under the club rules
he wasn’t permitted to pick it up from that position,
the unfortunate fowl flapped around in its gore for
a quarter of an hour. The Act provides a £lO fine
for anyone ill-treating any animal, and the magis-
trate sensibly held that wounding was “ill-treating,”
and said 10s. and costs. A lot of people—including
pigeons—have been awaiting a law of that sort for
a long time.
* * * *
A Melbourne promoter tells of a well-
known Australian lightweight coming to him with
tears, in his eyes, pleading for a brother pug. “Gor-
bli’,.” he said, “you orter give old Ginger a fight. He
ain’t a bad draw, veh know, and if vou put him up
agin Potty Cobbit they’d fill the ’all all right. Garn.
give him a fight! He’s got this here ’pendercitis and
a orful sore leg, and he’s gotter go into the ’orspital
soon. Give him a fight, won’t yeh—pore beggar?”
* * * *
Freddie Welsh, the vegetarian light-weight
who beat ITughie Mehegan at the National Sporting
Club last month, showed the Australian camp some
subtle points one way and another. For example it
leaked out, during the training, that Welsh had had
one of his eyes so badly opened that stitches were in-
serted. Frederick entered' the ring on the night of
battle with an ominous-looking wound showing
under a mass of sticking-nlaster over his right eve
Mehegan, in accordance with the custom of his trade'
devoted a large part of his attention to the injured
antic. He connected with it time and again. But
though he knocked the sticking-plaster to blazes, he
failed to draw blood. The explanation lay in the
met that it was the other eye which was in trouble
The wound was bogus—a tribute to the skill of ari
artist in theatrical make-up.

Herbert McCoy got the surprise of his lif„
at Sydney Stadium last week when he met Jack P i
for the Resident Lightweight Championship of a!,
tralia. He expected a cyclone for half a dozen round
—he had met it before —but he also expected that S
time went on the storm would blow itself out h
caught the tornado all right; in the second round it
laid open an old wound, and he bled profuselv
McCoy is particularly game, and finding he was eet
ting behind on points, he raged in with terrific sDenrt
trying hard for a knock-out. But Read has improve,!
out of sight since he has been training with the nig
gers, and he, too, considered that the best defence
was to be the attacking party. So they both at
tacked, and there was much rushing and clinching
It went the full 20 rounds ; and although
McCoy would score a round, he was unable to keen
pace with his leaping opponent, and when the
sounded the truce, referee Scott immediately pointed
to Read as the winner. There was some awful
thumping in the fight, but barring the re-opened
wound on McCoy’s nobility, there wasn’t much to
show for it —which proves what a splendid thing n
is to be thoroughly fit.
On Saturday night Midwood, the huskv
youth who but recently emerged from the amateur
ranks, and who, after being knocked down bv De
Balzac four times in one round, got up and won' met
Dave Smith at Sydney Stadium. They started ’vim-
fully, and it was immediately seen that Smith had
durine- his travels completely changed his method of
fi<rhting. He used to be a boxer of the Australian
tvpe: now he is a scrambling, wrenching fighter of
the Yankee order, and his chief weapon of offence is
a right cross. While Midwood used a straight left
and used it hard, he could keep Smith off, as for
some reason Smith dislikes being hit on the nose and
jaw. A tap on the nose will always disconcert him
But when Midwood ceased to poke out his straight
left. Smith lifted his eyes to Heaven and murmured
“The. Lord hath delivered him into mine hand!” And
then he got in close and used the right cross
Although it was soon obvious that Smith was too
good a ring general for his opponent, Midwood fought
on doggedly, and took punishment by the cartload.
Knocked down again and again, he was twice saved
by the gong, and an enthusiastic crowd cheered him
as they have rarely cheered a boxer in Sydney before.
Seconds and admirers alike clung to the hope that
he would land one wild whirling blow on Smith's
chin —the sort that Smith doesn’t like—and out him.
But as time went on he lost the power of attack.
What punches he did give had no sting in them, and
Smith bored in for a knock-out. But though he
butchered Mid wood badly, Smith was unable to knock
him out. Midwood had an awful time in the ninth
round, and early in the 10th he was knocked down
again, and while he was taking a count the towel
was thrown in and Smith declared the winner.
All the same, he should have been disqualified.
He won the fight fairly enough, but while Midwood
was down he hopped on to the ropes and sat there,
and by resting and removing his feet from the floor
of the ring forfeited the fight, according to the rules.
Referee Scott was busy counting, and did not see the
breach. He had scarcely time to do so, for hardlv
was Smith on the ropes before he was literally blown
off them by the blast of indignant hooting. It was
terrific. A dozen steamer sirens suddenly let loose
could not have made a more appreciable din; and
Smith fell off the ropes as though an elephant had
kicked him in the back of the neck. Of course, it
looked horribly flash and infernally ungenerous to a
brave opponent, but, all the same, those who know
Smith best are quite sure that it was merely thought-
lessness. He was feeling good, and was so delighted
to feel that way, after so many rounds with so hard
a hitter as Midwood, that he just hopped up to let
off his exuberance. However, apart from the incident,
the question, that occurs to one is whether Smith has
gone off or is Midwood a coming star. He is fright-
fully inexperienced, and the failure of Smith to out
him or beat him to mush in less than nine rounds
suggests that Smith is not a rising star of any magni-
tude.
* * * *
As everybody expected, the N.S.W. Rugby
League has secured the tenancy of the Sydney Cricket
Ground for the coming season : and the Rugby Union
has gone down before the weight of the other crowd's
bloated money bags. The Rugby Union’s inability
to reform itself has practically “bust” that body,
unless the other body perpetrates a bigger buster
against itself, which doesn’t seem likely while the
present level-head^ l management is maintained. So
it is probable that the star match of each week in
the coming season will be staged at the well-appointed
Sydney Cricket Ground. If the League can induce
the bar to stock a better range of drinks, it will
be a boon and a blessing to the thirsty and shivering
onlookers. The bar badly wants reforming.
* * * *
How a man does go down hill once he has
ceased to be a managing director and is merely
“the accused”! On Monday the Sydney papers an-
nounced with a flare the “arrest of Mr. J. D.
Williams.” On Tuesday the matter was headed
merely “The Williams Case.” Sic transit gloria
Monday!

Gunn’s motor-cycle record between Syd-
ney and Melbourne is still unbroken, although no less
than eight attempts have been made upon 'ft-
Mechanical troubles counted five out, two attempts
were ruined by the riders getting bushed and taking
wrong tracks, and tyre troubles smashed up the other.
The best attempt was Tyler’s dash, but be didn’t dash
fast enough. At Yass he was 2hr. 15min. behind
Gunn’s time; at Gundagai, 2hr. 50min. ; at Alburv,
lhr. 55min. ; at Benalla, lhr. 39min. From Benalla
to Seymour their times were the same. Then Tyler’s
pace quickened, and 35 miles from Melbourne it
looked as though he would succeed in lowering Gunn’s
figures. But just at the critical time his machine
went wrong, and he proceeded to Melbourne per
motor-car.
“Armes” whispers words of comfort: —
Five students of Duntroon Military College have
been fired out, not for misconduct, but because the
authorities say they have not the brains. Now, I sav
to such fellows, “Buck up.” One of them may be-
come Commander-in-chief. Wellington’s mother said
he was a fearful dunce—an unmitigated disgrace
alongside his clever brother, afterwards known as
Wellesley. General Phil Sheridan, the most brilliant
figure in the U.S. Civil War, was put back a year
at West Point. Grant came out low down there, and
might have been rejected. Napoleon was not brilliant
at the Military College, and for seven years after en-1
tering the army it did not appear that he would make
good. On the other hand, Stonewall Jackson was a
brilliant College Professor. Caesar, Cromwell and .
Clive never had any military instruction. And quit- j
ting the military profession for a last minute, I re-
call that a well-known teacher at the Melbourne
Church of England Grammar School, nicknamed
Peggy, declared that Oscar Asche could not be en-
trusted even with a little part in the College thea-
tricals.
HYDROPLANING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Comes a sudden, frenzied stutter
And along a white spray gutter
Scoots the noisy hydroplane.
You must blink and peer again
Ere you sight the standing figure
’Mid the spray dust that the jigger
From the rippling surface smashes
As its clean-cut bow it dashes
When it’s jumping off its tail.
From the wave tops see it springing,
As its loud explosions ringing
Wake the watching, wooded shores.
Oh! it clatters and it snores,
Straight across the level sea 1
And its quiv’ring hull is lashing
Straight across the elvel sea!
Streaking past the white sails blowing,
Never swerving, never slowing,
Goes the oscillating thing ;
And it runs across the water
Like a wildfowl from the slaughter
Making sudden foolish flights ;
With broad flashes it alights;
Then the small propeller racing
Tears to foam the Summer sea,
And it rushes, whitely lacing,
As if Nick himself were pacing,
And the engine’s deaf’ning sputter
Swiftly sinks into a mutter ;
But the clatter of its flight
Drifts across both bay and bight,
While the glimmer
And the shimmer
Of its foam remains in sight.
It is just a water devil when it rips
across the main,
Is the splashing, dashing, lashing,
petrol-driven hydroplane.
x.s.w.
E. O’F.

The other Monday a Melbourne house-
breaker was captured by a local police ser-
geant under circumstances which reflected
great credit on the vigilance of —the neigh-
bors. The neighbors, having discovered the
man’s presence in a house during its owner’s
absence, kept him boxed up like a rat in a
trap whilst the police-station was being
rung up. Hence the capture. House-
breakers run in all shapes, and this was one
of the oddest creatures in the business. He
might have been dressed for a quick-change
act in a panto. He wore two coats, one
sweater, a couple of vests, and an obviously
false beard—a bushy black beard of the
Pirate King pattern. Also, he was armed
with a revolver, loaded in five places, and a
number of fishhooks. The loaded weapon,
which appeared to be damning evidence of
his murderous character, proved to have
been recently taken from another unguarded
villa residence. Its lawful owner had bought
it to protect himself against burglars, and
apparently the first burglar who came along
had stolen it.
“Bash” presents the fruit of long re-
search :
The building' which is not to be pulled down for
the erection of the Morning Hellagraph’s 97-
storey premises was first tenanted by Dr. Angel
Money, vvho was succeeded in 1850 by one of tne
Six lamilies. This family (No. 5) refused to give
space in the backyard for George Coppin’s Iron
Pot, but, some 25 years later (October, 1839), it
allowed Governor Bligh and John Pascoe Fawkner
to establish in the kitchen the first Australian jam
factory. The Iron Pot was used in the process.
The quandong jelly turned put by the firm was
lavishly patronised by the original Hordern and
E. W. Cole. Mr. Hordern was then (1805) on the
historic slope of Flagstaff Hill, not far from the
West Melbourne swamp. Before this he had founded
the first paper in Adelaide, for which he had
Thomas Makdougall Brisbane as junior devil and as-
sistant sweeper-out. lima de Murska and Teddy
Lonnen were at that time (1895) appearing in a
patter-and-dance turn at the Olympic Josshouse of
Varieties. Lola Montes gave sacred songs at the rival
house. The popular drink of the period (1789) was
raspberry hush, so named by The Bulletin’s Reli-
gious Editor from the appearance of Lola’s face one
Good Friday Night when the grease-paint ran. It
(the raspberry hush) was a mixture of champagne
and oyster cocktail, sipped with a straw through a
tinned peach. Lord Beerthirst and J. S. Butters
had no time for it, but when Garnet Walch wrote
“Joseph of Canaan” for Little Tich and Lottie
Collins (opening in Perth on Boxing Night, 1863),
a party from Robert Lowe’s country house at Glenelg
Beach, including John Thomas Smith and the non-
coms of the 17th Regiment, were greatly delighted
by a topical reference to the novel drink, added
to Walch’s play by W. M. Akhurst and Joe Jeffer-
son. Lowe was a lavish patron of the arts. His
name appears in the list of subscribers to the first
novel published at Port Darwin (1767) —the joint
masterpiece of “Curse o’ Moses” and “The Oldest
Inhabitant.” This work, “The Convict Cowpuncher,
being Passages in the Life of Peder Pedersen,” was
published in a subscription edition of five vols. at
355. net, or in one vol., with a three-color cover, at
9d., to be exchanged at any time on payment of
tuppence. Four hundred and eighty sets of the
edition de luxe have been offered at various times
to the Library of the Australian Parliament, at the
moderate average price of 84 guineas, but, to the
lasting disgrace of this great nation, it was resolved
by Mr. Willis that the Speaker be no longer heard,
and that bas-reliefs of scenes in the life of Bacon
be added to the Cook monument as the result of a
concert to be given by Amy Castles, under the aus-
pices of the Dickens Fellowship, together with a set
of the works of Bernard O’Dowd, presented by the
Shakspeare Society.
Maoriland’s High Commissioner, Tam
Mackenzie, is to be the guest of George V.
when that potentate goes along to inspect,
the big warship that Maoriland told John
Bull to build, out of his own pocket, and
keep for himself as a gift from a dutiful
child. The invitation is no doubt intended
to assure M.L. that its interest in the ship
(about per cent, on a couple of millions)
is being kept steadily in view.
“Melb”: Conductor Steinmetz, of St.
Patrick’s Cathedral here, writing to the
press, makes a statement which interests
li.C.’s all over the Globe. Steinmetz de-
clares that the so-called “Proper” of the
Mass is sung according to the Pope’s direc-
tion, in Gregorian, and that the rest doesn’t
matter, wherefore it is filled up with inno-
cent selections from Gounod, Beethoven,
Mozart, Haydn, Guilmant, Saint-Saens,
Paladilhe, Rousseau, and the other great
composers. The purists, nevertheless, still
assert that the Pope has explicitly ruled all
such composers out; and this is the view
taken by the Melbourne Jesuit Churches of
St. Ignatius and the Immaculate Concep-
tion, which have restored the simple old
fashioned Gregorian throughout. On the
other hand, bear in mind that Archbishop
Carr saw the Pope lately, and has not said
a word about the Cathedral music.
“Pariah’s” township :
Five out of every 10 Victorians know that Colac
is on the Gippsland line. The other five can tell
you exactly where it is. It is on page 26 of the
Victorian Railway Guide. That and 12s. 9d. will
get you there.
The residents are very proud of the Murray-street
drain, which, with others, disembogues into a swamp
nearly in the heart of the town. It is not a nice
swamp.
There is also a butter factory, which last year beat
all the Maoriland factories in the prices obtained in
London for its butter. This concern pays suppliers
from £14,000 to £29,000 monthly. In the pay
office there is—or was —a conspicuous collection box
for the local hospital. One month’s contributions
totalled 9d.
Though most of the branch roads are from bad to
worse, the councillors are fortunate enough to have
their dwellings on what good ones there are.
The main topics of conversation are cows, onions,
potatoes and cows.
The ladies are great on politics of the Liberal
persuasion. Said I to one of the most prominent:
“But the Liberal party has no platform!” “Oh, yes,
they have,” she said. “Well,” I replied, “what is
it?” Said she: “I’ve forgotten just for the moment,
but I’ve a copy in my desk.”
The shire has an overdraft of over £2OOO. The
rate is Is. 6d. on a 20-odd year-old valuation, and
in reality is only a 6d. or 9d. rate. To rectify this
the council intends shortly to raise a loan.
Professor Warren, of Sydney University,
is not only spreading far and wide a useful
knowledge of Australian timbers, but is con-
tributing to the scientists of Europe much
valuable information about the methods of
determining the commercial properties of
timbers generally. Half the enthusiast’s
life has gone in the engineering workrooms
of Sydney University, where the testing of
iron and timber has been his chief occupa-
tion. When he took up the school 29 years
ago his material equipment was extremely
limited, but he and his pupils set out to
make weights, scales and other necessary in-
struments out of the materials about them,
and with even these homely contrivances he
succeeded in reaching important results.

For Comfort and Luxury
The Carrington Hotel, Katoomba,
is without equal on the Mountains. The fine, bracing air of Katoomba, with its cool
nights, makes it an ideal spot to recuperate after strenuous City life. Whilst for a week-
end holiday, a stay at the “CARRINGTON” will add greatly to your enjoyment.
This Hotel embodies every comfort and convenience. Its location combines beauty with
accessibility. Situated on the highest point of Katoomba, it commands a magnificent
view of the surrounding scenery, yet it is but a minute from the Station.
There is accommodation for 120 guests, and the building has been furnished throughout
in lavish style. The “CARRINGTON” possesses its own Electric Lighting, Water and
Ventilating Plants, and the Garage, with a capacity for eight Cars, is one of the finest
out of Sydney. Each Car is under separate lock and key.
A feature of the “CARRINGTON” is the Hydropathic Department, with its High Fre-
quency, Electric Light, Vapor and Electric Baths. An expert Masseur is always in
attendance to treat Ladies and Gentlemen staying at the Hotel according to their Medical
instructions. TARIFF: £3 10s. per week; 12s. 6d. per day.
DAILY TRIPS TO JENOLAN CAVES BY MOTOR
You can make this trip by one of the modern “Itala” Cars, leaving in the morning and
returning in plenty of time for dinner at night. Return Fare, £2.
THE CARRINGTON HOTEL, KATOOMBA.

From the Old Soldier
In itself the recent export of a small mob of horses
to Japan (B. 30/l/’l3) doesn’t matter much. What
does matter is the craze for exporting everything the
foreigner can be persuaded to buy. The idea that
the best use he can make of his produce is to export
it, and that the less he uses for himself the more
thrifty he is, has been so persistently harhmered into
the Australian’s head that the belief has become
fixed; and the exports must be all the best, while
the worst is always good enough for home consump-
tion. So the export of stud stock —horses, cattle and
sheep—is regarded as a fine stroke of business, though
the local supply of the best class of stock is depleted
accordingly. Applied to the warhorse supply, the net
result is that the constant export of the best mares—
for the exporter insists on taking only the best—
results in the gradual deterioration of the breed.
There is no special breed of horses for war,. but
there are three very well-marked types—the heavy
draught for heavy artillery and transport, the lighter
draught for field and horse-artillery, the weight-
carrying saddle-horse for the obsolescent cavalry. The
mass of nondescripts wanted as mounted infantry
cobs, infantry officers’ chargers and packhorses need
not be considered. The three well-marked types are
important. If the Government establishes studs, it
will have to breed all three; but especially it will
have to breed the two draught classes, because the
utility in modern war of the old style of regular
cavalry is, at best, doubtful. The Japs won all their
victories in Manchuria without it, and they were op-
posed bv the most numerous, if not the best, cavalry
in the world.
It is more than 270 years since Cromwell wrote:
“If we had 2000 horses that could but trot 30
miles!” This is more or less the difficulty of every
commander. People who haven’t seen war have no
idea of the enormous demand for horses for transport.
After the first battle, not only are the waggons of the
ammunition columns emptied to fill those of the bat-
teries, but their horses are taken to replace the
casualties among the teams of the same batteries,
and have to be replaced somehow. It is quite likely
that no organisation of Government studs could pos-
sibly keep up with the Australian army’s demands
for horses for war, though ft would help by popularis-
ing the breeding of useful horses. What is impera-
tively necessary is to stop the export of brood mares
of the useful class. The blood horse is all very well
in its way, as a cross to breed in a dash of speed and
courage; but, now that artillery has an effective
range of 5000 to 7000 yards, and the infantry rifle
2000 yards, the value of mere spurts of speed is a
good deal discounted, and the ability to “trot 30
miles" becomes more valuable than ever.

BEALE HIGH-GRADE PIANOS.

The above are just a few of the many testimonials that we are continually receiving
from users of Beale High-Grade Pianos.
Such success has only been obtained through our unwearying efforts to produce an
instrument of the best quality. That we have succeeded is proved by the opinions of
those who should know best —the users of our pianos.
To obtain this quality we have invented patent methods of construction which render
our pianos more durable, while retaining and enhancing all the musical qualities of the
great makes of the Old World. The use of an iron wrest-plank which is part of our
patent steel tuning-system —is as great an improvement in the art of piano manufacture
as was the superseding of the old wooden-frame by the iron-frame which is used in all
modern pianos. In addition, we have introduced many other improvements, such as our
method of building up the casework from cross : plies of wood which is all thoroughly
seasoned here, and hence tit to withstand Australian climatic conditions much bettei tha.n
timber seasoned in the cold, damp climate of Europe. In order to get the best of s 1 ed
artisans, we pay higher wages than those awarded by the W ages Boards m the various
trades. We naturally get the best artisans, and thus the best of skill. W e use only the
finest of materials.
We realise that an Australian-made piano is subjected to much closer scrutiny in
Australia than any imported piano, and therefore we make—we are compelled to make—
our pianos much letter than those of our competitors. But then, by making pianos in
Australia we are in a position to make them much better suited to Australian conditions.
Any competent cabinet-maker will tell you that timber seasoned in the cold, damp climate
of Europe cannot be expected to withstand Australian conditions of dry heat as well as
timber which is seasoned in this climate. This is a most important point in piano-
making, and is one good reason why so many foreign pianos develop cracked sounding-
boards, split tuning-systems and faulty casework soon after arrival in this country. But
no man can say that he ever saw a Beale piano with a cracked sounding-board. We
absolutely guarantee that the sounding-boards of the Beale Pianos will never crack in any
climate, because our special system of construction renders cracking an impossibility, at
the same time that it greatly enhances the tonal qualities of the instrument. Beale
pianos contain the only practical steel tuning-system in the world —so there can be no
question of splitting of the wrest-plank. And then our patented system of cross-plied
woodwork ensures permanence of the outside case of the pianos.
Be sure of this: the better musician you are, the more you will delight in a Beale High-grade Piano.
We have always on hand a number of good second-hand pianos, taken in part payment of
Beale Pianos.
Call and inspect the Beale Pianos now. Inspection does not put you under any obligation
whatever. You suit yourself entirely as to whether you puichase.
If you cannot call, write for Catalogue, which we will be glad to send post free. We sell for
cash or on easiest terms.

THESE MAGNIFICENT CATTLE,
founded upon Mr. James Stuckey’s renowned
Te Rangitumau Herd, are practically all
eligible for entry in the ENGLISH HERD
BOOK.
They cannot be surpassed in the world for
EVENNESS OF TYPE, CONSTITUTION,
EARLY MATURITY and GRAND RICH
DARK COLOR.
THE TE! RANGITUMAU-BUSHY PARK
HERD have* had a memorable career. For
mapy years they have carried all before them
in the Fat Classes, and have more than held
their own in the Stud Sections. This is their
WONDERFUL SHOW RECORD!
Between November, 1905, and December,
1912, they secured in the Stud Classes no
fewer than 49 CHAMPIONSHIPS. 113
FIRST PRIZES, 56 SECONDS and 24
THIRDS.
In the Fat Classes they were awarded :
231 FIRST PRIZES, 149 SECONDS and
59 THIRDS.
The following are a few of the
TROPHIES WON BY THESE FINE
CATTLE :
Manawa Bowl, twenty guineas, won outright,' most
points in Fat Cattle at Masterton.
Special at Hawera tor Yearling Stud Hereford Bull.
Mr. S. Cundv’s Sterling Silver Cup at Wanganu ;
for points in Fat Cattle.
Messrs. Barraud and Abraham’s Trophy, £IC
IPs., most points in Fat Cattle at Manawatu, won
outright repeatedly.
Champion Prize, presented by Messrs. Pitt, Son
and Badgery, for best Fat Ox, any age or breed, at 1
Sydney, 1009. The same bullock also won First
Prize for Hereford Fat Ox —any age-—and the Cham-
pionship for heaviest Ox on the Show Grounds. He
was only six years old !
A steer shown by Mr. Moore won outright a piece
of Silver Plate, presented bv Hereford Association,
for best two-year Hereford Steer, also for best three-
year Steer (Hereford), shown at Wanaganui, Mana-
watu, and Masterton, 1911-1912.
The cattle have been notably successful in Freez-
ing and Shop-bullock classes, thus showing their
qualities of Early Maturity.

The course of this gentle little concern is as placid
as a cucumber. It sticks to Ballarat and has no
branches anywhere else ; it is the one and only “pro-
vincial” bank on the mainland of the Commonwealth
—-the one and only bank whose head-office isn’t in a
State capital. Unfortunately, Ballarat, where it has
its home, isn’t violently progressive: so in the past
seven years the bank’s advances have moved along
only from £297,896 to £303,354. Its paid capital
is £85,000; add reserves, £83,193; total share-
holders’ funds, £168,193. The liabilities of all sorts
amount to £256,136. For every £1 of shareholders’
money in the business there is thus only 30s. 6d
of the public’s. Further, the liquid assets—all cash
and public securities —amount to £121,745. In view
of those figures, any depositor who loses sleep won-
dering whether his money is safe with this bank is
much too nervous to live long. Naturally, with so
little of the public’s money to play with, and with so
much .of the funds in liquid assets, the earnings are
not big. Calculated on the basis of capital and re-
serves combined, they work out as follows:
June, 1909 £5 3 2 p.c. June, 1911 £5 4 6pc
Dec., 1909 5 3 6 „ Dec., 1911 5 5 9
June, 1910 5 4 0 „ June, 1912 5 410 ”
Dee., 1910 5 2 8 „ Dec., 1912 5 7 3 ”
Even that would be impossible but for the centralisa-
tion of the business and the extraordinarily low ex-
pense rate. Run on its present lines there is no hope
of a quick rise for anybody, but there is every pro-
spect Of a very fair return with almost a minimum
of risk. Further, there is a steadily accumulating"
reserve*. which will either (if things go right) make
the dividend a shade better by-and-bye or (if things
go wrong) be a splendid protection for the capital. '
