So far as the past half-year is concerned the direc-
tors’ report doesn’t tally with the published accounts •
but the directors’ report is probably wrong. Any-
aay, it has been assumed in the foregoing that it is
Like other Western (N.S.W.) collieries the Vale
ot Clwydd did poorly until the Ldhgow Coal Asso-
ciation was formed and they left off mutual throat-
cutting. The future depends largely upon the
maintenance of that or some similar arrangement -
and there is no reason at all to expect that it will
be disturbed. Through this association the co. has
been able not only to pay a good and regular divi-
dend, but also to put by a very handsome sum.
As at Dec., 1905, the assets other than land and
plant were valued at £14,748. Deduct liab lities
£2061, and you have £12,687 of more or less
liquid assets net. Now the more or less liquid
assets are £16,144; less liabilities £1243. leaving
£14,901 net. _ That increase from £12,687 to
£14,901 doesn’t look much for seven years; but
meantime £11,072 has been put into brickworks
Capital was increased from £30,000 to £45,000
in 1910; but that increase is represented’ by
value added to the mine and plant. So it boils
down to this: the co., while it has paid its divi-
dends, has in seven years made surplus profit suffi-
cient to pay for the whole of the new brick-makin"
plant, and there has been about £l5OO over, plus
anything put into mining property and plant
Altogether it is an excellent record, even though a
mine is a wasting asset, and a part of its apparent
profit is really used-up capital.
THE UNSUSPECTED BEGGAR
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sydney Ferries Co. has decided to increase the
rates for season tickets. The report submitted at the
half-yearly meeting yesterday contained the following
paragraph:—
“The past year has been one of exceptional activity
and progress. The expansion of the company’s busi-
ness is satisfactory, but the cost for a long time past
is greatly in excess of the new revenue earned, due to
increased rents, rates, taxes and cost of material, but
mainly to increased wages under the high award of
tVages Boards, and there appears to be no hope of
relief in these directions.” —Daily paper.
He was stout and he was sturdy, and his golden
watch-chain bright,
As festooned across his waistcoat, was a truly gorge-
ous sight;
All his clothes were new and shiny, and the radiance
of his tile
Almost dimmed the sizzling arc-lights. Oh, he was
a mass of style 1
But he blubbered on my shoulder, and in strangled
4 accents said,
“Can you spare a man a copper, sir, to buy a crust
of bread?”
He was fat beyond all reason, and his pink, clean-
shaven neck,
Flotving flood-like o’er his collar, almost made the
thing a wreck ;
He had diamond studs and sleeve-links, and his
cheque-book I could see
Resting in his silken pocket, as he leant and wept on
me.
“You are tight!” I muttered fiercely. “Cab-it home
and go to bunk!”
“I m an Unsuspected Beggar!” sobbed the stout, ap-
parent drunk.
“I’m an Unsuspected Beggar! I’m a stout and
gilded sham !
And, if honest folk rebuke me, well, I do not care a
d—n !
These misleading signs of richness are my everlasting
Curse,
For, upon me soul, I haven’t got a shilling in me
purse! ”
(Hot tears trickled through the fingers that he’d
spread out like a fan.)
“Help the Unsuspected Beggar, if you call yourself a
Man!”
N.B.W.

HORSESHOE SALMON
HORSESHOE makes a dainty, appetising meal served direct from the
can. It offers the skilful housewife a wide choice of “hot” dishes suit-
able for all meals—for all occasions.
\
Curried Salmon—A Delightful Dish.
Ingredients: 1 tin Horseshoe Salmon, cold boiled rice, 4 hard-boiled eggs, salt,
cayenne and butter.
Method: Chop the eggs and mix with Salmon, rice and seasoning. Mix curry
powder with melted butter. Place mixture in enamel saucepan and stir till
very hot. Serve in hot dish with slices of lemon.

IN BANANA LAND
Governor Macgregor told the Dalby people
he didn’t see how he could spend Sunday
better than in laying foundation-stones of
hospitals. The Chieftain is altogether a
quaint Caledonian. A Presbyterian by up-
bringing, he does mason work on the Saw-
bath. A countryman of Carlyle’s, he does
not smoke. A Heelander by descent, he
never touches whisky. If he hadn’t been
. coddled in the tropical climate of Papua and
North Queensland, he would long ago have
died of his virtues.
“Boswell” : With due respect to a “North-
ern Democrat” (B. 30/l/’l3), the Denham
Ministry isn’t half as strong as it looks on
paper. There are many sources of trouble.
Digby has got himself and the Courier into
a mess with the sugar-growers. The mer-
chants of South Queensland are screeching
in Paget’s ear that they want the same low
rate of carriage (55.) for every 37 miles of
railway as that obtaining on the Port Alma-
Rockhampton line. Denham at Adelaide
declared his allegiance to the Meat Trust,
which is feared even by Tories, the Demo-
cratic press having recently drawn their
attention to the fact that Uncle Sam is now
paying Mr. Trust Is. 7d. per lb. retail for
his bit of neck steak. On the other side, though
it has its faults in plenty, the “whole hog”
Labor party in Bananaland is Big Aus-
tralian to the core, and rightly looks upon a
State Frighter as a dangerous lunatic. And
Big Australianism is a growing cult, which
covers a lot of smaller sins. So, one way
and another, if the Laborites could precipi-
tate a State general election, in the present
state of the public mind, T. J. Ryan would
be a lot nearer the Premiership than he is
now.
Died recently, under very tragic circum-
stances, the manager of a Queensland
farmers’ milling co. An investigation of the
company’s affairs disclosed a deficiency of
nearly £6OOO. If the secretary of a Labor
Union had passed out under such circum-
stances, how the Tory press would have
blazoned the glad tidings !
Queensland is a country of strange juggles.
Explosives coming to Brisbane must be un-
loaded at Pinkenba and brought up bp
lighter. Heavy penalties are provided for
violation of the rule. But explosives bound
for Rockhampton must be unloaded at Port
Alma and sent on by rail. Kidston was re-
sponsible for this little bit of jugglery.
John Ivemp has been appointed City En-
gineer of Brisbane, where he has been en-
gineering for the Water and Sewerage
Board. Thomas Kirk, his predecessor, has
gone out on a gratuity equal to six months’
salary. If Kemp has the ability to effect
reforms and has a leaning that way, a great
opportunity is his in Brisbane. But he will
probably have to kill a few aldermen first.
Passed away a few days ago, aged 77, Geo.
Mant, a typical old-time Queensland squat-
ter. - Mant came to Queensland nearly 60
years ago, and was identified nearly the
whole of his long life with Gigoomgan station
and the successful breeding of shorthorn
cattle. He was firmly convinced that Aus-
tralia under Labor is booked for political
Sbeol, and he leaves a numerous family in
the full enjoyment of so much of the dis-
aster as has thusfar arrived.
“Doctor” Kidston alleges that “but for
certain personal reasons he would have
enjoyed a contest for Wide Bay with Mr.
Fisher.” No doubt. Sixty-five years and
the surrender of a snug sinecure of a thou-
sand per annum are very weighty “personal
reasons” indeed.
Home Sec. Appel has got a new ornament
for the outside of his waistcoat. There was
but one bridge left in Australia where a toll-
gate was still in business; this was over, the
Burnett, at Bundaberg. The other day this
sole surviving toll was abolished, and the
last threepenny-bit that was taken was
mounted in gold and passed on to the Mini-
ster.
“D.L.M.”: It is hardly an exaggeration
to say that in pear-ridden districts a
stalk of pear springs from every emu’s
footprint. When left to itself the seed
might not come up for years, and may per-
haps never grow at all, but after passing
through a bird it germinates immediately.
Some little time ago, 490 emus were yarded
in a flock in West-Central Queensland. The
yard was of wire-netting, 12ft. high, and
leading to it were two calico wings, a few
miles in length. Thirty horsemen spread
themselves out over the pear-and-emu in-
fested country, and turned all emus towards
the wings. Once they had the flock within
the calico arms they had only to galloD be-
hind. The emus were sold at a price which
covered all expenses, and the purchaser
made a handsome profit by converting the
flock into emu oil. The emu used to be a
protected bird, but it is now clear that if
human beings are to retain Queensland and
oust the prickly pear the emu must go.
The Great Illusion.
(Suggested, by Norman Angell.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Ape who long had teased the jungle tenants
With simian trick and guile and mad manoeuvres,
Whose dearest joy in life
Was dropping mighty nuts on mighty skulls
And peppering helpless hides with prickly fruits
That fell like hail and stung like Hell, and scattered
The wildings east and west,
Decided he would drop his aimless life,
Give up his friv’lous gibes and senseless jabber
And for the benefit of Life at large, and also
To cultivate the Soul the Lord had lent him,
Would turn Philosopher.
One morn he bent from out a bafiyan mighty
And thus harangued the Forest.
“ ’Tis plain,” he said, “that war among the beasties
Is at an end for various weighty reasons.
Imprimis, yonder Bear is stout and lusty,
And used to feed on fruits and roots and honey,
Of which he hath a most astounding store;
The Crocodile grows fat on roots of lotus
And finds the flags that fill the Nilus shallows
Afford a charming meal;
The Hippo, and the Elephant are peaceful;
The grim Rhinoceros surveys his wallow
With calm, contented smile;
And as to them with teeth and claw who raven,
Their ravening days are done,
For but yest’re’en I heard the Libyan lion
Propose to sundry Leopard folk and -Tigers
That they should blunt their teeth and swear upon
the altar
To ravage never more ;
Wherefore, I do conclude that War is over,
The Wolf and Lamb will lie down now in clover
And Peace will rule for aye.
And as his long harangue he gravely ended,
A burst of wild applause-rose up to greet him
Amid the enthusiastic jubilation
, Of every Carnivore.
Next day they named him Lord High Arbitrator,
Bade him descend to earth
And give his laws to all assembled creatures,
Who then and there would vow
To do his best without demur or murmur
No matter what it were.
The Ape came down.
“Now first I do decree,” he said, “that Leo,
The Forest King, do set a high example
To all his Subjects here in Court assembled,
And let me cut his claws.
My teeth are white and sharp, and I will straightway
Take up his mighty paws and trim them neatly
With great, exceeding joy.”
The Lion smiled ; his bloody claws extended
To our Philosopher, who bent to business
Without delay. A furious roar succeeded,
And then there came a scuffle and a scramble
And forty beasts were fighting in a fury
For forty simian bones.
MORAL.
From treetops preach your Peace without a pause,
But don’t get down to clip your neighbor’s claws.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 1, 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
Governor Macgregor took a day off yester-
day, and went to see the inter-State cricket
match. A collection of country shows
awaits his attention in the newly-hatched
month, and his time-docket is expected to
show some excuse for his wages.
Canon and Mrs. Pughe, with their pretty
and popular daughter Peggy, to-day left for
Sydney, where they will take an Orient
boat (or as much as they can get of it)
for London.
The State barge Lucinda wears the
pathetic expression of a very broken-down
gentlewoman. She certainly cannot help
her age, but she might be given a coat of
paint to advantage. And when that is done
she should be removed ftom her usual resting
place in one of the most unsavory parts of
the city. The day the Denmans literally
shook the dust of this city off their feet,
six rubbish carts occupied prominent posi-
tions on the frontage, and waved 428 striped
odors in farewell. Every distinguished visi-
tor is similarly mistreated.
Dr. and Mrs. Jackson gave a big at-home
at their gunyah at Toowong last night as
a farewell treat to daughter Morag, who will
leave for England so soon as her marriage
is signed and sealed next week. The hostess
wore black, and the bride-to-be was adorned
in pink. Most of the young things who go
to these glorifications were present, as well
as a fair representation of the elder folk
who don’t go so often on account of rheuma-
tism.
That popular medico, Dr. Hirschfeld, is
on his way back to Brisbane on the Scharn-
horst. Mrs. Hirschfeld is back from her
Melbourne visit, and is endeavoring, to cope
with the vagaries of carpenters and plas-
terers who are renovating the place against
the doctor’s return.
Next week will be a busy one for clergy-
men, bell-ringers, cab-drivers and waitresses.
On Monday, Ida Clowes, and one Webster,
of Sydney, will murmur the usual “I wills”
to a large and sympathetic audience. On
luesday, the Roe-Jackson wedding will
(D.v.) happen at 2.30; and at six o’clock
Miss Collins, of Tamrookum, and Lieutenant
Harris will put a match to the torch of
Hymen. Hard-grafting curates are hardly
ever out of their clerical bowyangs these
days, and are threatening to apply for a
wages board.
Two more engagements. The first prom-
ises pretty Cecile Taylor, daughter of Mrs.
Lumley Hill, to Whittingham, M.L.C. In
point, of age the prospective bridegroom has
the disadvantage of about a score of years,
but in tastes the two are well matched. The
lady is an ardent horsewoman, and an all-
round good sport. The second engagement
brackets “Chook” Powell (about as awful
as “Pip” Powell in the way of fancy names)
with the future of Miss Gladys Hobday, who
has inherited a goodly share of the artistic
talent of her father, the late J. S. Hobday.
The hostess at a recent “function” ex-
tended invitations to mere presswomen in
these graceful words: “You may come out
and look at the grounds, but you may not
come into the house!” She didn’t say what
was the matter with the house, but you
can’t be too careful these days, my dear.
So the forewarned took no risks.
This State had rather more than its share
of accidents last month. Since I wrote you
about 20 workmen were injured and one
killed at Balmoral, through the misfiring of
a fuse. On Wednesday, a train ran off the
lines at Murphy s Creek, between xpswich
and Toowoomba. The breakdown gang was
hard at work clearing the wreckage when
another train dashed into the party, killing
four outright and wounding others, of whom
three subsequently died. The usual inquiry
is to be held. It would be more satisfactory
if there was some inquiry before these ex-
traordinary mistakes are made. Another
derailment resulted in one more man losing
his life. It has been a gloomy and un-
profitable week for too many unlucky people.
The Adelaide Steamship Co. has ih th
stranding of the Innamincka, at the m™!'
of the Mowbray River, had the fi
misfortune which the Ravens prophesied r
it after the loss of the Yongala. I am told
that Innamincka” is abo. for “White >
grave.” If so, it has failed to live up W
reputation on this occasion, as no lives w lS
lost when the cyclone, which has been raein
up north like a demented fiend, bumped th
vessel inland.
Yance Jones, of the Commercial Bank of
Australia, is bound for Japan. He will
early next month, and on arrival at Yoko
hama and other important places in Nippon"
will be shown over the city and harbor works
and permitted to camp under the forts and
take soundings and tracings and have a good
time generally. That is, if there’s anvthim,
in reciprocity, he will.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
From a Northern professional man:
Queensland’s immigration system has degenerated’
into a fraud and snare—a fraud mostly. I ami
told on high authority of immigrants who
have died of consumption within six weeks
of their arrival in Brisbane, and of others
who have gone to fill Diamantina and Dalby and
Stanthorpe sanatoriums. To-day I found a consumn
trve immigrant, who had already been at Dalbv and
Diamantina sanatoriums, and is now advised in
again shut herself up in one of these institutions «
she is momentarily in danger of haemorrhage from’the-
Pemberton Ellibtt Walcott, Queensland’s,
new federal Public Service inspector, is 44.
years of age, and has been connected with
the administration of the Public Service ever
since the Commonwealth was born. Prior to
that he toiled in the Brisbane postal service
He has guided the steps of three previous in-
spectors—Bourne (retired), Ilealy (dead)
and Skewes (transferred to Melb.) —and has
done it so well that he now takes up the-
reins himself. Incidentally,, he gets a rise of,'
£340 per annum.
“Boswell” from Brisbane :
J h ° _ the Hi ff h Court recently counted the'
JN.b.W. influx of Criminals Act out on appeal, be-
cause one State has no power to leg:slate against
the citizens of another State, a similar Act is in full
blast in Queensland, and men are still being lassooedii
and convicted under it. A prisoner at St. Helena;
who had been hunted' down under this Dog Act or
whatever it is called;, the other, day got his chance
and assaulted the arresting “demon” as he was stroll-
ing through the. workshops. For this the man was
pulled up before, the gaoli beaks and sentenced, in
camera, to 12 months’ extra, without any pretence of
publicity. But 1 don’t expect the Denham Govern-
ment, which lives,- mostly by the policeman’s trun-
cheon, to right such abuses. Hitherto, its legisla-
tion has been largely directed towards protecting
Commissioner Cahill from some imaginary doom. At
present that official is the onlv one of his species
under the protection of a special Act. For that rea-
son he may yet be known as “Pelorus Jack.”
“Condamine”: “Don Q.” (9./l/’l3) nfi s .
states the case when he says that the land-
owner in Queensland can do as he likes with
his prickly pear. All land selected in the
last few years carries an obligation to dis-
perse the vegetable. One thing that cer-
tainly should be done is to penalise holders
of clean country if they allow the pear
to encroach. On the western line, paddocks
that were clean three years ago are well
carpeted now. In most cases the owner
lives away, and lets the ground to a tenant.
The magnitude of the problem of eradication
mav be judged from this. There are large
areas, over 50 miles from present settle-
ments on the Moonie, for instance—where
even a wild pig cannot penetrate. A scheme
of _ eradication that has every appearance of
being It is now being tried at Dullacca,
where a Yankee chemist named Roberts has
succeeded in applying a gas that has been
proved able to kill the leaves and branches,
though its effect on the main stem and bulb
is not yet known.

Missing Friends.
PURSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division
of the High Court of Justice, made in the Mat-
ter of the ESTATE of ROBERT TOWNSEND HIP-
PISLEY, Deceased, Hippisley v. Hippisley, 1912, H.
1515, and dated the 24th October, 1912, the follow-
ing inquiry is directed, viz.: An inquiry whether
Charles Ernest Hippisley, named in the will of Robert
Townsend Hippisley, is living or dead, and, if dead,
when he died, and whether he left any and what will,
and whether he was ever married, and if so when and
to whom, and whether there were any children of the
said marriage, and who are his legal personal repre-
sentatives.
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN, that the said
Charles Ernest Hippisley and all persons claiming
through or by descent from him are, personally or by
their solicitors, on or before the 24th day of June,
1913, to come in and prove their claims at the
chambers of Mr. Justice Joyce and Mr. Justice Eve,
Room 692, Royal Courts of Justice, Strand, London,
England, or in default thereof they will be peremp-
torily excluded from the benefit of the said order.
Wednesday, the 9th day of July, 1913, at half-past
two in the afternoon, at the said chambers, is ap-
pointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
Dated the 17th day of December, 1912.
A. KEEN, Master.
ROBBINS and CO., 218 Strand, W.C., London,
for Abbot, Pope and Abbot, Bristol, Solicitors
for the Plaintiffs.
Note. —The above-named Charles Ernest Hippisley,
who was a son of the testator, Robert Townsend Hip-
pisley, a solicitor, of Bristol, England, is now, if
alive, about 51 vears of age. He went to Australia
in Februarv, 1889, and was there employed by Messrs.
Robert Reid and Co., and afterwards, till May, 1897,
by Messrs. Sands and McDougall (Limited). He
resided at Rochester Lodge, Collins-place, Melbourne.
He left Melbourne in May, 1897, by the Orient steam-
ship Orizaba, for London, where he stayed one or
two months, and is believed to have resided at 123
Fulham Road, London. In October, 1897, he left
London, and until April, 1898, was employed by
Messrs. J. Gurlick and Co., of Alderley Street, Cape
Town, South Africa, and resided at 43 St. John
Street, Cape Town. He returned to Melbourne in
1898, and expressed his intention of going to Queens-
land or to Sydney. He is alleged to have been seen
in London by an old schoolfellow in 1908.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum,
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot the ear
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
horn deaf, we
cannot pretend - ■
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order forrn below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

GROW HAIR
A Simple, Safe and Reliable Way that Produces Surprising Results.
NEW PREPARATION CAUSES BIG STIR AMONG HAIR specialists.
The above clearly shows Results from a Short Course of my True Hair-Growing Compound.
10,000 FREE TRIAL BOXES.
Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
| lam not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
1 had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of,, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip,,l fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Dentistry has conferred an enormous blessing upon
the Australian people. To no profession have they
greater reason to show gratitude. Look about you
and you will find there are few indeed in this coun-
try who do not own increased comfort at meals,
better digestions, better looks, and better health
generally, to the much-abused dentist.
—Speech at a Melb. Dentists’ Conference.
Somebody in the U.S. Treasury has invented a
sort of whizzing, light-fingered laundry appliance
which, within the space of 2 i minutes, souses,
wrings and irons paper money, and ejects it in a
perfectly clean and crisp condition for re-issue to
the public. The Treasury, which formerly fur-
naced the precious rubbish, reckons the machine
will save it a million dollars a year. The uom-
monwealth Treasury might profitably interest itself
in the device, and decree frequent washings for all
the notes that pass in over the bank counters. The
insanitary rags that are doled out by Australian
tellers are enough to make a dosser shudder. And
yet clean people have to accept them and
possibly infect tneir clothes by carrying tiiem rounu.
So used are the public to this dog’s-eared money that
the occasional crisp new note is regarded by most
with suspicion, and diagnosed by the inexperienced
as a neat advertisement for a picture show or
patent medicine. *
“John Shearer’’: Mr. C. J. Hutton (B. 16/1/’l3),
talking for ;his co., belts the pig-growers’ co-opera-
tive efforts, and says that the private co. is the pro-
ducers’ real friend. To help prove his case he men-
tions that during August his co. paid Vic. farmers
7.72 d. per lb. dressed weight. I’m glad to hear it.
Unfortunately I’m not a Victorian, but a Queensland
grower, and know that during the first week in
August, at Nambour, when the J. O. Hutton Prop,
was the only buyer in the yards, the porkers were
bought on the basis of 3£d. per lb. dressed weight.
Ditto at Landsborough. Incidentally, if you’ll turn
up the Commonwealth Year Book, you’ll see, under
the heading “Pig and Bacon Industry,” Mr. Knibbs
alleges that in Queensland, “allowing for a trading
capital equal to the amount invested in machinery,
plants, etc., the interest return [of the ham-and
baeon-curers] is 74 per cent, on £126,664.” In
N.S.W. the interest return is 20 per cent, on
£126,323. In N.S.W. the Byron Bay and other co-
operative concerns have gone into the ham- and
bacon-curing business very largely. Private Enter-
prise does nearly all of it in Queensland. Who is
the “real friend” of the producer—the manufacturer
who makes 74 per cent, out of the producer’s raw
material or the one who pays so good a price that
there remains only 20 per cent, for profit and de-
preciation?
* * * *
The readiness of the confidence worker in seizing
an opening is demonstrated in the case of Merrin
versus Corcoran and another, which arises out of the
misfortunes of an affectionate tinsmith of South Mel-
bourne, who made himself acquainted with a nice
girl named Jervis while mixed bathing. It was a
case of love on sight, and a brief courtship was fol-
lowed by a sudden engagement. Then Milly’s duties
as nursemaid with a peripatetic family took her to
Ballarat for a time. During her temporary absence,
Merrin, the susceptible tinsmith, was waited upon
by a stout, distressed, middle-aged person, who ex-
plained that she was Milly’s mother in urgent need
of £2. The gallant Merrin supplied the poor
woman’s need, and next day a portly male stranger
called in a state of mental agony, and said he was
Milly’s father. He wanted £3 to save the old home.
He got it. Within the week the anguished female
was back in tears and tribulation. It appeared that
Milly’s poor father, in a burst of impetuosity accel-
erated by beer, had done damage to a policeman, and
£5 was wanted to bail him out. Merrin, wishing
to do his best for all parties, provided the fiver. There
were two smaller demands after that, but Milly has
now returned, with an assurance that she has no
parents, and lias had none for many years ; and one
Corcoran and another are wanted to explain matters.
That somebody has reduced broaching to a fine art
is evidenced bv the rows that have been proceeding
in Adelaide and Melbourne over the cargo of the s.s.
Beltana. The thieves seem to be the most easy-
going, leisurely workers in existence. Not only are
cases of softgoods, furs, razors, etc., opened and
emptied, but when the contents have been removed,
the packing is replaced and the cases fastened down
again so methodically that experienced carters and
expert packers can detect nothing wrong. A case
of silverware and another of furs, for example, were
shipped in London. On reaching Melbourne the
cautious carter noticed one nail missing from its
place, and refused to accept delivery. That ease
contained only waste paper. The silverware case
showed absolutely no mark of tools, yet all it con-
tained was a couple of ladies’ hats, evidently from
some softgoods consignment. Four silvered vases
were found in a case that ought to have held nothing
but drapery. Besides the mystery of how a gang of
thieves finds time and opportunity to ovehaul cargo in
this thorough fashion, another problem arises—how
do they get the goods ashore? £l5O worth of furs
can’t be stowed in the coat pocket; neither can a
man hump 2cwt. of razors with ease.
* * * *
Maoriland is to have a cut-in at the French meat
trade. French law is peculiar in that it requires
heart, lungs and liver to be left in the carcase. A
number of mutton growers in Hawkes Bay and the
South Island have combined to supply a consignment
dressed as required, and the frozen flesh will get
forward on its journey next March.
* * * *
One of the funniest and not the least business-like
of a hundred and one books of snippings that have
issued from Cole’s Book Arcade (Melb.) is entitled
“Mother.” A portrait of E. W. Cole at the age of
81 is the frontispiece. The genial old identity calls
himself the “editor,” and he dedicates the volume
“to the Memory of his Mother, in gratitude for all
her loving care.” The first of the texts which the
purveyor of maudlin sentiment dedicates to the
memory of his long-lost parent is “A mother’s love is
best of all (West African),” and, for the rest, the
book is a collection of paragraphs, exclamations,
verses and storiettes with mother for their subject
every time. A number of old woodcut illustrations
of mother in all her phases are really interesting.
Most of them date back to the age of crinolines, and
the picture of a hooped mother saying “Goo-goo” to a
very old-fashioned infant is enough to make even
eugels weep.
One time I lived in some content—
I ran a boarding house at Kew.
My humble boarders came and went,
And all were glad to feed on stew.
I would the dogs were ’neath the loam
Who cursed my house and wrecked my home!
The boarders whom I lodged at Kew
Had teeth it was not safe to gnash.
They had to eat what they could chew,
And very partial were to hash.
I gave them hash, I stewed them plums,
And aye was careful of their gums.
With teeth of bone and teeth of gold
They’ve given diners courage new
On tough bull-meat to take a hold,
And tear the stout rump-steak in two.
Then meat went up, but sorrow’s crown
Was in the way the meat went down.
The empty mouths of all the crew
That gathered at my table-side
They filled with grinders hard and true
To tear the chicken bone and hide.
Instead of grace, with phrases rude
I cried for mercy on the food.
Too clean they picked my wretched bones,
My substance they devoured all day.
I sit here on the wayside stones,
A withered creature, and I pray
That they may taste the Devil’s brew
Who turned the boarder’s soul from stew.
VICTORIA.

ILIOUSNESS
BODY RACKED BY RETCHING 8 CHEST PAINS.
SUFFERED 5 YEARS-OTHER REMEDIES USELESS
ONVINCING testimony to the wonderful cura-
tive virtues of Bile Beans is that of Mrs.
A. Nicholson, of 50 Campbell Street, Glebe,
Sydney, who says:—“For over five years I
was a great sufferer from violent bilious attacks,
indigestion and severe headaches. Awful pains
between the shoulders and in the chest tortured
me, and my appetite disappeared. Terrible fits
of retching weakened me, and my liver became
sluggish, and 1 felt weary and had no desire for
work. Although I tried many remedies for my

The Grey Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T T’S the steady man’s the good man,”
I my mither said to me;
’Twas the good man, the grey man,
my mither picked for me—
Snug cot on the loaning, an’ kine an’ chicks
and corn,
The mellow glow o’ sunset instead o’ wild
red morn.
“Steady man and staunch man,” my mither
said to me —
Burn betwixt the level braes, an’ no’ the
open sea!
Peat an’ coal the fire feeds (some hae only
Glass-pane in our window frame, an’ meat
an’ a’ to eat —
Silk gown for my Sunday wear, a feather in
my hat —
Pit-brow wench an’ farmer’s lass a’ can envy
that.
“Man wi’ siller in the pouch, that’s the ane
for thee;
Steady man an’ grey man,” my mither said
to me.
In the long June gloaming the moths go
breathing by—
Feathers dropt from angels’ wings on a
golden sky;
And in all the farm lands up among the
wheat
Button-bright the poppies grow, warm and
drowsy-sweet.
Turning o’ the stocking-heel (while the lads
again
Are hand-fasted with the girls in the bow-
ery lane) —•
Silly thoughts a wife to have, wi’ her own
good house
Fast as bee in clover bed, or in grain a
mouse.
Oh, the moth has drifting wings, an’ the
moths are free!
“The grey man’s the good man” — and that
is a’ for me!
Green salt pools a-glimmer, little wrinkling
waves,
Honey-colored moonlight, where the grey
tide laves;
Beaches white a-shimmer, white an’ wild an’
wet;
Rainbows o’ the foam spray whiles I can’t
forget!
Butterflies in daisy fields, gulls across the
sea....
The four walls, the swept hearth, the grey
man for me!
Winter, when the earth outside, white as
dead maid’s breast,
Lifts the hummocks of the snow to the
frozen west;
Bubbling gases in the slack, gay as young
desire,
So I see two bonnie eyes blazing in the
fire;
Hair as black as broken coal, lips as red an'
fine....
And a ghost creeps from the ash, burns a
kiss on mine.
Sits an’ nods my own good man, ’neath his
grey roof tree;
Sleep wi’out a stir for him — an' the dreams
for me!
“It’s the steady man’s the good man,” my
mither said to me;
That’s the good man, the grey man, my
mither picked for me;
Snug cot on the loaning, an’ kine an’ chicks
an’ corn,
The mellow glow o’ sunset against a wild
red morn.
“Steady man an’ staunch man,” my mither
said to me —
Burn betwixt the level banks.... out, ah
....the wide....wide sea!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
A Set of Furs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MRS. TRIBBENS’S boarders were
distributed about the verandah after
the Saturday mid-day dinner, when
word was passed along that disim-
guished-looking stranger in the top hat anu
frock coat, who had just passed up the
street, had returned and was making tor
the front gate. All conversation ceased as
he walked slowly up the steps and rang the
bell. Mrs. Tribbens herself answered it and
seemed puzzled at his first question. Ihen
she gave a perceptible start of recollection
and hurriedly asked him to step inside, tn a
few minutes, she reappeared alone on the
verandah. , , , ,
“What is it, Mrs. Tribbens? they pleaded
in whispers. , ,
The landlady looked about her nervously.
“He’s a lawyer, and he’s come to see Alice,
the maid.” ~
“Alice!” they chorused blankly. What
on earth for?” , , ,
“I don’t know. But he says he has good
news for her, so I expect it must be money.
Looks both envious and incredulous were
exchanged. “Money! Just fancy.
“Some people do have luck !” drearily mur-
mured Miss Gummer, the Post-Office girl.
She and her fiance, Mr. were tnen
scraping and saving in anticipation of the
manifold expenses of matrimony, and heie
money was simply showering on the mai ,
who already possessed a decent banki 0
account. ..
A few minutes passed, and then all weii
startled by peals of loud laughter from ie
sacred drawing-room. “Juft listen to t a
girl!” cried the women. Whatever will t
lawyer think of her?” , „ fVI „
They were shocked later on when the
giggling maid emerged from the ball entrance
with the abashed visitor, and brightly waved
him off the premises with a friendly, We ,
ta-ta for th’ present. Be good. ,
As soon as the top-hatted one had escaped
they crowded round the lucky girl,
started to congratulate her in a P at ™uising
way. “You must be glad! thev moaned.
“How perfectly wonderful to come into all
that money!” , ..
Alice laughed at them happily. >
ain’t so much —a matter of six undred an
twenty punds.”
“Oh, how lovely!” „ „
“Yes,” continued the happy savage care
lesssly, “it ain’t so bad, but it ain t
prise. It’s from me uncle s estate—l knoo
it was coinin’ some time.”
\Vhy, you're quite a rich girl now! You
wont work any more, will you?” cried poor
Miss Uummer.
“Why, o’ course I will, Miss Gum’r! 1
aia ’t, enou sh to stop workin’, and, !
what s more, 1 wouldn’t leave Mrs. Tribbens j
not fer worlds! She’s been like a mother to
me, an’ besides, I wouldn’t know what to do
with meself, any’ow.”
There was some subdued applause at this;
but it didn’t exactly fire the enthusiasm of
her female listeners.
“If 1 came in to some money,” announced
a bleak spinster freezingly, “I would go for
a trip round the word and Broaden My
Mind!”
This speech was applauded to the echo.
Alice, the practical, wiped her hands on
her apron. “Aw, I’ll just put it in the
bank for a bit, till I make up me mind.
I’m all muddled-like just now—-don’t seem
to know what I’m doin’, what with the
fuss an’ all. Money’s rotten in a way—wor-
ries you an’ brings sorrer, I think. I don’t
know anythin’ about ’vestments, and shares,
an’ such; an’ propperty’s nothin’ but payin’
rates, an’ gettin’ the drains seen to, an arf
the time the people won’t pay up. I’ll just
leave it in the bank fer a bit.” Having sad-
dened herself with these reflections, she re-
turned to her kitchen and left the idle women
to wear the subject to rags.
The eminent Mr. Bodger, senior male
boarder of the establishment, encountered
the excited gossips on his way in to tea, and
was immediately bailed up and overwhelmed
with full particulars of the legacy. After
considering the six different accounts, he ex-
pressed his unbounded admiration of Alice’s
prudence, and undertook to give her the bene-
fit of his ripe experience. “I always liked
that girl,” he concluded ponderously. “No
fal-lals or foolery about her at all. She’s
too smart to throw away the money 7 on trips
or dresses. She’s got a good head —knows
her proper place, and doesn’t want to leave
it. Damme, I wish all women were as sen-
sible !” As usual, he left his female audi-
ence somewhat annoved. Bodger’s down-
right style was apt to be disconcerting.
After the evening meal, Mrs. Dodd-Gar-
stead, a vitriolic, overdressed woman who
spent all her life grading the world and prov-
ing conclusively that one-half of it wasn’t
fit to black the other half’s boots, expressed
it as her firm opinion that the girl should
be instantly discharged, as she would not
now be worth her salt as a servant. Money,
she explained, always had that effect on the
lower classes. Anyhow, it was intolerable
that, while one had to scrape and save, the
servant was simply reeking . of money, and
must be sneering and laughing behind one s
back. It was too idiotic altogether—the
Law should see to it that money went to
the gentlefolk who knew how to use it, not
to servant girls who were only fit to wash
up dishes and make beds.
After this outburst, the boarders on the
verandah were silent for a space. Bodger,
who had listened to it in stunned silence,
was clearing his throat preparatory to de-
fending the good sense of Alice, when a
giggling female courier arrived with the
news that the lucky one was now talking
wildly about Paris over the washing-up tin,
and threatening to get' into the best society
and go to balls and receptions every night
of her life. There was a burst of malicious
laughter, and Bodger sank back in his deck
chair and fell to gloomily stroking his beard.
“What did I tell you?” snapped Mrs.
Dodd-Garstead. „ ,
“If I had that money,” began Mr. Dadclip
thoughtfully, “I would buy a nice little. place
within handv distance of town and go in for
poultry.” He unfolded his scheme at great
length, and showed that there would be
heaps of money in it. It was a rather good
round game, and they each took a hand in
it with more or less success. By the time
they had quite finished, someone else ar-
rived with the intelligence . that Alice was
now talking severely of giving it all to the
church, because she wanted to do a bit ot
good in her life, and what was the use of
going to Gay Faree if you couldn t talk the
language and didn’t know how to be gay like
the rest! _,
Whilst Mrs. Dodd-Garstead was working
off the rage generated by this communication,
Bodger heaved himself out of his chair and
went off, rudely grunting something to the
effect that no confounded woman was nt
to be trusted with a penny piece.
And all this time, Lempson and Miss
Gummer, whose wedding-day was now in
sight and who were, consequently, very ner-
vous and worried about many things, especi-
Illy furniture and finance, were wretched y
calculating if, by any chance, Alice would
consider a proposition to build them a small
cottage and let them pay it off as rent.
They agreed that it would be a splendid
investmlnt for her ; but neither of them
could bear to broach the subject to the only
capitalist m the house.
During the next three days, Alice got more
inexpert advice about the investment of her
money than a shipload of heiresses could have
absorbed. She was implored to start tea
rooms, poultry farms, lolly shops,, boarding-
houses. boatsheds, hatshops, manicure pa
fors, registry offices, toy shops, news agen-
cies billiard saloons, boatsheds and other
enterprises. Mr. Dadclip grimly estimated
that she would require £75.000 capital to fol
to all this advice
used up a Rood deal ot her working time,
and usually, when she escaped from some
financial genius to the kitchen it was oMy
to reject the heart and hand and horse ana
cart of some love-lorn tradesman. Ihe
butcher was repelled the first day; and being
„ full blooded man, used to having his own
wav he ever afterwards dashed the meat
down on the kitchen table in a. wild and
desnerate fashion. The other kitchen-door

anon there was no lausrhter or chaff to be

Some poisons warn you with a sudden smarting - pain and violent sickness.
Not so uric acid for uric acid poisoning - is slow and deceptive. It may go on in the
system foi years without the patient realising his danger —that is why it is so serious, and why it
causes more suffering and disease than any other poison.
There is always a certain amount of uric acid getting into the blood, but we have nothing
to fear from it so long as the kidneys are active, for healthy kidneys filter this dangerous poison
out of the blood and dispose of it with the waste water, through the bladder. That is the great
work of the kidneys—to keep the blood pure.
But when the kidneys get weakened by a cold, overwork, stooping about, working in
diaughts or extreme heat, or excesses of any kind, they cannot filter the uric acid out of the
blood. That is how most dangerous diseases begin.
. You may not notice anything the matter at first, except a drowsy, heavy and oppressed feeling, an occasional dull
ache m the back, and the necessity of emptying the bladder more often than usual. At this stage a few doses of Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills will generally remove all traces of uric acid poison and kidney weakness. But later on, when the
uric acid gets settled in the system and causes lumbago, rheumatism, sciatica, sharp pains in the loins and back, gravel,
urinary obstruction, inflammation of the bladder, and the swellings of dropsy, a thorough course of Doan’s Pills may be
necessary before the uric acid is disposed of and the kidneys are restored to health.

and flushes them out, and releases the accumulated water in dropsy.

The shocking announcement —-it was made
at dinner—instantly put a stop to all eating.
Knives and forks fell with a clatter that
suggested a beaten regiment laying down its
arms.
The first coherent exclamation came from
Bodger. It was a deep and heartfelt “Good
God!” —the cry of a strong man who
had just received his death-blow.
Miss Gnmmer, tired and fretted with end-
less arguments and calculations, shrieked
faintly and dabbed at her lips with a small
handkerchief.
Lempson, the bridegroom-elect, went pale
and nodded his head hopelessly.
Mrs. Tribbens fanned herself weakly with
the tea cosy.
Mr. Dadclip, the poultry enthusiast,
settled his glasses on lxis nose and looked
wildly about him.
But Mrs. Dodd-Garstead was the hardest
hit of all. When she realised the full signi-
ficance of the grinning maid’s news, she
rose slowly from her chair and gasped, “How
dare you ! The idea of you —a servant —
darinp to buy such furs —things that only
ladies should wear! Oh, how dare you!
You ought to he thoroughly ashamed of your-
self. You don’t know your place ! I—l. . . .
Oil, I can’t bear it!” And she fell back in
her chair, and, leaning her elbows on the
table, supported her distorted face in her
hands, while she wept with the greatest pos-
sible enthusiasm into the remains of her
pudding.
Within a week Alice was the chief amuse-
ment of the suburb, then in the throes of a
dull summer. Whenever ordered out on an
emergency errand, she would say archly, “I
think I'll just go and put on me furs !” And
she would, no matter how murderously hot
the day. Some of the remarks wrung from
Mrs. Dodd-Garstead by these bear-like forag-
ing excursions into the blazing heat were
classics in their unpleasant way.
Then Alice achieved fame easily by faint-
ing one morning in the street whilst hurry-
ing home with a parcel of sausages, and
being borne tenderly to the hospital in a pass-
ing motor. A Sunday paper got hold of the
story of the Girl in Ermine, and with photo-
graphy made an interesting four columns of
it. Alice’s full history was set forth, and she
was honorably bracketed with a leading
musical-comedy actress as the owner of the
best furs in Sydney. Felicity brought her
all sorts of friends and enemies. Artistic,
comfort-loving people praised her for her ap-
preciation of the nice things of life; clerics
denounced her from their pulpits for her
dangerous love of luxury. Some charitable
people were furious with her for spending all
the legacy on herself; a lot of uncharitable
folk said she never had a legacy. Amongst
the latter class was Mrs. Dodd-Garstead,
who, such was her rage and envy, heroicallv
disregarded all the circumstantial evidence
and bluntly intimated that she knew the girl
was no better than she should be. She
seemed almost unable to control herself when
the Most Talked-of Maid in Sydney r>p-
proached; and when that paragraphed
female hurriedly retreated, her manner sug-
gested that it was as much as she could do
to keep from pursuing her and doing her a
deadly injury. All day long she watched
Alices comings and goings like a cat, and
snarled over them to the exclusion of every
other subject. The set of furs was wearing
her out body and soul, and all hands grew
apprehensive. Alice herself tottered to and
fro, wearing the look of one who waits for a
typhoon to break. The snowy, black-tailed
splendor had become a curse.
There was exactly two weeks and three
days of this sort of thing before the tragedy
happened.
It was just before tea, when Alice was
busy with the table, preparations, that Mrs.
Dodd-Garstead ran in from the yard, shriek-
ing that the house was on fire. The dining-
room rose as one boarder and ran to make
sure she wasn’t lying again. It was quite
true. . Smoke was pouring from the window
of Alice’s room, and the flicker of flames
could be seen through the glass.
As luck would have it, Lempson had been
lazy, as usual, and had left the garden hose
screwed on to the tap, instead of uncoupling
it and putting it away. The ladder, too, was
lying handy ; so it didn’t take him long to
climb up and get a stream playing on the
fire. A quarter of an hour later the house
was safe, but Alice’s room was a charred
and evil-smelling den.
Mrs. Dodd-Garstead, very bright-eyed and
flushed, hovered on the outskirts of the
stunned crowd, murmuring consoling tilings
and giving, on the whole, a very fair imita-
tion of a sympathetic woman, shocked by an
overwhelming catastrophe.
White-faced Alice flew upstairs as soon as
the fire was out to assess the damages.
When she came down, after five minutes’ ab-
sence, Mrs. Dodd-Garstead was the very first
to approach her. “Your coat, dear,” she
cooed, “is it really burnt?”
Bodger. in the background, grasped his
beard and stared hard at the back of her
neck.
The stricken Alice seemed genuinely
touched by this exhibition of sympathy.
“Yes’m,” she panted rueful]v, “it’s burnt orl
right—burnt all to cinders !”
“Oh, you poor thing!”
Alice nodded, misty-eyed. “I dunno! T
’spose it wuz a judgment on me for wantin’
to go to Paree an’ be a gay. Ah. well! I
won’t buy no. more furs when I get me
money from th’ ’nsuranee company.”
Mrs. Dodd-Garstead fell back two steps;
her jaw fell about two feet. “Do you mean
to say !”
“Yes, mum. Wasn’t it lucky!”
Mrs. Dodd-Garstead expressed her delight
by rushing straight up to her room and hav-
ing violent hysterics. When she had quite
done, Mrs. Tribbens, attended by Messrs.
Bodger and Dempson, A.D.C.’s, waited on
her and presented an ultimatum, in as digni-
fied a manner as possible, through the key-
hole. She and her husband were to be out
of the house by eight o’clock, and a cheque
for the damage would be required witliiu a
week.
Then they descended solemnly to a band
of boarders oppressed with a sense of dis-
aster and the outrageous odor of burnt cloth-
ing. Dinner was taken in funereal silence,
and, until someone told her to stop, simple
Alice kept on moaning in a dazed way, “Pore
thing! Didn’t she take it to ’art? Funny*
’ow things turn out. Y’ know, I used to
think she didn’t like me wearin’ it. Pore
thing!”
KODAK.
Smithers and O’Regan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O’REGAN was a fine stalwart son of
Ireland who had been sentenced for
his sins to teach the country school
at Jones’s Pocket. An out-and-out patriot
was O’Regan—a staunch Home Ruler, a
whole-souled hater of the Sassenach and a
sworn foe to the Black North. Inspector
O’Hara, who used to examine the schools in
O’Regan’s district, was of precisely the
same brand of political opinion as O’Regan
himself. If you passed O’Regan’s school
on the day when his,pupils were instructed
in singing, and you chanced to look in,
O’Regan would promptly greet you in his
heartiest fashion, and, turning to his
pupils, would remark: “Now, bhoys and
girls, give Mister Blank a taste of your
quality.”
Proudly the note of the pibroch is sounding.
And the children of Jones’s Pocket would
roll out “O'Donnell Aboo!” with a fervor
that would carry you back hundreds of
years to the days when O’Donnell was a
name that made the Sassenach soldiery
shake in their shoes and wish themselves
the other side of the Channel. When the
last echoes of “O’Donnell Aboo” had died
down, O’Regan would lift his baton again,
and the song-birds of Jones’s Pocket would
regale the visitor with that plaintive
strain, “The Wearing of the Green.” Other
ditties of Celtic origin were in O’Regan’s
repertoire, but “O’Donnell Aboo” and
“The Wearing of the Green” you could
strike without fail every time.
About three miles from Jones’s rocket
stood the school belonging to Fig Tree
Scrub. Although so near one another, the
two were in different inspectorial dis-
tricts. Reginald Smithers, the Fig Tree
Scrub pedagogue, was also a patriot,
though of a different brand —a Jingo of the
deepest dye, who had resolved to imbue the
district with his sentiments. With the very
noblest of motives, of course, Smithers had
introduced into his school such melodies as
“Rule Britannia,” “Sons of the Sea,” “The
British Grenadiers,” the which ditties were
sung and re-sung until the very dogs that
ran about the school-ground nosing for a
casual crust could almost give you in a
nasal howl, “Some tork ov Alexander,” or
“Sons orv the Sea!”
Inspector Robinson, who presided over
Smithers’ district, cherished a most in-
tense admiration for “Rule Britannia,” and
thought that “Sons of the Sea” was the
finest musical composition of modern times.
Not that this fact had the slightest weight
with Smithers —not for a moment. If
Robinson thought that the verse running
But yer can’t beat the boys orv the bulldog breed
That made hold England’s name,
was the finest poetical outburst since By-
ron’s day, who was Smithers that he should
deny Inspector Robinson the luxury of oc-
casionally listening to the spirited anthem’
Incidentally, O’Regan and Smithers re-
ceived magnificent reports from their respec-
tive inspectors, with particularly compli-
mentary remarks about the singing.
Quite suddenly, and without giving anv
notice of the change, the Education De-
partment rearranged the boundaries of the
districts of Inspectors Robinson and
O’Hara, with the result that one June
morning Inspector Robinson walked quietly
into O’Regan’s school and set to work on his
annual examination. At the close of his
operations he requested the teacher to put
the pupils through a song or two, and in
five minutes was listening dazed and horri-
fied to that fierce anti-Sassenach battle-
hymn, “O’Donnell Aboo.” One touch of
the spirited Celtic chorus was enough for
Robinson.
“But. Mr. O’Hara, have they no other
song?”
“Certainly, sir,” said O’Regan proudly,
and forthwith the youngsters pealed out:—
Oil, the Wearin’ ov the Green,
The Wearin’ ov the Green,
They’re hangin’ men and women for the Wearin’ ov
the Green !
Robinson seized his hat and fled.
A week or two later Inspector O’Hara
dropped in on Smithers. Smithers was
somewhat taken aback. He, like O’Regan,
knew nothing of the Departmental re-
arrangement that had taken away his
ultra-jingoistic superior, and saddled him
with an enthusiastic Home-Ruler. Conse-
quently, he had no new musical treat to
offer when O’Hara demanded a song or two.
Tip rose, naturally, the strains of “Rule
Britannia”: and when O’Hara requested a
change in the bill of fare, Smithers shunted
off on to “Sons of the Sea” —
'Ave you ’card the tale of forrin nashuns
Buildin’ ships unceasinTy?
O’Hara nearly burst with disgust, and sig-
nalled Smithers to stop the performance;
but the youngsters went gallantly on:—
Sons orv the Sea,
Orl British born :
But yer can’t beat ther boys orv the bulldawg breed
That made hold England's name.
The Inspector had to listen through to the
bitter end. Then he also fled.
A month or two later O’Regan had the
pleasure of reading the annual report of
Inspector Robinson on his establishment
and its progress :
The tone of the school in Jones's Pocket is not what
it might be. The Union Jack is seldom hoisted and
saluted as it ought to be, and the songs taught are
highly unsuitable, and not calculated to foster a spirit
of patriotism. The teacher’s ear for music appears
to be very faulty, and the children’s disposition to
bawl in singing a chorus is very noticeable.
A similar document from Inspector
O'Hara struck Smithers one morning:—
Much fervor and vigor are evident in the singing of
the pupils, but very little of either melody or har-
mony. The teacher, unfortunately, mistakes political
enthusiasm for correct vocalisation.
The latest rumor from Jones’s Pocket
and Fig Tree Scrub is to the effect that
O’Regan is wrestling in genuine agony of
spirit with “Sons of the Sea,” while
Smithers is busy practising that fine old
Irish melody, “O’Donnell Aboo.”
F. BORN.
